### Ice Loss\-\--1NC

#### The loss of ice is spectacularized to justify colonial expansion

**Dodds** and Smith **23**, Professor of Geopolitics, University of
London (Klaus, and Jen Rose. \"Against decline? The geographies and
temporalities of the Arctic cryosphere.\" The Geographical Journal 189.3
(2023): 388-397. Accessed: 6/17/25)

In our reading of the Arctic, [we argue that contemporary Arctic
geopolitical discourse too often pivots around simple causalities such
as less sea ice equates to growth in maritime traffic and possible
geopolitical tension]{.underline} (for a critical review, Depledge,
2019). [This in turn invites a phalanx of technical-scientific experts
to qualify claims about the preponderance of open water or dampen down
expectations that the Arctic will inevitably be a global maritime
highway. These are particular and familiar colonial moves towards
possessing the Arctic]{.underline}. The relationship between colonialism
and northern territories, including water, is not straightforward given
that many of those lands incorporated into colonial-nation states such
as Canada, the United States and Russia that utilised varying projects,
tactics and techniques to dispossess Indigenous peoples of their lands,
lifeways and livelihoods (Kollin, 2001; Ruiz et al., 2019). [What is
often understood as a settler impulse to eliminate to replace by way of
genocidal warfare is not neatly projected onto landscapes of the Arctic,
especially in thinking of the particularly situated (ongoing) colonial
histories in Alaska and Canada]{.underline}. The enactment of allotment,
and terra-forming forest and plain into agriculture including ranching,
though attempted, was not successfully replicated further north. The
early needs for resource extraction as animating the core principle of
settler-racial-capitalism was not easily met by virtue of the presence
of ice and glacier fields of Arctic landscapes. [Colonial movements and
travels in search of the Northwest/east Passage of the white nationalist
race to the illusive North Pole were temporary and fleeting---apart from
later public policy directed towards the encouragement and support of
homesteaders and gold miners to populate northern spaces, colonial
actors in the North often did not come to stay. Elemental substances
such as frozen earth, sea ice and glaciers were surveyed, mapped and
manipulated, with colonial agents seeking to thaw out permafrost and
restructure parts of the Arctic landscape in terms of agriculture. The
needs of colonial desires to cultivate particular foods in particular
ways that aligned with property making through labour and land were
thwarted by ice and its liminal shapes of constituting land not
water]{.underline} (Smith, 2021).

The [coupling of scientific expertise and racist legal policies of
dispossession ultimately created similar outcomes of longstanding
oppressive colonial regimes that continue to constitute the
present.]{.underline} For one example, [plans such as the so-called
Inuit High Arctic relocations in the early 1950s were hatched in Canada
to resettle Indigenous communities in what is for now understood as
northern Quebec, as part of a strategic calculation designed to populate
a 'sparse and spartan' northern territory.]{.underline} The [relocation
strategy proved to be a costly and devastating failure, as it was built
upon the notions that all cold climates are ostensibly the same and
therefore Indigenous peoples should be able to live successfully within
them---racist thinking that either misunderstands or overlooks centuries
of relationships built across Indigenous peoples and more-than-human
kin]{.underline}. At the base of this thinking is a temperate-normative
assumption that an adapted cold weather community in one part of
northern Canada could simply be trans-located for the purpose of filling
a less populated area. The Inuit High Arctic relocations serve as a
poignant reminder of how the initiatives of relocation are currently
iterative---at one moment in the 1950s[, Indigenous peoples of the
Arctic are made to undergo enforced relocation by decision of a
colonial-nation-state meant to 'uplift' or to meet the end of an act of
colonial sovereignty, but in the contemporary moment in which Native
peoples find their homes underwater due to carbon consequences, those
same nation-states will not assist in relocation efforts]{.underline}
(Audlaluk, 2020).

The [storification of the extent and thickness of sea ice is integral to
Arctic geopolitics---often framed as loss followed by revelation and
enhanced activity and engagement by Arctic and non-Arctic states and
stakeholders]{.underline} (Dodds, 2010, Dittmer et al., 2011). There
have been three aspects to all of this that we believe deserve more
explicit and considered attention. [Fundamentally, further attention
needs to be given to how these analogies of sea ice work to produce
different framings and relationships within and beyond the Arctic.
First, we might ask what is the role of sea ice as 'buffer' rather than
'barrier' and/or 'hazard', and how does such an analogy complicate
iterative accounts of sea ice\'s annual rates of extent or shrinkage
(with the inference being that less sea ice extent and thickness means
more likelihood of activity and possible conflict over the use of polar
waters).]{.underline} [If sea ice is considered to be a barrier then
that implies that there is either an obstacle to movement (which someone
or thing wishes to cross) and/or that there is a distinction to be made
between one area or another. For example, the term barrier reef is used
to signify an ecological space betwixt shallow water and
shoreline]{.underline}. Whereas sea ice as a material barrier is often
used to help identify the Arctic Ocean as distinct from the warmer
Atlantic and Pacific Oceans. Sea ice as buffer foregrounds something
quite different; in this rendering[, ice is said to perform a series of
ecological services including regulating ocean temperature, reflecting
heat away from ocean and absorbing the energy released by wave action;
whereas sea ice as hazard is framed as such because commercial and
military shipping wishes to traverse ice-covered waters]{.underline}.
Tropes such as 'polar Mediterranean' have long been used to re-configure
the Arctic Ocean and maritime passages as capable, at some point, of
being usable (Steinberg, 2014).

\[TEXT CONDENSED, NONE OMITTED\]

This shift in the framing of sea ice has developed in two distinct
areas. In stories about climate change and coastal communities in
Alaska, it has become increasingly common to recognise the 'buffering'
effect sea ice plays in mitigating the erosive damage done by winter
storms to coastlines. The intersection between shoreland, water and ice
is thus recognised as interstitial space; with a capacity to absorb and
deflect wave energy away from the rock, soil and sand of the coastline.
A reduction in multi-year sea ice with thinner annual or young sea ice
in turn reveals buffering limits; thicker sea ice being far more likely
to be able to absorb the energy associated with wave action. We see this
enacted in real time with the recent typhoon Merbok that moved through
the Bering Strait and left many coastal communities with damaged homes
and destroyed subsistence infrastructure. Another way sea ice scientists
have framed sea ice is to posit it as a 'lid' to acidification, which is
caused by the ocean becoming saturated with carbon dioxide emissions.
Younger sea ice in particular being less able to act as a 'lid' to both
warming and amplification trends. With less sea ice, open water is
better able to absorb heat and disrupt in turn the relationship between
water, ice and air. Acidifying waters have direct implications for
future biodiversity, as some creatures including shell-building
organisms cannot tolerate acidification beyond a certain degree of
concentration. As Cheryl Katz (2019) noted in her investigation of the
acidification of the Arctic Ocean: But as marine carbon levels climb,
more and more carbonate ions are being used up, lowering the ocean\'s
ability to buffer, and causing acidity---measured as a drop in pH---to
rise. Those same carbonate ions are needed by creatures like starfish
and clams, whose shells or skeletons are made of the calcium carbonate
minerals aragonite or calcite ... Melting away the ocean\'s icy lid
exposes yet more water to take up yet more carbon dioxide from the
atmosphere. The acidification of Arctic waters has direct implications
for local and Indigenous fishing and shellfish harvesting in coastal
Alaska, with the fastest rate of acidification occurring in the Beaufort
Sea and Bering Sea. Acidification affects growing seasons or molluscs
and crustaceans, reproduction rates and species distribution (for
example, clams and crabs). What the analogy of the 'buffer' does is to
highlight energy transfer rather than simply assume 'disappearance' of
sea ice per se. The relationship between land, sea and air in the Arctic
reveals well the 'vertical reciprocity' between the three, as more open
water means more heat absorption, rising air and water temperatures,
greater acidification and diminishment of multi-year ice (Adey, 2010).

\[PARAGRAPH BREAKS RESUME\]

S[econd, sea ice and its relationship to scientific-military imaginaries
and practices reveals polar waters to be hazard rather than buffer---an
obstacle to unfettered mobility. Contemporary iterations of Arctic
geopolitics owe their poignancy to earlier antecedents. During the Cold
War, sea ice was conceptualised as a barrier to movement]{.underline}
(Roberts, 2015). A slippery subject that would prove a difficult
adversary for submarine captains as rival naval forces. Countries such
as Canada established national ice services in the 1950s to provide
further coverage and assessment for shipping users. [Sea ice research,
and for that matter permafrost science, was in part funded by national
governments to enable and improve awareness and understanding of
ice-covered waters and cold environments]{.underline} (and Indigenous
knowledges and expertise enrolled if often under-acknowledged). In [the
military arena, institutions such as the Office of Naval Research and
the US Arctic Submarine Laboratory in Barrow Alaska were charged with
funding research that sought to make ice more knowable, predictable and
governable. And yet the memoirs of US nuclear submarine captains and
published research on sea ice revealed the opposite---sea ice was and is
notoriously dynamic, confusing and unsettling to those seeking to go
under it, transit it at the surface and/or break through from
below]{.underline} (for example, McLaren, 2008). Depending on
seasonality, wind direction, ocean temperatures and currents, militaries
and scientists were involved in a host of national and international
initiatives designed to improve seasonal charting, environmental
management plans, strategic patrols, and resource extractions plans.
Richard Powell\'s work on Canadian scientific authority in the 1950s
illustrates well the manner in which sea ice was caught up with
sovereign and security agendas (Powell, 2017).

[Coastal states such as Russia and Canada were also eager to ensure that
their ice-covered waters were properly protected--- environmentally and
legally---from transit traffic]{.underline} (Steinberg et al., 2015). In
the 1960s and 1970s, [Canadian governments in particular were alarmed by
the prospect of US oil tanker traffic going backwards and forwards from
Alaska via the Northwest Passage]{.underline}. The [presence of sea ice
acted as a politically helpful shipping hazard and thus possible
deterrent to foreign-registered ships wishing to cross through waters
the Canadian authorities considered to be 'historic' rather than subject
to transit rights.]{.underline} While Canada put in place additional
environmental regulatory safe-guarding measures (for example, the 1970
Arctic Waters Pollution Prevention Act), the Northwest Passage remains a
source of official legal disagreement between Canada and the United
States. [Diminishing sea ice, thus, acts as a catalyst for speculation
about whether this **waterway** might **become a source of future
friction** either because the waters are thought to be in principle less
hazardous (]{.underline}a point that is often contested by shipping and
sea ice experts alike) or because relevant details of the United Nations
Law of the Sea Convention (UNCLOS and Article 234) for 'ice-covered
areas' are thought to be less relevant in operational terms. As the
Article in question notes:

> Coastal States have the right to adopt and enforce non-discriminatory
> laws and regulations for the prevention, reduction and control of
> marine pollution from vessels in ice-covered areas within the limits
> of the exclusive economic zone, where particularly severe climatic
> conditions and the presence of ice covering such areas for most of the
> year \[i.e. 6 months and one day\] create obstructions or exceptional
> hazards to navigation, and pollution of the marine environment could
> cause major harm to or irreversible disturbance of the ecological
> balance.

Article 234 was the only example of where UNCLOS recognised the presence
of ice-covered areas. Later the UN body the International Maritime
Organisation\'s Polar Code (entered into force in 2017) sets out
requirements for shipping entering and leaving polar waters. [While
acknowledging that coastal communities 'in the Arctic could be, and that
polar ecosystems are, vulnerable to human activities, such as ship
operation']{.underline} (emphasis added, Polar Code 2017). While the
Polar Code noted a 'could be' for possible impacts on Arctic coastal
communities of climate change[, it addresses the operational challenges
posed by sea ice with considerable assertiveness. Sea ice is framed
throughout as a technical problem that can be mitigated by collective
action. Solutions to sea ice will emerge through legal, technological
and training interventions.]{.underline}

Third[, sea ice as register of global rather than local or Indigenous
futures]{.underline}. [When coastal communities in Alaska are posited as
on the frontline of climate change, sea ice and permafrost are
**spectacularised** in the sense of being made to stand for a
**harbinger of an unwanted global future**]{.underline} (Bronen, 2010).
There is [often a slippage between the **everyday** and local and the
**global** and planetary]{.underline}. [What gets lost in this slippage
is the notion that ice/frost/snow become caught up in an extractive
economy which prioritises the ramifications for extra-territorial
parties, global elemental spaces and the 'buffering' affordances of ice
and frozen ground]{.underline}. The [Arctic is in crisis primarily
because it will not keep its frozen ground frozen and its sea ice
intact. The very thing that Cold War frontier engineers dreamed of
annihilating is now being cursed for not being resilient enough
(]{.underline}Fleming, 2010). [We further assert for a ground to
articulate an Arctic that does not lose the verifiable danger and
precarity of ice melt and the livelihoods that continue to be changed,
transformed and dispossessed, which is largely consequential from the
enactments of racial capitalism but that also holds a refusal to fall
victim to an alarmist, polemical, devastation and declensionist
narrative.]{.underline} The declensionist narrative is one that has been
historical and often wielded to discuss Indigenous peoples and their
inevitable disappearance conveniently from resource rich areas. Care
must be taken when reporting the disappearance of ice. As Inupiaq poet
Joan Naviyuk Kane writes:

> Much of the discourse around the Arctic and Indigenous people
> preoccupies itself with destruction ... It is couched in either the
> concrete documentation of disappearance---erosion or submersion of
> land due to climate change or climate-caused displacement, for
> instance---or in the figurative language of loss (cited in Nunatsiaq
> News, 2018)

Taking up Kane\'s project, [is there a way to identify the enactments of
colonialism without a spectacularised event of damage, loss and the
horror of that loss, as the vessel of discussion?]{.underline} And [is
it possible to have that conversation without lioning Indigenous
resilience as a life-lesson for non-indigenous peoples
elsewhere]{.underline}? As Eve Tuck and C. Ree put it:

> Damage narratives are the only stories that get told about me, unless
> I\'m the one that\'s telling them. People have made their careers on
> telling stories of damage about me, about communities like mine.
> Damage is the only way that monsters and future ghosts are conjured
> (Tuck and Ree, 2013)

### Indigenous Inclusion Link\-\--1NC

#### TEK is essentialized and forces Indigenous people to become stereotypical research subjects for the West

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
Berkeley in Comparative Ethnic Studies (Jen.Â Ice geographies: The
colonial politics of race and indigeneity in the Arctic. Duke University
Press, 2025.)//kay kayðŸ§Ÿ

[tek, then, is often **essentialized** and forced into a container as
though it could exist in an apolitical and ahistorical form severed from
the relations it co- constitutes. There is a tendency to reduce
Indigenous knowledges **to something only** botanical, **ecological**,
or somehow **about sustainability**, conservation, or preservation. As
Cana Uluak Itchuaqiyaq argues, "**Indigenous knowledges** are **not
secondary** to western, 'scientific' ones.]{.underline} Indeed Inuit
knowledges of our lands and waters are scientific, sovereign, and often
sacred . . . these knowledges come from our communities observing,
interacting, surviving together, and depending on the same lands and
waters where our ancestors existed." 62 There is a [drive to incorporate
local Indigenous knowledges on the ground]{.underline}, separate from
political relationships in which they are embroiled, [to build a better
set of representations for data, figures, and, inevitably, policy. This
**is an inadequate framing** as Indigenous science and knowledge sets
**cannot be bracketed**; **they are ongoing relations and practices**
that are political, juridical, and spiritual and must be consistently
maintained and reiterated]{.underline}.63 It is not Indigenous peoples'
responsibility to save what has been contemporarily cast as a dying
planet. [Native peoples, communities, and polities are boxed by colonial
regimes that make it appear as though they should save a dying earth as
mythologically, inherently land- based people--- and not that they might
have their own strategies as sovereign entities who govern as they best
see fit. One way that Arctic Indigenous knowledges have been usurped
through add- and- stir tactics of inclusion is through scientific
**research projects that laud collaborative methodologies without
meaningful engagement and representation**]{.underline}. In 2020,
Kawerak, Inc., a regional non-profit corporation serving the Bering
Straits Region organized under the auspices of ancsa, wrote a letter
addressed to the National Science Foundation's polar program Navigating
the New Arctic (nna). The letter indicted poor research practices and
included statements such as: We cannot overstate the need for true
collaboration among Indigenous Peoples . . . we have grave concerns
about the impacts from the nna process and funded projects to date. The
nna has funded projects that claim . . . to be collaborative, to do
knowledge coproduction, to include partnerships with Indigenous
communities, and to address questions that will 'help' or 'assist'
Arctic residents. [Many of these projects (and many more which were not
funded) do not and will not fulfill any of those claims.64 This
important statement resonates with Cana Uluak Itchuaqiyaq's urgent call
for equitable, meaningful collaboration with Indigenous peoples in
Arctic science research]{.underline}. Itchuaqiyaq writes, I have been
researched. My family has been researched. We have given access to
ourselves, our experiences, our ancestral knowledges, and our homes to
fulfill others' ambitions, all in the hope that these actions can help
our community. We have trusted others to depict our lives, experiences,
knowledges, and communities fairly and accurately. We have lent our
social capital to outsiders and vouched for them so that they may gain
access into the homes of others in our community. We have incurred risk.
/ Tell me: What risk have you incurred?65 These calls for responsible
scientific engagement with Arctic Indigenous communities don't arrive
from nowhere. In this moment of climate change, the most dominant form
of ordering and narrating ice geographies is made through understanding
ice as a scientific dataset. Ice as a material for objective
laboratorial analysis is often severed from the political and social
contexts from which it was removed toward the goal of telling planetary
stories. [Scientific data has long been extracted from the Arctic and
continues in contemporary forms such as through drone imagery and
satellite data that tracks sea ice melt and glacial retreat.66 The
technologies to surveil, track, speculate, and predict ice melt **seem
to proliferate** with the minute.]{.underline}

### Land Development Link\-\--1NC

#### Land is a flattened georgraphy that is weaponized by the Global North to force Indigenous people into submission. 

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
Berkeley in Comparative Ethnic Studies (Jen.Â Ice geographies: The
colonial politics of race and indigeneity in the Arctic. Duke University
Press, 2025.)//kay kayðŸ§Ÿ

**[Land as flattened geometry is just one part of the
issue.]{.underline}** As Meredith Palmer argues, **[the metrics of
measuring land are embroiled with histories of anti- Indigenous racism
in what she calls "the racist acre."]{.underline}** 11 **[Colonial and
imperial tools and tactics of measuring and observing land try to make
it into inanimate materiality for capitalist accumulation, sacrifice,
and often abandonment over and against Indigenous
relationship]{.underline}**s.12 Max Liboiron, following Sandra Styres
and Dawn Zinga, has described **[that process as one of making Land into
land.]{.underline}**13 In this formulation, **[Land refers to "the
unique entity that is the combined living spirit of plants, animals,
air, water, humans, histories, and events recognized by many Indigenous
communities. When land is not capitalized \[land\] refers to the concept
from a colonial worldview whereby landscapes are common, universal, and
everywhere, even with great variation."]{.underline}** 14 **[Capital L
Land is relations; small l land is resource.]{.underline}** Tuck and
McKenzie write correctly, then, that "**[Indigenous intellectual
contributions rarely fail to engage in issues of land and place---
especially via conceptualizations of tribal identity, sovereignty, and
treaty rights--- yet when these discussions are taken up by non-
Indigenous and settler scholars, the saliency of land and place is
frequently left out of the picture."]{.underline}** 15 Put another way,
**[it is not simply a problem that land is flattened into passive static
space through technology and narrative alike, but that those flattenings
are also the overlooking, undermining, and an attempted severing of
Native sovereignties and relationships.]{.underline}** [Staying
attentive to the ways that land has been designated, measured,
surveilled, and documented through technologies that are anti-
Indigenous demonstrate the need by Native thinkers to continue to
describe and reclaim land relationalities in scholarship and in
practice.**16 Enactments of colonialism also coerce Native peoples to
regard, discuss, and interact with land in strategic
formulations**]{.underline}. Often, **[those strategies are necessarily
iterative, as colonialism is as historical as it is a contemporary
project, maintaining and making itself anew]{.underline}**. [Native
polities, communities, and individuals have had to employ what Gayatri
Spivak has called "strategic essentialism" to keep homelands under
Native governance]{.underline}.17 [This is not always how Native people
may want to formulate relationships to their lands. Federal and state
sponsored colonial projects are often beyond the control and purview of
Native peoples, communities, and polities, such as allotment and
property- making, identity regimes of blood quantum calculations,
removal and relocation from homelands, theft of children and placement
into boarding schools, pollution, and environmental racism.]{.underline}
**[These are all colonial land relations that most often require(d)
military force and simultaneous if not subsequential bureaucratic
violences.]{.underline}** As Michelle Daigle has written,
**["Theorizations of colonial conquest, resistance, and liberation
require interrogations of the fixed spatialities and temporalities that
Indigenous peoples continue to be relegated to discursively and
materially."]{.underline}** 18 [These ongoing histories not only
continue to shape the lives and politics of Native peoples who relate to
more temperate lands, they also shape the ways that ice geographies and
Indigenous Arctic peoples must strategize and articulate their specific
relations to ice]{.underline}. **[Arctic Indigenous peoples feel the
climate aftermaths of lands extracted and turned inside- out in more
temperate Indigenous territories elsewhere. Indigenous Arctic ice
geographies are often negatively compared to more easily capitalist
extracted, temperate Indigenous lands further south and rendered
desolate, empty, or violable]{.underline}**. Yet, as Sheila Watt-
Cloutier writes, **["The Arctic may seem cold and dark to those who
don't know it well, but for us a day of hunting or fishing brought the
most succulent, nutritious food. Then there would be the intense joy as
we gathered together as a family and friends, sharing and partaking of
the same animal in a communal meal, to live in a boundless landscape and
a close- knit culture in which everything matters. . . . Like
generations of Inuit, I bonded with the ice and snow."]{.underline}** 19
[In a similar sentiment to Watt- Cloutier, Leslie Marmon Silko writes in
her essay "Interior and Exterior Landscapes" about relationships to
desert places: Hopi Pueblo elders said that the austere and, to some
eyes, barren plains and hills surrounding their mesa- top villages in
northeast Arizona actually help nurture the spirituality of the Hopi way
. . . the Hopi people might have settled in locations far more lush. . .
.But there on the high, silent, sandstone mesas that overlook the sandy,
arid expanses stretching to all horizons,]{.underline} **[the Hopi
elders say the Hopi people must "live by their prayers." Great
abundances of things . . . tend to lure human attention away from what
is most valuable and important.20 For Silko the desert offers a
relationship that supports and enriches a spiritual relationship, most
important above all in her sensibility.]{.underline}** Across Silko and
Watt- Cloutier, **[the landscapes of ice and desert are crucially life-
giving and sustaining. Many of the technologies used to measure and make
land into property don't neatly map onto desert or ice, defying what
settler law might try to make of it or make it into.]{.underline}**

### Land Reclamation Link\-\--1NC

#### The aff's call to 'reclaim the land' is built on Western understandings of sovereignty -- reject it. 

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
Berkeley in Comparative Ethnic Studies (Jen.Â Ice geographies: The
colonial politics of race and indigeneity in the Arctic. Duke University
Press, 2025.)//kay kayðŸ§Ÿ

**[The strategic, and often necessary, utilization of accepted colonial
interpretation of what counts as land and land relations has resulted in
keeping some homelands intact, yet it is not without
consequences]{.underline}**. As Goeman writes, **[To claim land, it is
set up through the court systems that we must claim difference from
settlers. In our pursuit to differentiate our- selves from settler
colonists, we have territorialized land and body. The legal tensions
between stability and static versus change, adaptation, and growth are
very real for indigenous people]{.underline}**. [They are in a
particularly difficult political situation in which recognition of
cultural and racial uniqueness is detrimental to the overall physical
and cultural survival of indigenous people.]{.underline}26 **[These
kinds of land relations framed through a categorical difference from
whiteness as enforced and determined by settler law are at once the
mechanism that allows Native peoples to claim their land and also
creates harm in an identification of self and community.]{.underline}**
[The original moment of articulating difference from colonists to make
claims to homeland is not a singular event. This difference must be
reiterated over time to maintain access to land and things like treaty
rights. Legal relationships to land are also not homogenous. The
opportunity to legally claim difference from colonists as precedent for
land claims and land rights, such as in Alaska, Hawaii, and in many
places in California, does not always exist.]{.underline} **[In many
places, no treaties were signed, and there is no legal or priority
access for Native peoples to their land relations. In Alaska, legal
conflict over what are called "subsistence rights" (access to whaling,
fishing, hunting, and gathering) continues into the contemporary moment.
When Alaska Native people demonstrated and lobbied for their subsistence
rights, those rights were denied by state legislature as
unconstitutional, race- based privileges.]{.underline}** **[Herein lies
one dimension of the problematic identified by Goeman: when rights are
articulated through racial difference in settler courts, they can also
be denied on those same grounds.]{.underline}** However[, in the case of
Alaska, the denial of subsistence rights flies in the face of
subsistence access legally falling under the auspices of federal Indian
law, not a state-level decision.]{.underline}27 **[Anti-Indigeneity that
operates to remove Native peoples from their lands continues the need to
further centralize the importance of land.]{.underline}** [This need,
unfortunately, also potentially minimizes conceptual space allowed for
water and ice and an analysis of how these elements cannot come into
categorial formation in ways that land is afforded. Put another way, the
colonial category of land as easily measured or captured by technology
and law can disallow other categories and relations, like water and ice,
to be claimed.]{.underline} **[Thus, Native studies must continue to
support Native polities' processes of land reclamation while
simultaneously contesting the legitimacy of settler- colonial
governments to determine such meanings.]{.underline}** **[The urgency
within the field toward land reflects the urgency of the politics on the
ground. The feedback loop of land relations, land theft, land rights,
land claims, land back, land scholarship is a loop precisely because of
the ongoing elements of land dispossession and enacted
coloniality.]{.underline}** Moreover, [because of the precarity around
land dispossession and the need to continually articulate land relations
in forms legible to settler courts, scholarship in Native studies that
pushes inquiries around reframing land differently can be read as
dangerous and potentially against grounded movements]{.underline}. With
this important reality in mind, **[I worry about the colonial grounding
of the concept of land that forces it to carry everything we ask it to.
We need to blur the boundaries of these orderings and
categories.]{.underline}**

### Oil/Gas Extraction Link\-\--1NC

#### The extraction of soil and gas are headquartered in Indigenous people's communities. 

**Mousoom '25** they are a settler of color passionate about the
intersections between environmental justice and race. (Mer. \"Blood on
Their Hands: Settler Colonial States in the Arctic.\"Â *Tapestries:
Interwoven voices of local and global identities*Â 14.1 (2025):
9.)//kaykayðŸ§Ÿ

[**One cannot talk about climate change without discussing oil and gas,
of which the Arctic is a major site of extraction.** The Arctic
Environmental Responsibility Index was created in 2021 by a group of
multinational academics (]{.underline}Overland et al 2021). [Oil and gas
companies operating in the Arctic were evaluated on criteria of how they
were minimizing harm to local ecosystems and Indigenous people near
their operations (ibid). **Out of all the Arctic countries, the
companies that are headquartered in the American Empire average the
highest ratings while the Russian Empire has the worst environmental
ratings (ibid).** Trends within the data showed that oil and gas
companies have a better rating than mining, likely because mining is
inland while oil is offshore (ibid). However, the Arctic Environmental
Responsibility Index lacks an analysis of scale.]{.underline} **[The
lowest-ranking company, based in Russia, has a mere 2 percent of the
revenues of the highest-ranking company from Norway]{.underline}**
("Rating of the Largest Russian Companies by Sales Volume" 2018).
[**While larger corporations score higher on the index, their
environmental impact may be higher due to the sheer amount of resource
extraction undergone.** The oil, coal and gas extracted by these
companies create the chemical byproducts of polycyclic aromatic
hydrocarbons]{.underline} (PAHs) ("Polycyclic Aromatic Hydrocarbons"
2009). [**This group includes hundreds of compounds that are recognized
as carcinogens, impacting not only human health but also marine
microorganisms---such as bacteria and fungi---that form the foundation
of the entire food web (ibid).** Climate change and the release of PAHs
are intertwined in a positive feedback loop, as rising temperatures in
the Arctic accelerate the melting of sea ice]{.underline} (Christensen
2024). [Traditionally, sea ice has trapped PAHs, preventing their
harmful effects from reaching marine organism]{.underline}s (Stow, Sova,
and Reimer 2005). **[However, as this ice continues to melt, more PAHs
are released into the marine ecosystem]{.underline}** (Christensen
2024). **[Current climate projections suggest that an ice-free Arctic
summer could occur as early as 2030, even with substantial reductions in
emissions, potentially resulting in a dramatic increase in PAHs in
marine environments]{.underline}** (Kim et al 2023). **[It isn't just
marine Arctic ecosystems that are suffering. The evidence is in the
soil. Soil acts as one of the world's primary storage of carbon, keeping
it away from the atmosphere and thereby mitigating climate
change]{.underline}** (Volkova 2021). [Half of the global supply of soil
organic carbon is in the Arctic (Thawing Arctic Peatlands Risk Unlocking
Huge Amounts of Carbon" 2019).]{.underline} [These 1.7 trillion tons of
carbon in the Arctic predominantly come from peat, an organic soil that
is made out of partially decomposed plant matter]{.underline}
("Histosols" 2024). [Like sea ice, much of these soils remain frozen
year-round, hence the name permafrost]{.underline} (Tvid et al 2012).
**[But as accelerating warming thaws this permafrost, the previously
frozen soil gains moisture (ibid).]{.underline}** [As water moves
through the deepest layers of the soil, the wet peat gets exposed to
oxygen and releases carbon dioxide into the atmosphere]{.underline}
(Pearce 2017**[). The thawing of permafrost also awakens microbes deep
in the ground, who find these warming temperatures ideal for growing
(ibid).]{.underline}** [Like humans, microbes release greenhouse gasses
as a byproduct of their daily respiration]{.underline} (Barbato et al
2022). **[And so more microbes means more greenhouse gasses in the
atmosphere (ibid). However, not all microbes are reacting to climate
change equally, leading to uneven microbe population distribution which
threatens the microbial ecosystem and subsequently the health of the
soil]{.underline}** (Mallard & Pearce 2018). [These effects are
complicated by the soil ecosystem differences across the Arctic, all of
which remain vastly under-researched]{.underline} (Barbato et al 2022).
**[A possible analysis from the Arctic Responsibility Index is that the
blame for colonialism in the Arctic has shifted from the state to
corporations, but out of the 120 companies listed on the index, 15 are
state-owned]{.underline}** (Overland et al 2021). **[The most
environmentally responsible company is one of them.]{.underline}** [The
Norwegian government has a 67% stake in this company named Equinor ("Our
Shareholders"). In 2022 the oil corporation posted record profits,
leaving the government with a payout of 11.390 billion
dollars]{.underline} ("Equinor Fourth Quarter 2022 and Year End
Results").1 Another Norse state-owned oil company, Petoro is ranked
number 33 (Overland et al 2021). [This business also benefited from the
sanctions on Russian oil in 2022, pulling 48.356 billion dollars for the
country]{.underline} (SDFI and Petoro Annual Report 2022). [The
government revenues from these two companies comprised a full ten
percent of the 2022 national GDP (T]{.underline}rading Economics 2024).

### Sea Exploration Link\-\--1NC

#### Sea exploration comes at the cost of contaminating Indigenous people's homes and food supplies. 

**Mousoom '25** they are a settler of color passionate about the
intersections between environmental justice and race. (Mer. \"Blood on
Their Hands: Settler Colonial States in the Arctic.\"Â *Tapestries:
Interwoven voices of local and global identities*Â 14.1 (2025):
9.)//kaykayðŸ§Ÿ

[Known as Natchiq in the Inupiaq language, ringed seals are top
predators in the Arctic (NOAA Fisheries).]{.underline} **[Sea ice is
integral to the seal's livelihoods, which is why these animals stay in
the Arctic and surrounding regions]{.underline}** (National Wildlife
Federation). [They are the most common seal species.]{.underline} **[The
changing climate has melted the amount of sea ice that these seals
depend on, leading to increased cortisol levels that indicate chronic
stress]{.underline}** (Ogloff 2022). **[Seals with higher stress also
tend to have less blubber depth, which is correlated with a lower state
of health overall]{.underline}** (ibid). [Such grim health effects are
shown in how pollution is changing the cells and genes of the ringed
seals. **Ringed seals are now having significant expressions of the ARNT
gene, which is associated with polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs) and
dioxins (Boyi 2022).**]{.underline} [The cells of ringed seals also
produced increased levels of a protein called IL-2 (ibid). This is meant
to spur the body into an immune response, which means that the seals are
attempting to fight off the pollutants]{.underline}. **[However, this
state of constant defense against the pollutants can weaken the overall
immune response]{.underline}** (Ogloff 2022). [As such, the ringed seals
are susceptible to a mass casualty event in the case of an epidemic
(ibid). However, this is not the most pressing issue of seal consumption
by Indigenous people: the contaminants in the Arctic Ocean from shipping
and pollution also lead to increased mercury and selenium in the
fish]{.underline} (Simola 2024). [After consuming mercury and
selenium-laden fish, the seals metabolize these substances into a new
compound: mercuric selenide (ibid**). Although this is non-toxic to the
seals, it is harmful to human health once consumed, particularly to the
kidneys and the central nervous system**]{.underline} ("Mercury
Selenide" 2024). [These perilous effects are no doubt worsened by the
lack of access to medical care in rural areas of the Arctic, as
specialized doctors are only available through taking a flight. Seal is
a vital food source for the Inuit, as grocery prices remain exorbitantly
expensive in the Arctic due to high shipping costs. **As everything in
the Arctic is imported, prices like thirty-three Canadian dollars for a
two-pack of Nutella are commonplace (Allen 2023). In such remote areas,
there are few job opportunities except for hunting**]{.underline}
(Jessen 2015). [The Inuit use every part of the seal, not simply for
food but to create traditional water-resistant clothing for the winter
("Why is the Seal Vital for Indigenous Communities?" 2022). Even without
the necessity, hunting seals]{.underline} [is integral to passing down
cultural hunting knowledge to future generations]{.underline} (Sarmiento
2019). The Contradictions of the State and Settler Scientists **[As
settlers, we have historically benefitted from these government
assimilationist and polluting policies, and therefore the state's
actions reflect our own.]{.underline}** **[This shifting of
responsibilities should awaken settler colonial amnesia of those
involved in the Arctic. This is a phenomenon where people are not fully
aware of the colonialism that they benefit from (Said
1994).]{.underline}** **[It can manifest in obscuring the scale of the
atrocities or even flat-out denial. This misremembering leads to the
invalidation of those dealing with the effects of colonialism, and the
creation of false justifications for the violence.]{.underline}** **[The
settler scientific community is particularly culpable, as our research
into the specific effects of climate change not only ignores our own
role in creating such ecological violence through our privileged
membership in the state, but also how our scientific research is often
largely funded by the state.]{.underline}** [The National Science
Foundation, established in 1950, is a federal government agency of the
United States that funds scientific research]{.underline} ("History,"
2024).2 From the past fiscal year which started on October 1st, 2023 to
September 30, 2024, the National Science Foundation (NSF) was given 9.06
billion dollars. [**This funding is not solely for graduate students but
also undergraduates, academics and even companies, with the requirement
that they must be at least a permanent resident if not a citizen.** The
National Science Foundation has supported the work of at least 268
winners of the Nobel Prize. In 2024 alone, four of the six prize
categories were won by people who have received NSF funding]{.underline}
("NSF Continues Strong Legacy with Nobel Prize Winners," 2024). [This
does not include the multiple other government agencies in the United
States that fund scientific research, particularly the Department of
Defense, the Department of Energy and the Department of
State**.**]{.underline} **[Therefore it is not surprising that the vast
majority of the scientific studies cited in this paper are funded by the
state.]{.underline}** [This is functionally an oxymoronic system where
the state pays largely settler scientists to understand and critique the
systems that have been put into place by the state. This is not to scorn
or shame scientists who receive federal funding. In fact, I myself hope
to obtain a grant from the United States government to pursue a
master\'s degree in Arctic climate ecology.]{.underline} **[As a
researcher coming from a low-income background, such funding would be an
integral lifeline to my career. But in taking funding from the state,
scientists need to acknowledge that this fiscal sponsorship for
environmental research is not charity but rather a continuation of the
long legacy of scientists acting as agents of the state, from the
development of the atomic bomb to the Cold War space race. Even the
colonial developments discussed in this aper such as the Early Distant
Warning Line, oil and gas extraction, and continental shelf claims would
not have been possible without scientists.]{.underline}**

### AT: But AFF Good for Indigenous People\-\--2NC

#### The AFF can only understand Indigenous people's desires when it is in the context of pain and dispossession -- this leads to Indigenous people's stories being commodified for the academy. 

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
Berkeley in Comparative Ethnic Studies (Jen.Â Ice geographies: The
colonial politics of race and indigeneity in the Arctic. Duke University
Press, 2025.)//kay kayðŸ§Ÿ

[**The crucial push to retemporalize the golden spike of the Industrial
Revolution to the sixteenth century demands an acknowledgment and
incorporation of histories of violence that shaped the climate of the
globe.** Importantly, this retemporalization argues not only for an
acknowledgment of those histories but that they constitute the political
present]{.underline}. [**I also suggest that there is danger in
replacing one golden spike with another.** Within humanities and social
sciences scholarship there exists an over-privileging and over-
centering of the violence of colonialism, imperialism, and corporeal
violence, especially as historically enacted upon Indigenous peoples in
the Americas.]{.underline} [These histories of violence have been
absented from dominant narratives, and this is not by accident. However,
this also means that narrations of violence overwhelm many fields, and
often testimony is called for if those histories are not rehearsed again
and again.]{.underline} **[Therefore, there is already a humanist
"golden spike" for critical Indigenous studies that becomes the stakes
of the argument in which corporeal, anti- Indigenous violence must
always be illustrated for the reader. Eve Tuck and K. Wayne Yang put it
this way: "The subaltern can speak, but is only invited to speak her/our
pain."]{.underline}** [Damage- centered research may operate, even
benevolently, within a theory of change in which harm must be recorded
or proven in order to convince an outside adjudicator that reparations
are deserved . . .we observe that much of the work of the academy is to
reproduce stories of oppression in its own voice .]{.underline} . .
**[the collection of pain narratives and the theories of change that
champion the value of such narratives are so prevalent in the social
sciences that one might surmise that they are indeed what the academy is
about.75]{.underline}** [Placing the concept of damage- centered
research into relation with ice core data rendered through the article
by Koch and colleagues, Indigenous populations are incorporated into the
long durÃ©e of climate change discourse but only through death and
disappearance. This is inclusion by narration of violence enacted upon
them.]{.underline} **[Critical scholars of race and indigeneity are
pushed to cite the work as evidentiary toward, at most, potential
reparations if not at least remedial education. One hope might be that
through a reckoning with the sixteenth century, experiences of violence
may be better attended, and a more just version of historical enactments
can become a fact to all. I understand Tuck and Yang's argument of
damage- based research and necessitated pain narratives as related to a
bind experienced by academic]{.underline}** fields that Katherine
McKittrick has called "**[identity- disciplines": The incorporation of
"identity" into disciplined learning systems--- the institutionalization
of identity within the context of the university--- has resulted in a
grim reification of biocentric order. . . studying identity so often
involves demonstrating that biology is socially constructed, not
displacing biology, but, rather, empowering biology--- the flesh--- as
the primary way to study identity]{.underline}** . . . and **[identity-
disciplines function to uphold misery and empire and the segregation of
ideas and idea makers precisely because all disciplines are differently
enfleshed and classified and hierarchized . . . For disciplines under
duress--- almost always identity- disciplines]{.underline}**!--- [there
are redoubled costs to doing anticolonial and decolonial work:
participate in the existing system, love and study the categories as
they are, or be harmed. . . . live with the racism and the awfulness you
walk through every day--- and economize it]{.underline}--- [or get
out]{.underline}.76 For McKittrick, there are multiple ways that the
university expects and rewards pain narratives. [Narratives that
reiterate colonial/Â­ imperial/ racist violence are especially rewarded,
but not only from identity disciplines. Identity disciplines are most
often populated by scholars of difference. Institutionally, those
disciplines are rendered most precarious by the workings of capital and
ongoing ideologies of white supremacy. We are pushed, coerced, and
expected to make violence our golden spike and our experiences of pain
as authentic economies that secure our futures]{.underline}. **[Within
the article by Koch and colleagues, through ice core science, the
violence of the Great Dying is read in the atmosphere. It is the primary
way that Indigenous lives are then rendered legible. This at once erases
Indigenous peoples of ice geographies entangled with ice and conceals
the nonhuman relationships enacted and maintained--- in particular ice,
air, and plant respiration as well as the pollen, trees, mud, and other
storied materialities. Those beings are reduced to data points and
invisibilized in their multidimensional living to make arguments about
the death of specific humans and the aftermaths of those
deaths.]{.underline}** [The use of ice core, charcoal, and pollen data
is toward a measure of reforestation and the change in atmosphere based
on a transition of plant activity, not necessarily a multidimensional
accounting of human life and/or lifeways with plants. Those plants
respirated differently because of a relationship made with specific
humans. As a result of imperial violence, plant life through
reforestation respirated air differently, and this shaped the global
climate through cooling.]{.underline} **[Tree breath and tree breathers
are the populations that hold atmospheric data and in part become the
data upon which ice core arguments rest. In attending to those specific
relationships, the information that plant respirators and other beings
and materialities lend us, there are ways to craft stories that don't
necessitate elision of process, tools, and enactments other than
violence.]{.underline}** **[There are forms of using ice core data,
proxy data, and other forms of coring processes in smaller, local scales
that Indigenous knowledges support. All data comes from somewhere and is
used to make inferences about something. This does not mean violence
needs to be inherent to those processes of retelling. What an
interesting imaginative premise to entertain: asking the mud, pollen,
ice, air bubbles, and tree pollen to be in conversation with one another
about a shared experience or story of being alive, to not again cordon
off and keep information from touching or to only touch if it serves the
dominant narrative.]{.underline}**

#### Reparations are used to silence and dispossess Indigenous people. 

**Mousoom '25** they are a settler of color passionate about the
intersections between environmental justice and race. (Mer. \"Blood on
Their Hands: Settler Colonial States in the Arctic.\"Â *Tapestries:
Interwoven voices of local and global identities*Â 14.1 (2025):
9.)//kaykayðŸ§Ÿ

These state profits are likely going to fund the robust government
services that Scandinavia is known for. **[Although this proves vital to
settler populations, the welfare state has historically been used as a
tool for Indigenous assimilation in the Arctic.]{.underline}** **[In
1944, the Canadian government instituted a basic income program called
Family Allowances to help with the economic effects of the Great
Depression, and the dispatches of the Royal Canadian Mounted Police
implemented this policy in the Arctic (McNicoll 1999).]{.underline}**
[As police encroachments continued to disturb the natural ecosystem, the
Canadian government created a system that led to forced reliance on this
government\'s welfare.]{.underline} **[It was only through cash from the
Canadian police that villagers could buy Western products, and the
police quickly cemented authority over personal disputes and
fires]{.underline}** (Kramer 2022). **[The Family Allowances created a
material incentive for Canadian paternalism, encapsulated within the
Inupiaq term "ilira," which is the concept of unyielding obedience
(ibid).]{.underline}** [This would later prove to the Canadian
government's benefit, ilira being used to herd Arctic children to
residential schools designed to stamp out their Indigenous culture
(ibid). **The Norwegian government that profits off of polluting
extractionism is therefore another extension of such
practices**]{.underline}. **[The welfare benefits created from this
profit is designed to create unwavering ilira from both Indigenous and
settler populations, keeping our eyes blind to the state's role in the
climate crisis.]{.underline}**

## ADVANTAGE LINKS

### Agriculture Link\-\--1NC

#### Utilizing Western agricultural practices as a barometer for how successful Native life is undermines Indigenous practices and re-entrenches the ordering of the Anthropocene 

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
Berkeley in Comparative Ethnic Studies (Jen.Â Ice geographies: The
colonial politics of race and indigeneity in the Arctic. Duke University
Press, 2025.)//kay kayðŸ§Ÿ

**[Utilizing Western agriculture and attendant sedentarism as a
measuring device has the potential to reinforce an agricultural
measurement as ultimate arbiter of civilized progress and therefore
foreclose other forms of livelihoods as meaningful]{.underline}**. As
Antonello and Carey write, "[All societies do not advance
mechanistically and uniformly through stages of civilization; nor do
they move homogenously and simplistically through modernization or
capitalism or boom- and- bust cycles."]{.underline} 72 **[To use these
markers, even in critique of other problematic indicators like the
Industrial Revolution, is to undermine Indigenous narrations of how they
arrived in any given place and various formations of interacting with
land that support socialities.]{.underline}** Moreover, **[the myth of
the Bering Land Bridge is not separate from normative orderings of the
Anthropocene; these narratives mutually reinforce one another as they
rely upon interpretations of ice and ice geographies to do important
racializing and dispossessive work as explained in the following
chapter.]{.underline}** [Further, agriculture is a practice tethered to
specific temperate locales, and therefore one that is largely
incongruous to ice geographies]{.underline}. [Some of the findings in
this article are drawn from ice core data,drilled and extracted from an
Antarctic ice sheet, along with other proxy data such as charcoal and
pollen.]{.underline} I**[n this ice core study application, the
constitutive historical nature of ice and ice geographies to ice core
science is outside of its scope. The findings discussed are limited to
Indigenous peoples living but mostly dying and being killed in temperate
and tropical locales.]{.underline}**73 Within the article, too,
**[histories of imperial violence such as the use of enslavement,
starvation, and biological warfare are not included.]{.underline}**
[Those Indigenous peoples who are most directly entangled with the
colonial histories of ice and ice geographies are again kept on the
periphery. **Ice is enlisted to tell a specific kind of story that does
not account for its particular relation to historical and ongoing
colonization, and the processes of analysis that ice undergoes to be
rendered into data.**]{.underline} [Therefore, the where, how, and from
whom ice core data has been extracted, and how data is recontextualized
to make larger narrative meanings, is absented]{.underline}. **[Ice and
snow and their own material experiences are being operationalized for
teleological ends, even in critique. These measurements are made through
the abrupt Great Dying of Indigenous peoples of the Americas, not lived
practices. This is a demonstration of the limits of the ice core as
evidentiary.]{.underline}** [Ice core information doesn't contain the
complexities of lives lived and nuanced seasons of climate but are
haphazard sealed bits of small atmospheric information saved at
random.]{.underline} **[Certainly, proxy data of all forms can provide
important insights, but not nearly enough to tell a complete
story.]{.underline}** As Candis Callison puts it, "[In such a view,
regional distinctions vanish, as do individual stories of dispossession,
violence, and genocide."]{.underline} 74 **[The intervention here
operates in the large- scale spectacle of Indigenous peoples
disappearing and doing so in temperate and tropical
geographies.]{.underline}** The main and still important difference
being that the narrative language reflects that they are being actively,
violently disappeared.

### Arms Control Link\-\--1NC

#### Arms control measures are bourn out of a racial anxiety to prevent colonized powers from threatening the colonizer\-\--this double standard is used to maintain the colonial order 

**Mathur 20**, Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San
Antonio (Ritu, 7/12/2020, "The West and the Rest: A Civilizational
Mantra" in "Civilizational Discourses in Weapons Control," pp. 91-94
Date Accessed: 4/30/2023)

Price is emphatic in his assertion, '\[T\]here was no suggestion during
the Hague conferences that the use of asphyxiating shells was to be
regarded as legitimate against "uncivilized" nations.'37 Price insists,
'\[T\]here was no active establishment by those present that this
contractual language simultaneously legitimized and positively
sanctioned certain uses of asphyxiating shells (for example, against
nonparticipants) against those outside the ken of the civilized family
of nation.'38 On the contrary, there is a suggestion that '[one of the
qualifications of a civilized nation was to]{.underline} [partake in the
regulation of warfare]{.underline} that began among the European society
of states in the mid-nineteenth century' and '[the contractual language
of the]{.underline} [prohibition]{.underline} [was a legitimizing
inducement to join the society of civilized states]{.underline}, [an
invitation to self-discipline]{.underline}.'39 [The irony
of]{.underline} such [an 'invitation to self-discipline']{.underline}
[is]{.underline} succinctly [captured]{.underline} [by]{.underline}
[Albert Memmi as he observes how a]{.underline} deliberately
constituted, [asymmetrical relationship of power between]{.underline}
the [colonizers]{.underline} [and]{.underline} the
[colonized]{.underline} [emerged during this period]{.underline}:
[While]{.underline} **[it is pardonable for the colonizer to have his
little arsenals]{.underline}**, **[the discovery of even a rusty weapon
among the colonized is cause for immediate punishment]{.underline}**.
The Arab fantasia has become nothing more than the act of a trained
animal which is asked to roar, as he used to, to frighten the guests.
But the animal roars extremely well; and nostalgia for arms is always
present, and is part of all ceremonies in Africa, from north to south.
The lack of implements of war appears proportional to the size of the
colonialist forces; the most isolated tribes are still the first to pick
up these weapons. This is not a proof of savagery, but only evidence
that the conditioning is not sufficiently maintained.40 These
[limitations]{.underline} [were imposed by experiences of vulnerability
and fear that the colonial subjects might use]{.underline} [these
weapons against their]{.underline} own [colonial masters]{.underline}.41
This sense of fear and vulnerability in the West was further reinforced
with nineteenth-century memories of the African 'primary resistance' and
Indian 'mutinies for independence' which fundamentally altered
colonizers' attitudes toward their colonies.42The defeat of the Italians
by the Ethiopians in 1896 represented 'the first decisive victory gained
by troops that may be reckoned as oriental over a European army in the
open field, for at least three centuries.'43 The Japanese victory in the
Russo-Japanese war in 1905 was much publicized and 'presented not as a
local triumph of one nation over another, but as a victory with global
implications, of the Mongolian people over the European.'44 The
[military victories of]{.underline} the [non-European
powers]{.underline} [were touted to rouse racial anxieties]{.underline}
to 'hold the racial line.'45 Gunther etÂ al. in their exploration of 'The
West: a securitizing community?' argue that '[the performative effects
of a security semantics in which "the West" figures as the
threatened]{.underline}, [yet]{.underline} notoriously [vague referent
object]{.underline} [that has to be defended]{.underline} against
alleged challenges' [generate a 'rhetorical heat']{.underline} [that
sanctions]{.underline} [a culture of 'self-assertion]{.underline},
self-authorization and self-immunization.'46 It is through [these
practices of 'rhetorical instrumentalization' that 'the West' exercises
a]{.underline} 'performative **[power to construct politically relevant
distinctions' between 'the West' and the 'non-West']{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} [at the same time encourages allusive references to
the impending threats from the 'non-West]{.underline}.'47 There was a
need for 'constant preparedness,' and [weapons technologies
'considered]{.underline} [too horrific to use against
Europeans']{.underline} such as 'the notorious Maxim gun and dumdum
bullet or the French Coloniale bomber' [were]{.underline}
'[developed]{.underline} specifically [for]{.underline} [colonial
use]{.underline}.'48 Salter argues that 'the imperial origin of the
machine gun and strategic bombing is not insignificant' as 'aerial
bombing was first conducted in the colonial context' and Asia and Africa

[control over colonial possessions]{.underline} that
[constituted]{.underline} '**[a specific colonial form of arms control

prohibition]{.underline} and prescription [were fluid]{.underline} in
their efforts [to 'manage arms flows according to where people were

civilization, loyalty, and utility to imperial power]{.underline}.'54
[The desire of the West to maintain]{.underline} 'imperial influence and
[imperial order']{.underline} [produced]{.underline} [discourses
on]{.underline} free trade, [sovereignty, development and
civilization]{.underline} [within which arguments on
prohibition]{.underline} and prescription [of weapons could be
situated]{.underline}.55 Paternalistic and Orientalist discourses on
humanitarianism and disarmament were selectively deployed to disarm the
savages.56

### China Threat Link\-\--1NC

#### Their reading of China's rise is [alarmist]{.underline}, [self-fulfilling]{.underline} and leads to [racial profiling]{.underline}.

**Lachapelle 17**, postdoctoral research fellow in the Department of

Securitizer and the Racist 'China-Threat' Discourse---Ascension, Peak,

This very short introduction should suffice to understand that for
securitizers subscribing to these views, [any report
suggesting]{.underline} how or predicting that [China is **catching up**
to the U.S. can quickly **provoke** an **alarmist response** and even,
in the logic of **self-fulfilling prophecy**, create a **real
China-Threat**]{.underline} (Pan, 2004; 2013). [One of the most
characteristic features of a securitizer's discourse is the]{.underline}
"[language of **certainty** and **inevitability**]{.underline}"
(Al-Rohdan, 2007: 64). Song (2015) explains why. Given its scientific
origin relying on deductive reasoning (Song, 555), the China Threat
discourse take on characteristics of predictability. Thus, [convinced

**pessimistic understanding** of China's rise]{.underline} (Goldstein,
