**alarmist** and **worst-case tone**]{.underline}.

Song (2015: 156-159) also explains how historical analogies can provide
the most authoritative way for print media to securitize China,
especially for those news organizations catering to an educated public
or to political elites. In international-relations circles, [**Imperial
Japan** and **Hitlerian Germany** represent]{.underline} de-facto
[historical examples used to theorize the belligerent intentions of a
rising challenger like China]{.underline} towards a global order
(Overholt, 2008). In the print media, on the other hand, scholars have
identified the Cold War as a privileged historical analogy mobilized to
construct China as a security issue. As I have explained, following the
U.S.S.R.'s collapse in 1991, communist China was depicted as a new
ideological threat to U.S. way of life. "China r\[ose\] to fill a vacuum
caused by the demise of the Soviet Union" (Jeffery, 2009: 311). Overall,
[Song's framework help identify media securitizers' **key discursive
features**:]{.underline} **[pseudo-scientific]{.underline}** predictive
[**confidence**, an **alarmist** and **pessimistic tone**, and use of
historical analogies]{.underline}. Although scholars have criticized the
short-comings of the China Threat (Al-Rodhan, 2007; Jeffery, 2009), no
researchers have asked whether [its discourse's own **catastrophic
projections** and expectations **over-securitized**]{.underline}
Washington-Beijing **[relations]{.underline}**, and thus participated in
the Threat's mediatic downfall? If that were the case, however, it would
suggest that an internal factor---the discourse's own logic---rather
than external factors (e.g., U.S. foreign-policy changes) tend to lead
to its cyclical nature. This may even explain why the China Threat and
Beijing's containment never managed to become the U.S.'s dominant way of
dealing with China's rise.

Methods

This study investigates how media coverage of China's techno-scientific
expertise has changed over time. These changes form a compelling case to
assess the structure of the China Threat cycle and the role of media
discourse as China's securitizer. As we saw earlier, after the collapse
of the Soviet Union, communist China became the new enemy driving
American foreign-policy and security concerns in the 1990s. China-Threat
proponents claim that Beijing's sustained economic development and
military modernization are a menace to U.S. national security and global
hegemony. On the other hand, over the last 20 years or so China's techno
scientific development have driven Beijing's global ascension, playing a
key role in both economic and military realms, the very same domains
that tend to worry fearful American observers of China. Therefore, the
U.S. media's coverage of China's techno-scientific expertise allows for
an examination of the China-Threat cycle and the conditions ---that is,
the trigger mechanisms---causing this cycle to escalate and decline over
time. Analysis of this reporting will allow to analyze the role media's
way of writing China itself plays in these discursive episodes
constructing Beijing's rise as a security problem for U.S. global
hegemony. For instance, Al Rodhan (2007:62) indicates that dominant U.S.
media participate in "painting an all-powerful threatening China bent on
the destruction of the United States" (see Liss 2003 for a similar
argument on media's negative China coverage).

The data from this study come from a collection of news articles from
The New York Times. This choice was specific and deliberate. I collected
articles using Lexis-Nexis Academic, the world's largest electronic
database of legal and newspaper documents. I searched for eight keywords
between January 1, 1996 and December 31, 2016: 'China' or 'Chinese'
'science', 'China' or 'Chinese' 'scientist(s), 'China' or 'Chinese'
'research', 'China' or 'Chinese' 'researcher(s)', 'China' or 'Chinese'
'professor(s)', and 'China' or 'Chinese' 'technology(ies)' (for a
keywords methodology, see Ghaziani and Ventresca, 2005). To also collect
documents where two keywords might be separated by more than one word
(e.g. the development of science in a previously underdeveloped country
such as China), I used a Lexis-Nexis advanced search option to limit the
number of words between two keywords to ten, since doing so would allow
me to capture more documents while controlling for contextual relevance.

I began my sampling frame in 1996 because the mid-1990s were pivotal for
China's modernization process and for Sino-American foreign
relationships. While on the one hand Washington was committed to a
building strong economic ties with Beijing, on the other there were also
increasing tensions between the two countries (e.g., the 1996 bombing of
the Chinese embassy in Belgrade; the escalation of tension over the
Taiwan Straight). There were also concerns about China's stability
following the death in 1999 of Deng Xiaoping, the architect of China's
reform; and reports of developmental problems such as wealth disparity
and systemic corruption were also reaching historic highs at this time
(Vogel, 2011).

My data-collection strategy resulted in 1,845 articles. During the
review process, I identified and eliminated articles that were
irrelevant to the topic of China's techno-scientific expertise, i.e.,
those articles that had been electronically retrieved by Lexis-Nexis
based on my keywords search but that did not actually focus on the
subject matter. This data-cleaning process reduced the dataset to 1,185
documents. I then used a simple random sampling method to reduce the
number of documents I would analyse: after sorting these documents into
chronological order (from oldest to newest), I chose every fifth
document. Given the event-based nature of media coverage (Jones, 1993),
temporal distance is important in preventing the over-sampling of
specific events: selecting every fifth news story thus provided a
balance between over- and under- sampling.

The final sample consisted of 250 documents (13.5% of the original
result) that averaged 1,179 words but ranged from 92 to 8,793 words. Of
this sample, 239 were news stories (96%), 8 were op-ed pieces (3%), and
3 were essays (1%). As for their desk distribution, 130 were from the
foreign desk (51%), 40 from the business/financial desk (16%), 25 from
the national desk (10%), 20 from the science desk (8%), and the
remainder came equally from the editorial, education, and week-in-review
sections. Nearly one-fourth (26%; 65 documents) of all the sampled news
articles appeared on the front page of The New York Times. I coded
documents using a retroductive method (Ragin 1994) which alternates
between inductive and deductive approaches (see Table 1).

\-\-\-- TABLE 1 ABOUT HERE \-\-\--

Amongst the reliability procedures available, intercoder reliability is
most relevant to qualitative content-analysis research (Campbell et al.,
2013). Intercoder reliability determines if different coders would code
the same data in similar ways (Campbell et al., 2013: 2). Therefore, to
evaluate the reliability of my coding procedure I conducted an
intercoder reliability test with an independent rater. The final coding
architecture was reduced to 20 codes (see Table 1 below for final node
names). To do so, I randomly selected 10% (23 articles) of my original
sample. This inter-coder reliability test involved training an
independent coder on how to use my codebook, and having her code my
subsample to assess how similarly we coded the same data (Krippendorff,
2004; Campbell et al., 2013). This inter-coder reliability testing
helped me to ensure that the themes I identified in the data were not
idiosyncratic, especially since the second coder had a strong
quantitative background and was not a China specialist.

Inter-coder reliability test that yields a proportional agreement of
0.80 (80%) or higher is considered reliable (Krippendorff, 2004: 241).
In our case, convincing results of above 80% were obtained after the
first round of coding by the second coder. As Table 1 below shows, all
of my codes were reliable, and were thus retained for analysis. The
lowest score registered was 94.79% well over the established 80%
benchmark.

A research design centered on the coverage that one news organization
(The New York Times) gave to China's techno-scientific expertise is
limited in how fully it represents the total American mediatic
environment on that topic. On the related themes of China's rise and the
China-Threat, however, studies indicate very little differences amongst
dominant liberal media agencies such as The New York Times, The
Washington Post, The Los Angeles Times, and The BBC (Griffiths, 2013;
Zengjun, 2004; Liss, 2003). Both earlier and more recent media studies
indicate how The New York Times can not only set the agenda for other
news media (in US and abroad) but also help shape the views of the
Washington establishment and of U.S. foreign policies (Cohen, 1961;
Weiss, 1974; Davison, 1976; Graber, 1980; Bradley, 1984; more recently,
see Yu and Leung, 2005, for an example of how the Times shaped George W.
Bush's approach to the 2003 SARS crisis and his handling of the
situation with Chinese Premier Jiang). Coverage by The New York Times is
also more likely to entice state agencies to respond to a foreign crisis
with disaster assistance that any TV network coverage (see Drury, Olson,
and Van Belle, 2005 for a similar measure of the Times's influence). The
dominant voice in American media, balancing between performativity and
objectivity and having the longest (according to Liss \[2003\], the
first reporting of news from China came as early as 1868) and most
extensive coverage of China (as revealed by my Lexis-Nexis analysis),
The New York Times is the ideal candidate for a study of which aspects
of China's scientific rise are covered; and more importantly, how
exactly that country's techno-scientific expertise interacts with a
powerful othering discourse representing China as a threat.

China-Threat Cycles: Media Securitizer and Racist Legal Practice

My results show the presence of two China-Threat episodes in the pages
of The Times between 1996 and 2016. The first cycle occurred between
1996 and 2001, and the second between 2010 and 2016. Both rounds
displayed similar rhythms: escalation, peak, and downfall. These
findings are consistent with the idea that the coverage content and
framing of dominant U.S. print media like The New York Times capture the
very concerns and ideologies of dominant social and political elites. In
this case, these China-Threat waves in The Times coincide with two
periods of tension in the Sino-American relationship. But beyond the
journalistic reporting capturing the political zeitgeist, my findings
uncovered two significant mechanisms clarifying the inner workings of
sinophobic4 moments. The first concerns the peak phase and media
securitizer; the second, the downfall phase and racism. Results from the
first China-Threat cycle reveal how The New York Times itself played a
key role as a securitizing agent in the case of Dr. Wen Ho Lee. Namely,
beyond the event itself, the mediatic way of writing the event acted as
a powerful trigger furthering the discursive construction of China as a
security issue, a perception that was well established by the
late-1990s. My analysis shows how in both episodes, racial profiling
against ethnic Chinese-American scientists who had been falsely accused
of spying for Beijing contributed to the China-Threat downfall. I argue
these judiciary moments acted as reflexive trigger revealing how this
American over-securitization of China by powerful U.S. political and
defense actors---a stance that was intended to protect U.S.
interests---in fact ended up harming some of its own citizens:
scientists of Chinese heritage.

1996-2001 -- Early post-Cold War Anxieties: Dr. Lee's Case and Atomic
Trigger as China Threat Trigger

By the mid-1990s, the China-Threat views were circulating widely in
American political and media circles (Broomfield, 2003; Al-Rodhan,
2007). A rising Communist China was increasingly understood as a
military, ideological, and economic threat to U.S.'s national interests
and global hegemony (Yee et al. 2002). A generational perspective helps
to explain the connection between Cold War perceptions lingering even
after 1991 and the origin of the China Threat. For a significant number
of Americans who had been adults during the Cold War, the rise of
communist China was most likely to be understood in the context of what
they had learned from their country's more than four decades of
confrontation with the Soviets; it was hard to think that things would
be any different with the exponential economic rise of China
(Bloomfield, 2003). Formalized as the successor-state image theory in
international relations (Kaplan, 2005; see Jeffery, 2009 for a
systematic critique), this view can be summarized by the argument was
that "China r\[ose\] to fill a vacuum caused by the demise of the Soviet
Union and thus represents a 'successor state' to the old Soviet power"
(Jeffery, 311; for similar arguments, see Gertz, 2000; Bernstein and
Munro, 1997).

This escalation phase was apparent in The New York Times as well. The
trigger accelerating the China-Threat episode into its peak phase came
with the story of Dr. Wen Ho Lee's case. Between 1996 and 2016, no other
event was covered more times by The New York Times than this case (17
out of 250 sampled documents). On March 6, 1999, The Times broke the
details of Wen Ho Lee's case in a lengthy news story entitled Breach At
Los Alamos: A Special Report. China Stole Nuclear Secrets for Bombs, US
Aides Say.5 The scientist, a Taiwan-born naturalized American citizen
working at Los Alamos National Laboratory, was accused of having stolen
and shared bomb secrets with Beijing's spies in the 1980s. The reporting
was alarming. Its very first lines showcased The Times's securitizing
discourse: "China has made a leap in the development of nuclear weapons:
the miniaturization of its bombs. \[...\] Until recently, China's
nuclear weapon designs were a generation behind those of the United
States, largely because Beijing was unable to produce small warheads
\[...\] the backbone of a modern nuclear force."6 Information about the
atomic trigger for the hydrogen bomb, known as a "primary," was believed
to have been passed to Beijing by Dr. Lee while he was travelling to a
scientific conference in the late 1980s.

The legal case would be dismissed more than two years later in February
2001 in an embarrassing fashion for the Justice Department and the
plethora of federal agencies that had been swept up in collective
paranoia (Lee and Zia, 2003). Records indicate that the FBI "carried out
its largest computer forensic investigation ever," interviewing more
than 1,000 individuals over nine months, "scouring the globe for
evidence that Dr. Lee had leaked his secrets." 7 But they found not a
single trace linking Dr. Lee to the claimed nuclear leak. Mr. Lee was
not a spy entist, simply an ethnic Chinese scientist working at one of
the most important nuclear laboratories in the country and who might
have been in contact with Beijing's or Taiwan's spies in the 1980s.
During this time, Wen Ho Lee spent more than twelve months in a federal
prison, including nine months in solitary confinement, facing the
prospect of spending the rest of his life in prison (Lee et al., 2003).8
During his detention, a former FBI agent went as far as to suggest "it
is reasonable to suppose that \[...\] "100,000 Chinese Americans \[...\]
should, hence, be suspected of serving as intelligence agents for the
People's Republic of China" (Lyman, 2000: 685). Clearly, The Times'
story had triggered an unreasonable fear that China had deeply and
systematically penetrated the country's most strategic infrastructure.

Cold-War Securitizers

In Dr. Lee's case, the aspect of the situation that triggered the
China-Threat could be what was reportedly stolen, "the design of
America's most advanced nuclear weapon,"9 the miniature W-88 war-head,
the crown jewel of U.S. nuclear power; or where it was allegedly stolen,
Los Alamos National Laboratory, the iconic U.S. Department of Energy
location where the Manhattan Project and the world's first atomic bombs
were conceived. But I argue the key element was not merely the event
itself, but as the passage above captured, The Times's own securitizing
discourse. The way the media wrote the story was a crucial moving force
in further constructing Beijing as an imminent threat. As one journalist
involved in that story later candidly noted about The New York Times's
participation in the case, "Often what mattered was who the interpreters
were, and what perspective they brought."10 Apart from the print media's
own voice, one particularly efficient group of interpreters featured in
the media discourse were Cold War securitizers embracing an offensive
realist vision of the world. The article's closing sentences indicate
how securitizing agents used "the language of certainty and
inevitability" (Al-Rodhan, 2007: 64) and Cold War imageries: "Mr.
Redmond, the C.I.A.'s former counterintelligence chief, who made his
name by unmasking the Soviet spy Aldrich Ames, says he has no doubts
about the significance \[of these\] discoveries \[concerning Wen Ho
Lee\]. As he put it, 'This is far more damaging to the national security
than Aldrich Ames.'"11 Here a well-known securitizing agent was not
merely suggesting but affirming that Dr. Lee's actions were more serious
than those of Aldrich Ames, one of the most effective moles in U.S.
intelligence history, who admitted compromising "virtually all Soviet
agents of the C.I.A. and other American and foreign services known to"
him (Weiner, Johnston, and Lewis, 2014: 134). His role as a Soviet
double agent was subsequently tied to state assassination and the deaths
of C.I.A. agents (Weiner et al., 134-35). In other words, beyond the
alleged events directly related to Dr. Lee, The Times' securitization of
the narrative, in which Beijing was repeatedly constructed as an
imminent security issue, propelled the China-Threat cycle to its peak
stage.

The offensive **[realism]{.underline}** perspective that was prevalent
among the U.S.'s military and national security establishment
(Al-Rodhan, 2007: 62; Goldstein, 2007: 639-640) [suggested]{.underline}
that [China was]{.underline} at that point [on **nuclear par** with the
U.S]{.underline}. ---also known as cross-over (Jeffery, 312; Lim, 2015:
282-83), [which]{.underline} in turn [implied]{.underline} that [Beijing
would]{.underline} eventually [take a more **aggressive stand** in the
Asia-Pacific]{.underline} region. The point is not simply that offensive
realism can scientifically explain these bouts of China-Threat in the
media and political circles, but rather that The Times' own
[coverage]{.underline} gave voice to and therefore [legitimized
**Cold-War securitizers**]{.underline} holding such theoretical views.
Song (2015) argues that **[i]{.underline}**nternational
**[r]{.underline}**elations **[theories]{.underline}** (e.g., offensive
realism, power transition theory) themselves [participated in the
development of **fearful perceptions** of a rising China
in]{.underline}to the **[China-Threat discourse]{.underline}**. From
Song's perspective, [theory does more than **explain** reality: it also
**shapes** reality.]{.underline} Bourdieu (1991: 194-198) calls this
effet de thÃ©orie. As he explains, "[\[t\]heory]{.underline} itself [can
be applied to reality, by **agents**]{.underline} and organizations
[capable of imposing principles of vision and di-vision, or
of]{.underline} producing or symbolically [reinforcing their propensity
to systematically **privilege** certain aspects of reality while
**ignoring others**]{.underline}." As Goldstein (2007: 99) elegantly
explains: "With a sense of conviction matched by urgency, writing on the
subject \[[the **China-Threat**]{.underline}\] often [seeks to
demonstrate the wisdom of a particular line of reasoning
and]{.underline} then to **[offer conclusions]{.underline}** about the
challenges currently faced, [**predictions** about the trajectory we
will witness in the future]{.underline}, [and **policy recommendations**
that logically follow from the **preferred interp**]{.underline}retation
[of **China's rise**]{.underline}." For instance, on March 6, 1999,
there was no hard proof that China had stolen the nuclear secrets or
that Beijing's nuclear program had even reached that level of
development. But [The]{.underline} Times' [narrative
**promoted**]{.underline} offensive [realism's **core idea** that parity
between **Beijing's nuc**]{.underline}lear
capabilitie**[s]{.underline}** [and the U.S.'s meant]{.underline} that
[China would **display**]{.underline} increasing **[belligerent
behavior]{.underline}** with its neighbours and the U.S. Consider the
following passage from the Breach At Los Alamos piece : "while officials
question whether China will actually deploy a more advanced nuclear
force soon, they acknowledge that Beijing has made plans to do so at
some point."12 As Song explains, for offensive realist or power
transition theorists and those holding such views, ["\[t\]he threat
posed by a rising China to international security is reasoned
'**scientifically**' to be **inevitable**]{.underline}" (Song, 2015:
155). [It is thus not surprising that]{.underline} "\[[a\]ssuming the
worst is what China-Threat Theory does]{.underline}" (Al-Rodhan 2007:
57).

Can one then say The Times's report was a balanced journalistic act? To
access the impact of mediatic power on other social fields is a delicate
thing to do scholars tell us (Zhang 2010). But in the case of Dr. Lee,
however, several elements allow to see how media's own influence went
far beyond negative framing (Liss, 2003) or representing (Huang and
Leung 2005) the China-Threat. I argue the news organization acted as a
powerful media securitizer. Times and times again, March 6 piece's
[reliance on **Cold War imagery** and **worst-case**, **alarmist
scenario** suggests the]{.underline} print [media was far from
performing its role of]{.underline} journalistic [objective
reporting]{.underline}. Indeed, several elements point at the
newspaper's securitizing participation. For one, following his release
from prison, Dr. Lee successfully sued The New York Times along with
four other news agencies (Lee, 2003). A more direct supportive evidence
is a rare mea culpa published by The New York Times, "an unusual
statement assessing its coverage,"13 two days before the final court
ruling would decide the atomic scientist's legal fate in a two-part
report totaling nearly 13,000 words. The document portrays the newspaper
as a reflexive media securitizer discussing its own participation in the
late-1990s wave of China-Threat. As The Times explained,

> \[t\]he spying charges gained wide public attention on March 6, 1999
> after The Times reported \[the case of Wen Ho Lee\]. The Times's
> coverage \[...\], especially the articles published in the first few
> months, attracted criticism from competing journalists and media
> critics and from defenders of Dr. Lee, who contended that our
> reporting had stimulated a political frenzy amounting to a witch hunt.
> \[T\]he White House, too, blamed \[...\] specifically The Times, for
> having propelled an overzealous prosecution by the administration's
> own Justice Department. \[...\] In place of a tone of journalistic
> detachment from our sources, we occasionally used language that
> adopted the sense of alarm that was contained in official reports and
> was being voiced to us by \[...\] officials with knowledge of the
> case. \[S\]uch \[reporting\] should have been \[...\] balanced with
> the more skeptical views of those who had doubts about the charges
> against Dr. Lee.14 15

In this revealing passage, the U.S. newspaper acknowledges its role as a
China's securitizer as a result of its 1999 accounts failing to be
impartial. But at the same time, it points out how the newspaper was
caught in crossfire between two camps within the Washington
Establishment. As the reflexive media securitizer goes on to explain,
"The Times's stories echoed and often oversimplified by politicians and
other news organizations---touched off a fierce public debate,"16 a
debate between those fearing China and those eager to engage and
integrate China within the Western global liberal order. The stakes were
high. For instance, China-Threat proponents used The Times's story to
attack the Clinton administration's position. In the aftermath of the
Wen Ho Lee's story, the White House blamed the newspaper for giving
credence to securitizing forces. "At a time when the Clinton
administration was defending a policy of increased engagement with
China, any suggestion that the White House had not moved swiftly against
a major Chinese espionage operation was politically explosive."17 As one
journalist puts it, "Republicans were eager to score points against the
White House on China."18 The Republican camp's strongest effort to
securitized China, the Cox Report, is closely linked with The Times'
reporting.

Indeed, at the time of the Breach At Los Alamos article, the Selected
Committee on U.S. National Security and Military/Commercial Concerns
with the People's Republic of China, headed by Christopher Cox, a
Republican representative from California, was finalizing its report
investigating whether since the 1980s technology or information that
would have helped Beijing's military program had been sent to China.
Upon reading The Times article and without consulting with any nuclear
experts, the committee decided to expand their report with a chapter on
China's nuclear espionage. Two months later, in May 1999, a redacted
version of the 700 page classified Cox Report was released. The Times
later called this report "the hasty product of an ideologically
supercharged selected committee \[...\] led by Cox."19

To this day, the Cox Report (Congress, 1999) remains a classic
China-Threat document. In his history of "the yellow peril," a racist
discourse on Asian nations dating back to the late 19th century, Lyman
(2000) argues that the Cox report constitutes the most important
document in its re-emergence in the late 1990s and that the China-Threat
discourse is only the most recent manifestation of the yellow peril. If
it is so, my findings indicate very clearly that The New York Times'
Breach At Los Alamos article also constitutes a key text in the intense
China-Threat episode that permeated the media as well as political and
public attention at the end of the 20th century. Given the pre-eminence
of print media in the U.S. mediatic sphere, one wonders if Dr. Lee's
case would have even broken publicly if the newspaper had not decided to
run the story.

In China, IR scholars were also quick to link the China-Threat discourse
to the yellow peril fear and the genealogy of racist western doctrine
pertaining to China (Shi, 1997; Luo, 1997; Li, 1999; Shi, 2004; Chen,
2005). My analytical focus here is not on revealing whether the U.S.
mediatic portrayal of Lee borrowed from yellow peril imageries in its
construction of China as a security threat---although Hanser (2013)
found racist footprints in major U.S. print-media coverage of scandals
involving defective/contaminated products manufactured in China. Rather,
what I am concerned with is how [racial profiling appears to have played
a role]{.underline} in Dr. Lee's case. **[Racist or not as a discourse,
the China-Threat is racist in its consequence]{.underline}**. For
instance, in 1997 when the CIA and other security-related agencies took
stock of China's sudden advance in nuclear design, some voices of reason
noted that more than a few thousand scientists and federal workers had
had access to the W-88 warhead blueprint design, so it was premature to
think the alleged theft had occurred at Los Alamos. But, once Los Alamos
was nevertheless "established" as the institutional source of the said
leak, mostly because the W-88 was originally designed there, the
suspicion quickly fell on Dr. Lee, one of the few scientists with
Chinese heritage who had had contacts with Mainland Chinese colleagues
in the 1980s.

### Data Link\-\--1NC

#### Attachments to human and rational time to explain the development of Indigenous communities evades analysis of the colonial processes that extract from them to keep Western man intact. 

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as

[**In many instances, proxy data and historical data utilized to

scientists, academics, and journalists use events of human history to

as key markers of suitable evidence]{.underline}. As Alessandro
Antonello and Mark Carey argue **[in their critique of the "Human Time"
created by ice core temporal logics, mainstream narratives emerging from
ice cores choose devices such as: "This snow fell around the time Christ
was born, during the American Civil War, during the life of Plato,
during Roman lead smelting, the landing of Columbus, Chernobyl, and of

by Antonello and Carey stem from additional moments like the display of

displayed at the Melbourne Aquarium in Australia.]{.underline} The pair
write, **["The human time emerging from these simple timelines evades
analysis of the political- economic- social processes and structures
that have caused climate change, or that equipped Western nations with
the technical capacity, global military support, and geopolitical (Cold

people are invisible in the ice core presentations."]{.underline}** 65

of ice core data is the reproduction of the supremacy of whiteness and
specific forms of civilization. The ice core in its layers and depth-

that are being forced to intellectually touch through a lens that
privileges particular historical events.]{.underline}** In the ice core
context, [comparisons of air degradation between pre--Industrial

[the efforts to keep ice cores frozen and intact shows investment in the

### Democracy Link\-\--1NC

#### Tying authoritarianism to aggression activates America's [crusader mentality]{.underline} and stokes the [new Cold War]{.underline} with China

**Layne 20**, University Distinguished Professor of International
Affairs and Robert M. Gates Chair in National Security at Texas A&M
University. (Christopher, "Preventing the China-U.S. Cold War from
Turning Hot", *The Chinese Journal of International Politics*, Vol. 13,
No. 3, doi: 10.1093/cjip/poaa012)

[When it comes to US policy toward China, **great power politics** is
**only part of the story**]{.underline}. [American policy makers are
**revisiting the First Cold War** by characterizing Sino-American
relations as a **Manichean ideological struggle** between **freedom**
and]{.underline} (communist) **[authoritarianism]{.underline}**.
**[Discourse matters]{.underline}**, [and when American
policymakers]{.underline} and foreign policy analysts [**continually
play up** the fact that China's government is communist, they do so with
a **purpose**. At least **subliminally** they seek to: recall the most
dire First Cold War depictions of the Soviet 'threat']{.underline} ('the
grim oligarchy of the Kremlin'); [**delegitimize** China's
government]{.underline} in the eyes of the American public; [and create
an **'enemy image' of China** as a bad actor in international
politics]{.underline}. In short, [they seek to **unleash America's
'crusader state' mentality**]{.underline}.154 While not---quite (or
yet)---employing the kind of overwrought, lurid language of official US
pronouncements during the First Cold War, the rhetoric emanating from
Washington about China does evoke memories of documents such as
NSC-68.155 The Trump administration says great power politics is
'defined by geopolitical rivalry between free and repressive world order
visions \...',156 which means that [for the U]{.underline}nited
[S]{.underline}tates, [the Sino-American relationship is **more about
ideology** than it is about the balance of power. Lest there be any
doubt that ideology is a driving force in the United States' China
policy, the IndoPacific Strategy report declares:]{.underline}

> [Yet while the Chinese people aspire to free markets, justice, and the
> rule of law, the People's Republic of China (PRC), under the
> leadership of the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), undermines the
> international system from within by exploiting its benefits while
> simultaneously eroding the values and principles of the rules-based
> order.]{.underline}157

Similarly, Vice President Pence's October 2018 and October 2019 speeches
on China also emphasized the ideological differences between the United
States and China.158 And speaking in London in January 2020, Secretary
of State Mike [Pompeo declared that the **C**hinese **C**ommunist
**P**arty]{.underline}---not China as a great power---[is 'the **central
threat of our times**'.]{.underline}159 Speaking at The Hague,
Netherlands in June 2019, Pompeo stated that 'China wants to be the
dominant economic and military power of the world, spreading its
authoritarian vision for society and its corrupt practices
world-wide'.160

[The signs of the **Second Cold War** are **hard to miss**]{.underline}.
In a rare display of bipartisanship, Congress passed the Hong Kong Human
Rights and Democracy Act. One of the bill's sponsors, Republican Sen.
Marco Rubio (Fla.) said, 'The United States and the international
community must make clear to Chinese leaders and power brokers that
their aggression toward Hong Kong risks swift, severe and lasting
consequences',161 Rubio warned that Hong Kong is not 'simply China's
internal affair'. Gordon Chang, who comments regularly on China, has
suggested that 'contagion' from Hong Kong could spill over onto the
mainland and cause the collapse of communism.162 Beijing's policy toward
Muslim's in Xinjiang has been widely denounced in the United States.163
And Congress has passed veto-proof legislation to compel the Trump
administration to punish China for its human rights violations.164
[While geopolitics and economics **play a role** in deteriorating
Sino-American relations, the salience of **ideology** is **increasingly
apparent** on the American side.]{.underline}

The post-Cold War policy of engaging China was based on
the---erroneous--- assumption that admitting China into the WTO would
result in economic liberalization and, ultimately, political
liberalization. Or, to use Robert Zollick's phrase, China would become a
'responsible stakeholder' in the international order (with Washington
defining the meaning of responsibility).165 As the IndoPacific Strategy
Report says, 'At the turn of the 21st century, the United States
advocated for China's admission into the World Trade Organization, with
the belief that economic liberalization would bring China into a greater
partnership with the United States and the free world'.166 Instead,
according to Pence, 'China has chosen economic aggression, which in turn
emboldened its growing military', and it 'has taken a sharp U-turn
toward control and oppression of its own people'.167 Within the US
foreign policy establishment, the belief that engagement failed has
fanned a widespread sense of disillusionment with China.

The growing disenchantment with China is a result of the American
foreign policy establishment's own naivete. There never was any
realistic basis for believing China would change its economic growth
model, or its political system, in response to American expectations. US
pressure on China to adhere to American norms and values serves only to
heighten Sino-American tensions. This is not a new story. Chinese
president Jiang Zemin's October 1995 remarks to the UN Security Council
are illustrative. In that speech, Jiang observed that 'certain big
powers, often under the cover of freedom, democracy and human rights,
set out to encroach upon the sovereignty of other countries, interfere
in their internal affairs and undermine their national unity and ethnic
harmony'.168 [It is commonplace for US leaders to assert that American
values are **'universal'**]{.underline}.169 **[Obviously]{.underline}**,
**[they are not]{.underline}**, [but when the U]{.underline}nited
[S]{.underline}tates [acts on this belief, it is going down a path
fraught with peril.]{.underline}170

[Here we see influence of '**offensive liberalism**' on American foreign
policy]{.underline}.171 At its core,
[Wilsonianism]{.underline}---[liberal internationalism
American-style]{.underline}---[holds that the world is divided into
**'good' states** (**democracies**) and **'bad' states**
(**non-democracies**). The latter are deemed **incorrigible**,
**expansive**, and **aggressive**]{.underline}. As Vice President Pence
put it when speaking about China, 'History attests \[that\] a country
that oppresses its own people rarely stops there. And Beijing also aims
to extend its reach across the wider world'.172 [There is an
**eliminationist impulse** imbedded in Wilsonianism: if 'bad' states are
**troublemakers**, **regime change** is--- ostensibly---the **path to
peace** because, so it claimed, democracies: don't fight other
democracies]{.underline}; uphold an open international economic system;
[and **respect liberal values**]{.underline} both at home and abroad.173
[This **elminationist impulse** was very much on display in US relations
with the **Soviet Union**: American intervention in the **Russian Civil
War**;]{.underline} early **[First Cold War documents]{.underline}**
such as NSC 20/4; [U.S. **'psychological warfare' efforts** in the
Baltic States and Ukraine]{.underline} during the late 1940s/early
1950s; and, of course, NSC-68.174 [American policy makers ought to have
learned from the First Cold War that an
**important**]{.underline}---[and
**perilous**]{.underline}---[**threshold is crossed** when a great power
competition is **converted** into a **titanic ideological struggle**.
The former can be **modulated with**]{.underline} adroit
**[diplomacy]{.underline}** [but the latter invariably become **zero sum
contests**.]{.underline}

Conclusion: [Can a **Train Wreck in Sino-American Relations Be
Avoided?**]{.underline}

Two powerful forces---America's liberal ideology and power transition
dynamics---are shaping the future of Sino- American relations.
[**Liberalism** is the **most potent generator of American hubris** and
**overreaching**]{.underline}. As I argued in The Peace of Illusions,
[liberalism is America's own distinctive '**myth of
empire**]{.underline}'.175 [This is evident today in the attitudes of
the American foreign policy elite toward China. For them, it seems that
the **real** 'threat' to the U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates
[is not the rise of China's military or economic power. Rather, it is
the 'threat' to **America's identity**]{.underline}---[the **asserted
universality** of its model of political and economic
development]{.underline}---[posed by a non-democratic, state capitalist
China]{.underline}.176 China's rise has fueled doubts--- seldom
acknowledged openly---about the United States' future economic prospects
and, even more fundamentally, about whether America's model of economic
and political development remains superior to China's.177 As the Eurasia
Group's Ian Bremmer and David Gordon have argued, 'China's rise and
statecapitalist model present the most significant commercial and
geopolitical challenge that the United States has faced in two decades',
and 'China's state capitalism challenges the future of democratic
capitalism'.178 Indeed, as Princeton University Professor Aaron
Friedberg argues, 'For Americans the success of a mainland \[Chinese\]
regime that blends authoritarian rule with market-driven economics is an
affront'.179

[China is a **problem** for **American foreign policy establishment**
because it **taps into their deepest fear**: that a **powerful
non-liberal state** will be able to **close off** the world]{.underline}
(or a least its key regions) [from ideological and economic penetration
by the U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates. [This fear of 'closure'
is **inextricably rooted in American liberalism** and explains why the
U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates [has such a **difficult time
coexisting** with non-liberal states]{.underline}.180 [The **last
thing** we should want]{.underline}, however, [is for the Sino-American
relationship to **degenerate** into a **new, highly ideological Second
Cold War**]{.underline}. [When power transition dynamics are added to
America's liberal ideology, Sino-American relations are **highly
combustible**]{.underline}. Security studies scholars agree that [power
transitions are **powder kegs** because of what is at stake: nothing
less than the leadership, and nature, of the international]{.underline}
(or regional) [order are up for grabs. Power transitions are about
whether the status quo can prevail over the forces of geopolitical
change]{.underline} (revisionism). When rising challengers near parity
with the declining dominant power, invariably their dissatisfaction with
the existing international order bubbles up (as it did in pre-1914
Anglo-German rivalry, to which some have compared today's Sino-America
relationship).181 After all, the existing order was constructed by the
declining dominant power during better days to privilege its own
interests.182 Rising challengers, on the other hand, want to revise the
existing order and bring it into line with what they perceive are the
new relative power realities. They seek, as Gilpin said, 'to change the
international system in order to advance their own interests'.183 If the
United States really wants to avoid a head-on collision with China, it
will have to make difficult--- even painful---adjustments and adopt a
policy that accommodates China's rise. In this sense, the United States
and China are rapidly nearing what could be called an 'E. H. Carr
Moment'.184 The Carr Moment occurs when the power relations that
underpinned---and gave birth to---the prevailing international order
have shifted in favor of the rising---revisionist---power. Carr analyzed
the international political crisis of the 1930s caused by the breakdown
of the post-World War I order enshrined in the Versailles Treaty (1919).
The Versailles system cracked, Carr argued, because of the growing gap
between the order it represented and the actual distribution of power in
Europe.

**The 1AC tropes [foreign nuclear powers]{.underline} as [error

[mandates]{.underline} armed intervention and reinforces the West's

**Krige 22**, PhD in the History and Philosophy of Science from the

The proliferation of populist conceptions of national identity in many

### Miscalculation\-\--1NC

#### Their narrative of [nuclear miscalculation]{.underline} is a technique of [martial power]{.underline} that [discursively produces]{.underline} militarism as [accidental]{.underline}, which [legitimizes]{.underline} violence.

**Meiches 19**, is assistant professor of security studies and conflict
resolution at the University of Washington--Tacoma. (Benjamin,
12-9-2019, "Wars of excess: Georges Bataille, solar economy, and the
accident in the age of precision war", Security Dialogue, Volume 51,
Issues 2-3, Pg. 275-278, accessed on 6-17-2023)

[With the rise of industrial warfare, **accidents** became a part of the
**discourse** of armed conflict. In **international relations**
literatures, this is perhaps most **apparent** in the case of
**accidental** nuclear launch given the potential for nuclear use to
cause **planetary destruction** (Sagan, 1995).]{.underline} However, the
accident reappeared as a problem with the rise of precision warfare
because of the explicit emphasis on exactness, the reduction of war's
lethality, and the deliberate, even careful, character of war. In short,
the ability to govern war precisely existed in tension with the
probability of socalled accidental deaths. Precision warfare was, by its
nature, supposed to become accident free. Patricia Owens (2003) and Maja
Zehfuss (2011) have both offered excellent analyses of the dangerous
character of this logic. As Owens (2003: 599--600) opines, 'at issue
\[in this form of warfare\] is how civilian deaths are legitimated and
under what guises this occurs'. [According to Owens, the accident
**functions** as a way of explicating and extending **martial power**
and, more specifically, of **discursively** producing an event as
**accidental**: 'the events labelled "accidents" **were not** accidents
until they had been **narrated as such**, contrary to the liberal state
(and positivist) assumption that they must **self-evidently** be
accidents' (Owens, 2003: 616). Owens and Zehfuss explain how the
**category** of the accident **enables** advocates of precision warfare
to **deny** any relationship between supposed **precise** technologies
and their **capacity** to injure and **kill**.]{.underline} [In this
way, Owens and Zehfuss also confirm the **broader observation** of Paul
Virilio (2006) that the accident **constitutes** an integral part of any
**technology** (martial or not). According to this model, the birth of a
technology is also the birth of the accident, and, consequently, any
perspective that treats the accident as derivative is engaging in a
depoliticization of the dangers of technology and speed. This process of
depoliticization guts the possibility of democratic politics in
discussions of security and war]{.underline} (Glezos, 2013). Bataille's
writings on war illuminate several additional elements of accidents and
risk-transfer war. First, critical security studies literature chiefly
approaches the accident through the lens of ideology, discourse, and
narration. Here, [the **accident** functions as a kind of **discursive
trick** that separates **legitimate** and **illegitimate** violence and,
therefore, legitimate and illegitimate modes of warfare.]{.underline}
This point is an important rejoinder to the rhetoric of risk-transfer
warfare. However, Bataille's theories focus less on discursive or
epistemological claims about the accident and more on its ontological
status. As the previous section outlined, Bataille views warfare as a
mode of expenditure that emerges from conditions of excess. For Bataille
(1985), excesses emerge from a type of 'base matter' that is not subject
to representation and can only be testified to through inner experience.
This form of excess, which cannot be fully sensed or described, is thus
ontologically contingent and cannot be known in advance. Put
differently, for Bataille, contingencies are a constitutive part of
armed conflict regardless of the terminology or discourse that surrounds
them. Industrial warfare sought to eliminate these contingencies in
order to function according to a model of rational accumulation. The
recent rise of precision, risk, and the accident as frames for
understanding contingency occurs as part of this much larger
paradigmatic shift to reframe war as a problem of utility, rationality,
and ends. [The accident constitutes only the newest method not just of
legitimizing war but also of governing and eliminating the problem of
excess.]{.underline} Since warfare involves expenditures of excess, it
is necessarily rife with contingencies and accidents. [The **accident**
thus operates as a liminal event in modern armed conflict, both as an
**epistemological invention** to legitimate **specific deaths** and
**injuries**, and as the resistance of base matter and solar economy to
human efforts at **rationalization** and **governance**.]{.underline}
These theoretical observations make several interventions into debates
about precision warfare. First, Bataille extends existing critiques of
risk-transfer warfare by both situating them as part of a broader shift
in modern warfare and establishing that the very terms of the discourse
are antithetical to the conditions of solar economy. Bataille's thought
shows how the structure of rationality in riskless war relies on the
accident as a discursive supplement in order to comprehend the excesses
that will recur in a condition of solar economy even when armed conflict
is defined by utility, rationality, and accumulation. [The accident is
not only an **ideological trick** designed to **depoliticize**, but also
an **epistemological supplement** that **insulates** the precision
paradigm from its **inability** to grapple with excess. This provides an
alternative way of interpreting the concern for the accident since,
following Bataille, precision legitimates modern warfare by seeking to
eliminate the excesses that have continually reappeared in spite of the
broader transformation of war into a rational exercise.]{.underline}
Precision and the language of the accident constitute only the latest
effort to govern the excessive character of war. Second, this framing
also illustrates why accidents often receive unprecedented attention in
the context of modern armed conflict. Indeed, while critiques such as
those of Owens and Zehfuss reveal how the accident legitimates liberal
war, what might be called the affective dimensions of the accident
receives comparatively little scrutiny (Bataille, 1991a: 203). Here,
Bataille's work extends existing critiques of precision war by shedding
light on the formation of subjectivity in relation to the accident.
Recall that, for Bataille, the experience of sovereignty develops in
response to chance, the anguish of death, and expenditure without
reserve. Bataille further contends that human desires are constituted by
an ambivalent relationship to excess, transgression, and sovereign
experience. On the one hand, humans seek to conserve their life against
excess and, on the other hand, human societies emerge out of the
excesses of solar economy. Bataille (1991a: 243) posits that this
ambivalence generates an attachment to the possibility 'always
unexpected, \[of being\] relieved of the heaviness that the world of
utility imposes on us, of the tasks in which the world of objects mires
us down'. For Bataille, [the accident, as a form of contingency, reveals
a potential type of sovereign experience. The existence of this
possibility helps to alleviate the sorrow and futility of a world
defined by utility. Accidents function according to the model of the
miracle, the unanticipated, the unpredictable, or the impossible, which
nonetheless occurs]{.underline} (Bataille, 1991a: 242). Following
Bataille's reading, [the accident produces two reactions to sovereign
experience in modern warfare. On the one hand, the exposure to the
anguish of death **incites** the need to **control**, **manage**, and
**govern** accidents, a process that has been well documented by
critical security studies scholars (Bousquet, 2018; Campbell, 1998;
Rasmussen, 2006). On the other hand is the possibility that accidents
inspire **affective attachments** and fascinations as a form of exposure
to sovereign experience. Put differently, accidents spark a form of
excitement -- either in the midst of warfare or in **fantasies of war**
-- that has received comparatively little attention. The accident
becomes a site of desire because it ensures that warfare remains
**contingent**, implicitly resisting efforts to govern, rationalize, or
form, in Bataille's words, 'dull war'.]{.underline} This perspective
provides a model to explain why the fantasies, imaginaries, and even
experiences of martial accidents remain a point of intrigue, thrill, and
draw for modern subjectivities. Within the accident is the budding
potential of sovereign experience. Accidents thus support two
complementary forms of subjectivity. [The first responds to the
inevitability of accident as a means of securitizing and governing
contingency, while the second embraces the accident as a sovereign or
limit-experience that breaks with the overt rationalization of war and
social life. This point has significant implications for resisting or
contesting securitization regimes.]{.underline} In particular, if
Bataille's insight about the lure of sovereign experience is correct,
then classical strategies of critique, delegitimization, and demasking
power relations in precision warfare, and other contexts, are
problematic strategies because they do not confront the underlying drive
to embrace warfare's contingencies as moments of sovereign experience.
For Bataille, this desire for sovereign experience intensifies the need
to transgress, to explore prohibited zones and probe limits.
Consequently, delegitimizing the accident, emphasizing the transgressive
character of violence (or other parallel acts), paradoxically
strengthens the affective attachment to warfare by reinforcing the
notion that war holds out the possibility of sovereign experience.
Contesting security discourse on these lines would thus do little to
engage the frame of the accident because it does not support new modes
of expenditure, but simply re-emphasizes the transgressive character of
specific practices of securitization and violence. In this way,
Bataille's work shows the limits of existing critical studies of the
accident. To illustrate this point, claims about accidents involve not
only political questions about the meaning or significance of specific
injuries, but also issues of equipment malfunction, logistical
breakdowns, and encounters with surrounding ecology. Many of these
dimensions of the accident, as sites of excess and contingency, lead to
an enormous proliferation of new aesthetics, fantasies, and forms of
securitization in modern warfare. To briefly examine a single example, a
growing literature assesses the potential for 'birdstrike', collision
between aircraft and birds, as a threat to military readiness and
security (Richardson and West, 2000). In the birdstrike literature, a
multiplicity of different bird species appear as a new type of security
threat because of their possible interference with the mechanical
operation of airplanes or other airlift operations. Birdstrikes are
exceedingly rare events, which number in the dozens despite thousands of
airlift operations since the invention of flight. Nonetheless,
significant attention, monetary investment, major proposals to redesign
airports, and even plans to disrupt the ecological habitats of bird
species have all been framed as questions of security and military
necessity (Heims, 2011; Kitowski, 2011; Zakrajsek and Bissonette, 2005).
Accidents like birdstrikes exist far afield from the typical concern of
critical security analysis, yet the writing on this subject reflects
Bataille's point since this literature treats the threat of the
birdstrike as an event verging on the miraculous. As Bataille argues,
the transformation of war into a practice of accumulation generates new
excesses and expenditures -- in this context, the contingent encounter
between plane and bird. A martial paradigm defined by accumulation and
rationality views these excesses and expenditures (the needless loss of
a plane) as a threat to security. Hence, even statistically infrequent
birdstrikes constitute an interruption of the dynamics of accumulation.
These events attract incredible attention because they involve
contingency and the possibility of sovereign experience and,
accordingly, become the subject of intense, affective investment. The
vivid fear for multiple bird species and the consistent concern for
aircraft malfunction reveal a social imaginary drawn to the potential
for excesses and contingencies no matter the form and no matter how
statistically insignificant. Here, the birds become the object of a
certain kind of martial horror. Bataille predicts exactly this shift at
the level of desire and imaginary and goes so far as to suggest that,
for moderns living under a paradigm of utility, only the 'imagination
has sovereign moments available to it' (Bataille 1991a: 256).
[Bataille's work points to these more liminal **accidents** as key
places where excesses invite speculation about the potential for
sovereign experience and, at the same time, incite **new practices** of
securitization.]{.underline} In this way, the birds, as a partially
aerial ecosystem, become a new source not just of anguish and threat,
but also of sovereign experience, a new target in the expansion of armed
conflict to new ecologies, and a relief from the onerous character of
modern social life. While birdstrikes represents only a single case,
several studies have highlighted the importance of transgressive and
liminal roles in contemporary warfare, such as crowdfunding as a new
mode of martial organization or the insurgent potential of improvised
explosive devices (Grove, 2016, 2019). These examples also reflect
Bataille's insights about how expenditure, excess, and the possibility
of sovereign experience animate and shift practices of mobilization and
violence in contemporary armed conflict. In a similar way, Bataille
shows how the accident (along with other forms of contingency) is not
anathema to risk-transfer warfare, but instead functions as a
supplement, a moment of transgression amidst the overt rationalization
of warfare. Modern discourses on precision rest on a form of knowledge
that precludes Bataille's sovereignty: 'knowledge is never sovereign, to
be sovereignty would have to occur in a moment. But the moment remains
outside, short of or beyond, all knowledge. . .. \[I\]n short, we know
nothing about what ultimately concerns us, what is supremely important
to us' (Bataille, 1991a: 202--203, emphasis in original). The paradigms
of risk-transfer warfare, of the minimization of the accident, and of
securitization more generally are not capable of thoughtfully generating
new forms of expenditure. They derive, for Bataille, from a fearful
response to finitude and solar economy: 'we try to escape from this
elementary horror, but, in the darkness and dead silence, it maintains
the unpredictable and elusive movement of everything we have not been
able to reduce to the reassuring order, a movement to which we know we
shall later succumb' (Bataille, 1991a: 217), a paradigm that incites not
only risk transfer warfare but arguably the broader pursuit of security.
[As a consequence, **securitized subjectivities** seek opportunities for
expenditure in transgressions such as the **accident**. These desires,
in turn, transform the structure of contemporary
**warfare**.]{.underline}

### Revisionism Link\-\--1NC

#### Their narrative of insecurity associates Asia with disorder and the West with stability\-\--that's epistemic racism.

**Pan 21**, PhD, Associate Professor of International Relations at
Deakin University (Chengxin, "Racialised politics of (in)security and
the COVID-19 Westfailure," *Critical Studies on Security*, 9:1, p. 41,
DOI: 10.1080/21624887.2021.1904195)

Racialised politics of (in)security in Western IR

[Mainstream]{.underline} Western [security thinking is predicated on a
self-gratifying **imaginative geography**: the world is
divided]{.underline} roughly [into]{.underline} **[two]{.underline}**
contrasting [**zones**, one marked by **community**, **order**, and
**security** and the other by **disorder**]{.underline}, insecurity,
[and **threat**]{.underline}. [These imagined binaries are]{.underline}
both **[underpinned by]{.underline}** and emblematic of [a]{.underline}
thinly disguised **[Orientalist]{.underline}**, Eurocentric,
[and]{.underline} ultimately [**racist** idea, which equates the former
zone]{.underline} more or less [with whites and the West, and the latter
with non-whites]{.underline} (Barkawi and Laffey 2006; Howell and
Richter-Montpetit 2019). The idea, often passed off as fact (through the
Western-dominated subfield of security studies), has been constitutive
of the highly racialised realities in international relations (Bell
2013; Carrozza, Danewid, and Pauls 2017; Ling 2017; Howell and
Richter-Montpetit 2020).

Although by no means unique to the West, [racism is a **key source
code** of mainstream Western theory and]{.underline} practice of
**[security]{.underline}**. [Security presupposes a
subject]{.underline}, namely, what or [who needs security or should feel
secure]{.underline}, [which]{.underline}, in turn, [implies]{.underline}
what or [whom is to be secured]{.underline} from or
[against]{.underline}. Central to security studies, therefore, is an
implicit ontological bifurcation between the subject of security and its
threat. And the [security]{.underline}/threat [boundaries
are]{.underline} often [drawn along a 'colour line']{.underline}
(Anievas, Manchanda, and Shilliam 2015).

[The racially-mediated binaries in security thinking and practice are
enabled above all by **an epistemic hierarchy**]{.underline}, which
insists on 'the inherent superiority of one's culture over all others'
(Le Melle 2009, 77). Indeed, [racism is first and foremost **epistemic
racism**]{.underline}, which is '[the **foundational form** ...of
racism]{.underline}' (Grosfoguel 2010, 29). [The West's
self-representation as the]{.underline} quintessential modern
[**knowing** subject, who has the **rational capacity** to **know** the
**objective world**]{.underline} (Pan 2012), [is **epistemic racism par
excellence**]{.underline}. Since '\[k\]nowing is then linked to the
possibility of control of the known' (Walker 1988, 51), [the knowing
Western subject can enjoy certainty, **dominance**, and security, hence
the self-image as a zone of security. The West's **epistemic
certainty**]{.underline} thus [helps **lay the groundwork** for its
sense]{.underline} [of]{.underline} moral, political, economic,
civilisational, and geostrategic **[superiority]{.underline}**
[vis-Ã -vis other]{.underline} races and [peoples]{.underline}, [whose
'epistemic inadequacy' makes them **irrational**]{.underline}, ignorant,
uncivilised, [and **ontologically threatening**]{.underline} (even if
occasionally blissfully innocent and/or exotically attractive).

[Threats from 'zones of insecurity' have **take**n **many
forms**]{.underline}: tribalism, terrorism, crime, disease,
overpopulation, unregulated migration, **[espionage]{.underline}**,
[unfair **trade**]{.underline} practice, anarchical,
**[illiberal]{.underline}** power **[politics]{.underline}**, or some
combinations of the above (Kaplan 1994; Huntington 1996). Intellectually
sophisticated and racially agnostic as they are, many
([Western]{.underline}) [security concepts]{.underline} and approaches
[are in effect about]{.underline} methodologies of
**[eliminating]{.underline}**, minimising, [or **containing**
the]{.underline} (largely non-Western) **['zone of
insecurity']{.underline}**, whether [through]{.underline} democracy
promotion, **[norm diffusion]{.underline}**, free trade, transnational
activism, emancipatory intervention **[and]{.underline}** developmental
assistance, or by ways of alliance building and expansion, [forward
defence]{.underline}, military **[deterrence]{.underline}**,
**[containment]{.underline}**, power balancing, and border control.

Of course, boundaries of zones of (in)security can change. But redrawing
such boundaries is again often the prerogative of scholars who
self-identify with the 'zone of security'. [They routinely play a
crucial cartographer's role in **mapping** and re-mapping zones of
(in)security]{.underline} (Huntington's civilisational map and the
'Indo-Pacific' come to mind).2 [Their dominance in the **process of
knowledge production**]{.underline} about (in)security and about IR more
broadly [exposes and]{.underline}, indeed, often [solidifies a **colour
line** in academia that mirrors the **racialised realities of
IR**]{.underline} (see also Henry, and Haastrup and Hagen in this
Issue). Scholars of colour and/or from the South are at best treated as
area specialists, a lowly ranked niche in the scholarly epistemic
hierarchy, thus perpetuating the epistemic status quo that has helped
produce the racially-mediated binaries in security thinking and practice
in the first place.

#### The 1AC superimposes Western geopolitics onto the Arctic, which marginalizes indigenous knowledges

**KÃ¶rber** et al **17**, PhD, Scandinavian Studies, Humboldt-Universitat

[Even in twenty-first-century environmental humanities, Indigenous and

**discourses** and prac- tices **of the Arctic**]{.underline}. And while

and Finland the SÃ¡mi are classified as a minority language group, not as

Yet another **[aspect deeply intertwined]{.underline}** with both
**[knowledge]{.underline}** [and **political territory**]{.underline}
[is the question of land use and **resource extraction**]{.underline}.

government of Greenland, Naalakkersuisut, has control of domestic

the "North" for formulating a national ideology is unique; no other
country uses "North" as a badge of honor. Yet the Canadian government

Reconciliation Report," which contained an Inuit sub-committee, was
released in 2015, and the recommendations have begun, after years of
neglect, to be taken on board by the government.

[These examples demonstrate that the questions of territory and sover-

### Extinction Outweighs\-\--Offense\-\--2NC

#### Extinction outweighs is extremist, unethical, and shaped by flawed epistemological traditions.

**Archer 24**, Assistant Professor @ Maastricht University,
interdisciplinary environmental social scientist interested in the
intersection of technology and sustainability (Matthew. \"6. Sustainable
Futures\". *Unsustainable: Measurement, Reporting, and the Limits of
Corporate Sustainability*, New York, USA: New York University Press,
2024, pp. 168-194. DOI: 10.18574/nyu/9781479822034.003.0009. Accessed:
7/18/24)

Even as this debate became increasingly heated---Nordhaus at one point
accused Stern of "\[taking\] the lofty vantage point of the world social
planner, perhaps stoking the dying embers of the British Empire, in
determining the way the world should combat the dangers of global
warming"---Nordhaus applauded Stern's "\[selection of\] climate change
policies with an eye to balancing economic priorities with environmen-
tal dangers."16 [Conceptually, at least, the existential risk of
longtermism is not so far away from this quantified approach to the
commensuration of economic values with climate risks, and yet the former
seems radical while the latter seems relatively banal. From both of
these perspectives, the future is viewed through the **epistemological
lens of quantifiable risks and derivable discount rates**, populated by
statistical lives]{.underline}. [The policy implications of this
**should not be understated**]{.underline}. At one end of the spectrum,
[there are those who argue that our current happiness matters most and
that we should not make too many sacrifices today to help people in the
future; at the other end of the spectrum, there are those who argue that
no cost is too high to avoid existential risks, leading to a
characterization of events like the **Spanish flu** and the
**Holocaust** (and presumably the ongoing **COVID-19** pandemic) as
"**scarcely registering**" as **existential catastrophes.**]{.underline}

[In the anthropologist Ann Laura Stoler's work on the politics of co-
lonial archives, she identifies what she calls the vernacular
"**practicing epistemologies**" of powerful actors---"what they imagined
they could know and more importantly what epistemic habits they
developed to know it]{.underline}."17 From effective altruists like
Stephen to the data analysts, ESG advisers, and social impact
consultants we met in earlier chapters, the practicing epistemology of
sustainability professionals is relatively straightforward. [They
imagine that they can know the impacts of their various sustainability
projects in a way that allows these impacts to be compared with the
costs of these projects' implementation, whether it is a project geared
toward ending world hunger, mitigating the spread of HIV, reducing child
slavery, or promoting private-sector innovation in green technology.
Sustainability professionals want their interventions to be rational and
based on reason, for the positive impacts of these in- terventions to be
maximized relative to their investments, for the costs of sustainability
to be offset by its benefits.]{.underline} Their [practicing epistemol-
ogy presupposes that costs and benefits are **comparable and thus com-
mensurable** and that a common metric exists or can at least be
derived]{.underline}. The [epistemic habits they develop to know whether
an intervention or an investment is having the desired impacts are
equally straightforward: they develop and refine the definitions of
various social and environ- mental indicators]{.underline}, and they
amass a huge amount of data about those indicators.

**[Particular epistemologies]{.underline}**---that is, particular ways
of producing knowledge and particular ways of determining what counts as
knowl- edge in the first place---**[structure social reality and are
thus inseparable from ontology and ethics]{.underline}**.18 This
interrelatedness is fundamental to the notion of "[Indigenous
epistemology," which "assumes all epistemologi- cal systems to be
socially constructed and (in)formed through sociopo- litical, economic,
and historical context and processes."19 Recognizing the entanglements
of being, doing, and knowing is crucial to an ethics of worlding that
accounts for how various practices matter from per- spectives beyond the
myopic view of the market and the people whose power it obscures. If the
reality is that for-profit companies, "by their very nature," are
unsustainable and that the corporate form is inherently ecocidal, then
an **approach to sustainability that is grounded in produc- ing
quantitative data about impacts so the market can manage them is not
only a fool's errand but also deeply and irredeemably
unethical.**]{.underline}20

Reflecting on Indigenous practices of pyro-[regeneration, in which
controlled burns clear undergrowth and allow new plants and animals to
flourish, the archaeologist Paulette Steeves coined the term "pyro-
epistemology" to describe an important facet of critical Indigenous
scholarship: "A practice of Pyro-epistemology through the ceremony of
Indigenous ways of knowing, being, and doing is one **which cleanses the
academic landscape of discussions that misinform worldviews and fuel
misunderstanding and racism**. Such literary renewal clears the way for
**new discussions and intellectual growth in academic fields of thought
and centers of knowledge production**]{.underline}."21 Steeves's focus
on clearing the way for new discussions in centers of knowledge
production is relevant to the way sustainability professionals, through
the enforcement of their increasingly hegemonic practicing
epistemologies, reinforce enduring forms of oppression even as they
attempt to improve the lives of others. [Tracing the relationship
between the practicing epistemology of sustain- ability professionals
and the kind of future we might expect to emerge if that epistemology
remains dominant demonstrates the stakes of such a pyro-epistemology.
The politics of knowledge extend far beyond stilted academic
practitioner debates about what counts as evidence of sustainable; in
dismantling the practicing epistemology of neoliberal sustainability, we
come a step closer to what David Gegeo and Karen Watson-Gegeo call
"dehegemonization" and **rejecting** the **normalization of elite
visions of the future**]{.underline}.22

### Extinction Outweighs\-\--Defense\-\--2NC

#### 1. Calculating disasters as infinite makes it [impossible]{.underline} to accurately resolve any threat. 

**Bartha 24**, Professor and Head of the Department of Philosophy,
University of British Columbia, Vancouver (Paul. \"23 Pascal's Wager and
the Precautionary Principle\". *Ontology of Divinity*, edited by
MirosÅ‚aw Szatkowski, Berlin, Boston: De Gruyter, 2024, pp. 467-492. DOI:
10.1515/9783111332536-026. Accessed: 7/18/24)

According to Munthe (2019), [given that the basic premise of the
precautionary principle is the avoidance of catastrophic harms, \...
there seems to be no rationally justifiable end to the amount of
resources and time that we should spend on trying to clarify an
existential risk further\... Each such risk, therefore, appears as a
'black hole]{.underline}' that, by the awesome normative power of the
mere ultimateness of the harm it threatens us with, **[sucks into its
void all other things that might have been mobilized for the pursuit of
the good in human decision-making]{.underline}**. (Munthe, 2019, p. 51)
As Munthe frames it, the black hole challenge involves a nightmare of
**[ceaseless dithering about disaster scenarios]{.underline}**: [the
threat of ultimate harms "suck\[s\] all available resources into endless
endeavors to clarify **ridiculous eventualities** of **horrific
things**]{.underline}" (Munthe, 2019, p. 58). I construe the problem
more broadly: [PP seems to entail that 100% of our time, money and
energy should be devoted to the prevention of catastrophe. As an
instance of the black hole challenge, consider NASA Budget, adapted from
Steel and Bartha (2022). Suppose that NASA has to decide how much of its
budget to devote to preventing a catastrophic asteroid impact with Earth
and how much to other valuable missions]{.underline} (space exploration,
the Webb telescope, etc.). [Given NASA's ability to reduce the
existential threat of an asteroid collision, PP appears to recommend
that the entire budget be spent on asteroid tracking, developing and
testing methods of asteroid deflection, and so forth. This absurd
recommendation is analogous to Tabarrok's fanaticism objection to
Pascal's Wager]{.underline}. 17

#### 2. It also breaks down: if I asserted the plan also CAUSED extinction, there would be no possible action to take!

**Bartha 24**, Professor and Head of the Department of Philosophy,
University of British Columbia, Vancouver (Paul. \"23 Pascal's Wager and
the Precautionary Principle\". *Ontology of Divinity*, edited by
MirosÅ‚aw Szatkowski, Berlin, Boston: De Gruyter, 2024, pp. 467-492. DOI:
10.1515/9783111332536-026. Accessed: 7/18/24)

[Several authors have alleged that the precautionary principle is unable
to handle situations where there is more than one potential catastrophe.
They explicitly draw parallels to the many-gods objection, and as with
that objection, their criticisms take different forms.]{.underline}

The first is a charge of incoherence: [PP makes contradictory demands.
For example, Sunstein (2005) considers a version of PP that triggers
precautionary measures if there is any chance of catastrophic harm. But
if the precautionary measures themselves might have catastrophic
consequences, PP both recommends and prohibits them. In a similar
spirit, Manson (2002) imagines a scenario in which there is a tiny
probability that drastic cuts in greenhouse gas emissions might lead to
worldwide economic depression, political instability, war, and a
disastrous nuclear holocaust. It **appears that PP both recommends and
prohibits the drastic emissions cuts**]{.underline}. 15 Here, Manson
explicitly notes the parallel with Pascal's Wager and the many-gods
objection. Sandin (2009, p. 92) describes a similar dilemma:

Suppose someone argues, with reference to the precautionary principle,
that protocell research should be stopped because such research might,
through some possible but extremely unlikely causal chain result in the
end of all natural life on Earth\... It might then be countered that
there is an alternative scenario, also possible, in which not proceeding
with protocell research leads to the extinction of all natural life on
Earth\... \[Given that both scenarios are possible\] we should,
according to the precautionary principle, both proceed and not proceed
with protocell research.

[The second form of the many-disasters objection is a charge of
paralysis. Sandin (2009) points out that PP can be formulated as the
recommendation to "**abstain from all actions that might have harmful
consequences.**" In this case there is no incoherence, but "we are
**offered no action guidance** at all."]{.underline}

Finally, [the third form is a charge of non-discrimination.]{.underline}
Here, we modify an example due to Colyvan et al. (2010), Mangrove
Forest, to suggest that [PP fails to discriminate properly between two
remedies that might be adopted to prevent environmental disaster.
Suppose that both the destruction (D) of a mangrove forest and the
destruction (H) of half the forest are considered catastrophic outcomes.
Act A will destroy half the forest, while act B will destroy the entire
forest. It seems clear that B is worse than A, but PP provides no basis
for discrimination. For if we assign utility --âˆž to both D and H, then
both A and B have negative infinite utility (Table 5), so the two
options are **equally bad.** This is **plainly wrong!**]{.underline}

## ALTERNATIVE

### Afterlife Alt\-\--1NC

#### The alternative is a reading of the aff through [Alterlife]{.underline} -- a praxis grounded in [rejecting the Western frameworks]{.underline} constitutive of the affirmative and embracing the knowledge created by Indigenous people in the 'Arctic Wasteland' 

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
