**[I'd like to push a bit on the strategic necessity to argue for the
legible fullness of ice geographies, both ecologically and
socially]{.underline}**. **[The response to imbue ice geographies with
vibrancy is also to try to imbue ice with more acceptable qualities for
those unfamiliar with those spaces]{.underline}**. **[The urge is to
fill ice geographies with notions of normative life, to unmake or
improve its "wasteland" nature into something more recognizably aligned
with normative or capitalist value, something vibrant or even
pure.]{.underline}** [I'd like to offer a way to relate to ice and ice
melt in a similar way to what M. Murphy calls **"chemical
alterlife."**]{.underline} 13 **[Alterlife questions the normative
senses of what life is and "asks for an unflinchingly pessimistic
acknowledgment that these chemical relations are racist, harmful, even
deadly, and that it is up to you to take on the ways that you are caught
up in the killing (even if they are killing you too, just more
softly)."]{.underline}** 14 [Each of us is implicated with ice melt.
Though, like the metabolization of chemicals, there is an unequal felt
experience15 of that melt and, of course, the unequal contribution of a
capitalist- carbon "footprint" that makes melt in the first place.16 In
this alterlife, in which we are all caught up in the differently
experienced melt, the generative capacity of emptiness can act as a
reframing. I will not rush to narratively fill emptiness, to beautify it
toward progress.]{.underline} **[Erica Violet Lee writes, "A wasteland
is a place where, we are taught, there is nothing and no one is
salvageable. . . . Wastelands are spaces deemed unworthy of healing
because of the scale and amount of devastation that has occurred there.
Wastelands are named wastelands by the ones responsible for their
devastation."]{.underline}** [17 I add that wastelands are named
wastelands by the ones who have no relation, no history to lands deemed
empty, wasted, and/or destroyed. I'm unconvinced by the need to prove
otherwise, to fill emptiness. In looking closely at a snowflake, the
building material of ice, each snow crystal is shaped by its corners,
openings, bubbles, and empty spaces. **These characteristics are what
fundamentally constitute the productive and capacious nature of ice,
both scientifically and aesthetically. Empty spaces allow for matter to
temporarily cohere. Empty spaces allow for the unknotting of the bind
that yokes us as researchers to believe that we must know and report on
every knowable thing in every knowable aspect about our research
subject, object, place, and interlocutors, to name and intellectualize a
feeling into data before we've let it wash over and saturate

requirement for full, complete interpretation or translation. In
emptiness, critical co-theorizing, productive touching, and
contamination among us can occur without a final answer,** magnifying

complete mastery. In emptiness, there is also what I offer as careful
guessing or careful guesswork.]{.underline} **[Building from critical
Indigenous studies, guesswork honors emptiness, respecting the refusals,

that is desire- based]{.underline}**,18 **[that does not seek to build
new theories that displace Indigenous theorizing.]{.underline}**19
[Careful guessing is critically attentive to the ongoing dimensions and
histories of power relations that shape the responses to the question at
hand and the forms of questioning that seem possible and
impossible.]{.underline} [Who and what shape my questions that seem
naturalized to me? Careful guess-work upholds that I can guess to the
best of my knowledge, with accountability to place and peoples,20 and
with an ethic of anti- coloniality and decoloniality when
possible.]{.underline}21 **[Careful guesswork insists that objectivity
is an impossibility, that researchers are narrators who craft

to be consensual among all forms of our interlocutors, relations, and
the careful guessing that has emerged before us.]{.underline}**2 [In my

### Black and Native Praxis Alt\-\--1NC

#### The alternative is resistance grounded in Black and Native Praxis. 

**Smith '25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
Berkeley in Comparative Ethnic Studies (Jen.Â Ice geographies: The

**[If colonial insistence on the unmoving category of the terrestrial
might undermine water and ice in some ways, water and ice can also help
break those categories.]{.underline}** As Philip Steinberg and Kimberley
Peters write, "**[State ontology . . . is profoundly terrestrial. . . .
The modern state system is premised on a distinction between solid land
and liquid sea . . . ice confuses and complicates acts of territorial
and sovereign control. . . . The three- dimensional extent of the sea .
. . makes observation and knowledge, and therefore geopolitical control,
problematic]{.underline}**." 28 [Ice is land, ice is not land. Non-
Native juridical systems and cartography along with other property
regimes built to dispossess Native land have never been capacious enough
to represent the full contours of Indigenous relationships.29 Due to the
hyperattention land is given, there is little legal recourse with which
to strategically]{.underline} claim [other forms as relations and
beings. There have been several outstanding examples against the trend,
such as the Yurok Tribe granting legal personhood to the Klamath River
in 2019; Mutehekau Shipu (Magpie River) was declared a legal person by
the Innu Council of Ekuanitshit; and the residents in Orange County,
Florida, voted to grant rights to the Econlockhatchee and Wekiva Rivers.
Outside of the United States and Canada, Native polities and communities
are using legal realms in creative ways to claim and protect waters.
**In Aotearoa in 2017, the Whanganui River was granted legal personhood
status following the efforts and mobilizing of MÄori. In the following
pages, I'm concerned with how such relations and beings have been taken
up centrally across Black Diaspora studies and Oceania studies.** I co-
theorize with intellectuals and experts whose conceptualizations and
labors, in relation to their communities, are meant to somehow be
separate from ruminations on land and ice. In my ask to build alliance,
I wish to recognize that these formulations to water, land, and ice are
distinct and varied but certainly not unrelated]{.underline}. **[Land,
water, and ice thought together insist that to know land we must also
know water--- not a new formulation to many Black and Indigenous
thinkers across time--- and to know land and water we must also know
ice.]{.underline}** [Scholars writing about water and ocean as analytic,
either explicitly or inexplicitly, often upend the centrality of the
European continent. **In recent iterations, the turn away from the
continental has also been a turn away from land and terrestrial as
center. In much of Indigenous law, water relations are central to the
functioning of community and not in ways that divide water from
land.**]{.underline} [Enactments of water dispossession and theft
demonstrate its fundamental nature similarly to land. The privatization
of water rights, pollution of clean drinking water sources and other
forms of environmental contamination, and the damming of rivers by
colonial infrastructure, as well as attendant actions such as water
protection that emerge as a result, illustrate the crucial role of water
and access to water.30 Not unlike how land is regulated through settler
law, water has been legislated as a necessity, particularly in arid
regions needed for agriculture, and through the uneven protection of
tribal water rights. **Water cannot be overdetermined by colonial
bindings alone: the waters that run through Indigenous lands are the
arteries that feed and bind humans and their kin.31 As Melanie K. Yazzie
and Cutcha Risling Baldy write of water across Indigenous decolonization
stru**ggles, "Water is seen as an ancestor and as a relative with agency
within this network of life, one who deserves]{.underline} [respect,
care, and protection."]{.underline} 32 [These forms of relation are
common across Indigenous communities and in the same breath take

**[The Colorado River is the most endangered river in the

**[States--- also, it is a part of my body.]{.underline}**

**[I carry a river. It is who I am: 'Aha Makav. This is not a

**[the middle of our body, the same way it runs through the

**[of our land.]{.underline}**

**[never been true in America. America is my myth.33]{.underline}**

In these stanzas, '**[Aha Makav are inseparable entities simultaneously
land, water, and the human body, entwined and impossible to parse. Diaz

### Take Life Alt\-\--1NC

#### The alternative is to Take Life -- a method grounded in rejecting western forms of survival and sovereignty in favor of understanding the patterns and calls from the Land. 

**Smith 25** Rose Smith is a dAXunhyuu (Eyak, Alaska Native) geographer
interested in the intersections of coloniality, race, and indigeneity as
read through aesthetic and literary contributions, archival evidences,
and experiential embodied knowledges. She received her Ph.D. @ UC
Berkeley in Comparative Ethnic Studies (Jen.Â Ice geographies: The
colonial politics of race and indigeneity in the Arctic. Duke University
Press, 2025.)//kaykayðŸ§Ÿ

[Warren Miaraq Jones discusses similar tenets in his essay "The
Philosophy of the Circumpolar North," wherein he writes]{.underline},
**["The arctic and subarctic are unforgiving for those who don't
understand them. If you don't understand and respect the environment it
will take your life. Modern technology and life delude people into a
false sense of security. This land will still kill you."]{.underline}**
57 [This philosophy is not meant to dramatize the severity or reinforce
a hard- edged masculinity of ice geographies but is one that proffers an
orientation to landscape through a set of laws and relationship that
have been honed, refined, and upheld over time]{.underline}. Tanya Tagaq
writes, **["The Land has no hierarchy. The Land has no manners; you only
obey and enjoy what is afforded to you by her greatness]{.underline}**.
**[Only logic and great care ensure your survival. Only patterns of
skills gifted by our ancestors keep us living . . . we obey or we
succumb."]{.underline}** 58 Bathsheba Demuth's writing aligns in saying,
**["In the Arctic, such attention is not an option but is necessary, and
it yields appreciation for the precarity, the contingency, of simply
being."]{.underline}** 59 [Jones writes in another essay about this
formulation as a particular kind of Yup'ik awareness, drawing from
Harold Napoleon's writing on yuuyaraq, or "the way of the human being."
60 Jones states, "Our laws were known by all, and every Yuk was
responsible for holding themselves and others accountable to yuuyaraq
laws and structures. Yuuyaraq is the product of thousands of years of
wisdom from people living and learning the lessons of the
North."]{.underline} 61 **[This Yup'ik sense of awareness is formulated
in co- constitution with the land and its particularities, making it
resonate with Bigjim's offering that an Inupiaq life is "so involved /
with the movement / of the ice floe."]{.underline}** [The necessities
for attentive excellence in ice geographies are repeated across
literature originating with some of the first Inuit novels ever
published, Mitiarjuk Nappaaluk's Sanaaq and Markoosie Patsauq's Hunter
with Harpoon.]{.underline} 62 [Both novels offer spare prose that
chronicle the daily and seasonal practices of hunting, harvesting,
cleaning, and preparing food.]{.underline} [Each novel features a
protagonist who leads us through the daily goings- on in their homeland
icescape. In Nappaaluk we follow Sanaaq, and in Patsauq we follow Kamik,
two kinds of coming- of- age stories about a young woman and man,
respectively.]{.underline} In each story **[we learn of the
relationships and constant presence and alertness needed for each task
in Arctic spaces. There is little room for mistake, and movements must
be made with care and attention.]{.underline}** Moreover, [the "setting"
of the icescape---the milieu or atmosphere of the cold--- is its own
character. For instance, in Sanaaq, characters dive beneath the ice to
gather mussels, and even though Sanaaq's mother, Qumaq, does not want
her young daughter to go gathering for fear she "might bang into
something," she relents. Nappaaluk writes: "Qalingu began bashing away
with the ice chisel to open up a tunnel down into a large qulluniq. With
his hands he removed the shards of ice. He looked really tired. When the
opening was wide enough, he knocked the last shards out and, with the
water level no longer falling, everyone rushed to climb down under the
ice. 'Let's go! Let's go down,' said Arnatuinaaq very happily. . . . And
they went down."]{.underline} 63 **[Sadly for Qalingu, when gathering
mussels under the ice, he does not look closely enough to see that what
he collects are empty shells, much to the humorous delight of his
friends who later share their harvest with him. The characters in Sanaaq
are below and above the ice, swimming through the water and tunnelling
through the opening--- not simply participating upon the
surface.]{.underline}**

# AFF

## FRAMEWORK

### Arctic Debates Good\-\--2AC

#### Debates about arctic policy changes are [valuable]{.underline} for student skills, critical thinking, and ethical self-reflexivity. 

**Essex 24**, Department of Political Science @ University of Windsor.
(Jamey, 2024, "Coming in from the Cold: Arctic Council Debate Simulation
and the Political Geographies of a Global Region," The Geography
Teacher, Routledge, Taylor & Francis Group)

Liberal and realist theories---and the **[debate]{.underline}** between
them--- have dominated the field of IR for decades and strongly
[shaped]{.underline} not only [how we understand]{.underline} the
**[international state system]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} states as
territorial and sovereign actors but also the actual practice of
**[statecraft]{.underline}** and the makeup and operation of many
international institutions (Snyder 2004). The scenario exercise
described in more detail below, which builds from tensions between
realism and liberalism in institutions such as the Arctic Council, was
developed in the context of a senior seminar for university
undergraduate IR students. It is here, though, that geographic
perspectives on the international system, and the Arctic as a kind of
place, help complicate the liberal-realist dichotomy that dominates much
of IR thinking and that often presents geographic space in static ways.
Changes in the nature of the state and the conduct of international
relations together with economic globalization, climate change, and the
reemergence of nationalism have all challenged realist and liberal
notions of state sovereignty and territory. [This]{.underline} has
prompted a "**[spatial turn]{.underline}**" in the disciplines of
Political Science and IR, as scholars in those fields [have tried to
take spatial relations and geographic organization and representation
more seriously]{.underline}. Yet Clark and Jones (2013, 306; see also
Jones and Clark 2020) argue that attempts at working across disciplinary
divides often still founder on a basic sense of space as "an abstract,
uniform, featureless medium upon which human political action is played
out" [rather than building]{.underline} an understanding of [space and
geography as]{.underline} **[constitutive elements]{.underline}** [of
**international political relations**]{.underline}. Taking for granted
states as territorial units exercising sovereignty through
administration and defense of clearly bounded national space remains a
bedrock of mainstream IR theories.

By contrast, [political geographers]{.underline} have attempted to
understand and **[critique]{.underline}** these
[taken-for-granted]{.underline} notions of **[sovereignty]{.underline}**
[based in state territory]{.underline} for at least three decades.
Agnew's (1994, 58) work on the "territorial trap" in Political Science
and IR approaches is most useful here, and in this he argues that "three
contextual factors have interacted to reproduce the dominant view about
state territoriality" that limits these disciplines' ability to
understand shifts in sovereignty and the state's basis in territorial
control. [These are]{.underline}, first, a "**[preference]{.underline}**
[for abstract]{.underline} [and]{.underline}
'**[closed-system']{.underline}** [thinking among advocates of a
scientific (positivist) approach]{.underline}" that takes the
territorial state as the pregiven basic unit of analysis; second, the
"muddling of two terms, state and nation" in a way that "endows the
territorial state with the legitimacy of representing and expressing the
'character' or 'will' of the nation"; and finally, an "intellectual
division of labour" that dates to the First World War and that too
neatly separates the international arena and domestic politics as
different areas of study and practice (Agnew 1994, 58--59). While some
Political Science and IR scholars (Ethington and McDaniel 2007; Kadercan
2015; Kristensen 2015; see also Murphy 1999; O\'Loughlin 2018; Jones and
Clark 2020) have tried to incorporate a more geographically nuanced
approach, [these three traits continue to shape how these fields see the
state, territory, and the conduct of international affairs.]{.underline}

[**Political** and environmental **changes**]{.underline} [in the
**Arctic**]{.underline}, and the Arctic Council itself, [provide a
useful case for exploring the]{.underline} **[interplay between
disciplines]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} **[challenges to narrow
territory-based understandings of the state as a kind of political actor
contending with contemporary global issues.]{.underline}** Geographer
Klaus Dodds (2018, 191) noted in explaining the concept of the "global
Arctic" that the northern polar region "is not ... imagined to be a
regional container with hard boundaries but rather something created by
the intersection of territories and flows," the kind of place that makes
borders hard to demarcate and police while also disrupting rigid
concepts of national security. [Much of this need to rethink security
and sovereignty in the Arctic stems from the environmental processes at
work in the region, which is both a setting for some of the more extreme
consequences of climate change]{.underline} (e.g., rapidly decreasing
sea ice, melting of permafrost, ocean warming, and numerous positive
feedback loops that strengthen climate change processes) and a harbinger
of wider global-scale climate impacts, especially as the region opens
more widely to resource exploitation, maritime shipping, and
geopolitical tensions (Gerhardt et al. 2010; Steinberg & Dodds 2013;
Steinberg et al. 2015). It is no wonder, then, that many non-Arctic
states and actors (i.e., those without national territory above the
Arctic Circle) have taken a greater interest in the use and management
of the Arctic.

Lesson Overview

The lesson described here follows from the premises described above: The
Arctic is a global region but is divided and managed across many forms
of sovereignty; experiencing profound environmental changes with local,
regional, and global impacts; and a space in which traditional notions
and practices of state sovereignty and geopolitical thinking are being
both entrenched and challenged. It follows **[a simulation
format]{.underline}** [in which]{.underline} **[students]{.underline}**
are asked to [take on specific roles]{.underline} representing various
members of the Arctic Council [and]{.underline} to [act out]{.underline}
via **[research]{.underline}**, **[debate]{.underline}**, a formal vote,
[and]{.underline} subsequent **[writing]{.underline}** [of]{.underline}
reports **[a particular hypothetical]{.underline}** question [that can
demonstrate how]{.underline} liberal and realist **[theories in IR
operate]{.underline}** in practice [and how]{.underline} **[critical
geographic concepts]{.underline}** of region, scale, territory, and
state sovereignty can [help push]{.underline} **[beyond the
limits]{.underline}** [of these approaches]{.underline} in IR. The
hypothetical question posed centers on the addition of a non-Arctic
state--- China---as a permanent voting member of the Arctic Council. In
the scenario for the simulation, China has decided to move from being an
observer "near-Arctic" state and press for full membership on the
Council. This follows from recent moves by China to establish a stronger
regional presence through economic and scientific partnerships with
Arctic states as well as China's development of its own Arctic strategy,
which was released in January 2018 and describes the country as "an
important stakeholder in Arctic affairs" (State Council Information
Office of the People's Republic of China 2018).

While there has been no specific push for China to move from observer
status at the Arctic Council to full voting member and it is unclear
whether such a move would even be possible given the constraints of the
organization and the Ottawa Declaration, a hypothetical scenario like
this permits some creative thinking based in real-world possibilities
and strategies and can help students overcome biases and preconceived
ideas that may be inherent in dealing with existing scenarios based on
current events (Taylor 2013; Shaw and Switky 2018). [Simulating a full
special Arctic Council meeting at which this application is
**presented**, **debated**]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} **[voted
upon]{.underline}** [allows students to **research**]{.underline},
present, [and]{.underline} **[debate]{.underline}** [the role of
**non-** and near-Arctic **states**]{.underline} in this global region
and, given the Council's varied membership, to investigate the meaning
of territory and sovereignty in relation to national interests,
Indigenous rights, and regional and global environmental stewardship.
Guided by the core tenets of liberal and realist understandings of
international relations and the political geographic critique of these
approaches, [students develop a sense of the complicated realities of
international institutions in practice and the intersection of multiple
issues across place and scale in the Arctic region.]{.underline}

Goals and Time Required

In this scenario, students represent different members of the Arctic
Council, with one representative from a member state acting as chair and
assuring that the agenda is followed and speakers are recognized and
held to time limits, as they debate China's application for full voting
membership. In doing so and presenting positions for or against the
inclusion of China as a full member, they may consider various points
addressed in background readings and primary documents, discussed below,
and the following specific questions, which can help prepare students
for the in-class Arctic Council meeting simulation itself:

â€¢ **[What]{.underline}** major processes and **[trends]{.underline}**
[are driving]{.underline} political, social, economic, and environmental
**[change]{.underline}** in the Arctic region**[?]{.underline}** [What
are the pressing issues]{.underline} that the Council might have to
address in this, and how would the addition of China as a full member
enhance or complicate the Arctic Council's capacity to fulfill its
mandate[?]{.underline}

What are the principles that underlie the Arctic Council's existence and
mandate? What strategies and processes within and around the Council
ensure this mandate is met (or not)?

How do the different members and observers within the Arctic Council see
that body's role in managing the Arctic as a shared, global region and
providing a platform for pursuing national and international interests?

[What are the specific national or organizational interests]{.underline}
of the different actors within the Arctic Council, and how might China's
more complete integration into that body alter the dynamics of the
Council and how other actors approach it[?]{.underline}

â€¢ **[What forms of power are relevant to the hypothetical scenario
presented]{.underline}**, and how might different actors within the
Council present themselves within the context of a high-level
deliberation and potential vote on an issue of this
magnitude**[?]{.underline}**

How does the issue of China's status in the Arctic Council relate to
both the definition of the Arctic as a region and to broader themes and
trends beyond but affecting that region? [How does the Arctic's position
as a shared region shape different members' particular interests but
also how they view the intersection of issues across scales such as
climate change, economic development, cultural and political rights, and
global status?]{.underline}

This is a wide set of questions and, in practice, a full class meeting
of an undergraduate seminar course, totaling almost three hours, worked
well for this simulation, with additional outof-class time before and
after for preparation and writing of a summary report. This time allowed
for each member state, Indigenous representative, and observer state or
group to make a brief position statement, followed by open debate and a
final vote. This could be broken across two class periods, one for
statements and one for debate and vote, with time in between for side
meetings and negotiation, as would be common in any international body
that could take multiple hours or even days to consider and decide on
such a move. Like any high-level meeting, a clear agenda and
parliamentary procedure would guide the deliberations; a sample agenda
is shown in Figure 1.

Outcomes

The direct outcome of the discussion and debate about China's Arctic
Council membership application is a formal vote on the motion, "Should
the People's Republic of China be accepted as a full member of the
Arctic Council?" In practice, only the representatives of the Council's
eight member states would vote, while the six Indigenous peoples'
organizations that are permanent participants have the right to be
consulted on all Council decisions and negotiations and are supported in
this work by the Indigenous Peoples' Secretariat. Currently,
thirty-eight observers also have status within the Council, contributing
to projects, programs, and research initiatives through the Working
Groups that structure the body's primary activities. The observer group
consists of thirteen non-Arctic states (including China), thirteen
intergovernmental organizations, and twelve nongovernmental
organizations, providing a wide range of actors from which to assign
roles to students in setting up the simulation. While final
decision-making is in the hands of the eight Arctic state members, they
work by consensus rather than simple majoritarian votes, providing ample
room for students to negotiate and debate points of contention in
dealing with the particular scenario presented.

[While Indigenous representatives do not vote, the duty to consult them
and their right to address the Council during meetings give them a
powerful voice and an almost de facto veto power in the Council's
deliberations. These factors also complicate the notion that sovereignty
in the Arctic is exercised by states alone or that it is based solely on
territorial control by states]{.underline}. As Indigenous peoples often
reside across national boundaries and have rights articulated through
international framework agreements, most importantly the UN Declaration
on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples, incorporating their voices more
directly in Council deliberations and the simulation exercise also
prevents national interest and security from being read simplistically
and directly from state strategies. This also follows robust discussions
within the Council and Indigenous people's organizations about
Indigenous sovereignty and self-determination and highlights differences
within and between the permanent participants so that they are not read
as a single bloc with a unitary set of interests, capacities, or
national contexts (Cambou 2018; Landriault, Payette, and Roussel 2021).
[Likewise, the wide array of observer groups and states that would
provide consultative feedback on the question of **China's elevation**
from **observer** to full **voting member** introduces non-state actors
into consideration and **forces a debate** over the **political**,
**social**, **economic**, and **environmental** impacts of allowing a
**near-Arctic state** to sit alongside **Arctic
counterparts**]{.underline}. Following the statements, deliberation, and
vote in class, students write a report (at the senior undergraduate
level, students were asked to aim for five to seven pages) on the
primary issues that were debated, especially considerations of
sovereignty, territory, and geopolitics in a global Arctic; how these
related to their particular role in the simulation; and how these can
help students understand liberal and realist theories in IR. [In
addition to building familiarity with the specific empirical content
addressed in this simulation, such as issues around climate change,
sovereignty and territory, and geographic perspectives on IR theories,
students build research, presentation, and collaboration skills and gain
an appreciation for the complexity and nuance of geopolitics, diplomacy,
and international relations in practice.]{.underline}

### AT Research Only\-\--2AC

#### That's best: aiming for epistemic purity is [conservative passivity]{.underline} and undermines the [retooling of liberalism]{.underline} their framework [aspires to]{.underline}. 

**Vickers 19**, Department of Education, Kyushu University (Edward
Vickers, 2019, "Critiquing coloniality, 'epistemic violence' and western
hegemony in comparative education -- the dangers of ahistoricism and
positionality," Comparative Education Journal, University of Kansas
Libraries, Taylor and Francis)

The empirical and theoretical flaws of this approach are intertwined
with the problematic language in which its arguments are typically
couched. Historical and anthropological scholarship on East Asia and
other regions amply demonstrates that colonialist or neo-colonialist
attitudes and strategies of domination are not and have never been a
Western monopoly. But decolonial theory posits the more or less uniform
victimhood of non-Western 'others,' deriving claims for the moral
superiority of 'authentically' indigenous perspectives. Debating the
validity of such claims is complicated by an emphasis on
'positionality.' Readers are exhorted to judge an argument less by
standards of evidence or logic (often portrayed as camouflaging a
Western 'will to power') than on the basis of the writer's
self-identification or 'positioning.' [The language of '**epistemic
violence**,']{.underline} 'secure spaces,' 'epistemological diffidence,'
'border thinking' and 'location' [suggests an **image** of the
**critical scholar** as revolutionary guerrilla, valiantly **sniping**
at Western **hegemony** from \[their\]]{.underline} ~~his or her~~
[marginal redoubt]{.underline}. In so far as this reflects a desire for
a more just, tolerant and sustainable society -- one that values
diversity as a resource for mutual learning -- it is admirable.
[However, if we seek to **combat oppression**, in the educational sphere
or beyond, it is **incumbent** on us to **pick our
enemies**]{.underline}, and our language, [carefully. Aiming a
**blunderbuss** at the supposedly illegitimate]{.underline} or
self-serving ['universalism']{.underline} of 'modern Western social
science,' [while **ignoring** how calls for indigenisation and
'**authenticity**' are used to **legitimate** highly **oppressive
regimes** across **Asia** and elsewhere, is to risk **undermining**
those **universal social** and political values (freedom of
**expression**, civil **liberties**, rule of **law**) upon which
**critical scholars themselves rely**]{.underline}.

An embrace of 'opacity' or 'epistemological diffidence,' advocated by
several of the CER contributors, threatens to be similarly
self-defeating. While they share an admiration for the Argentine
theorist of 'decoloniality,' Walter Mignolo, the work of his brilliant
compatriot, the writer, poet and essayist Jorge Luis Borges, is far
worthier of attention. Borges' famous fondness for 'labyrinths' and the
paradoxical was combined with a sharp eye for gratuitous obfuscation and
circumlocution (see, for example, his story The Aleph, in Borges 1998,
274--286). Offering his own critique of the fashion for opaque jargon in
mainstream social science, the Ã©migrÃ© Polish sociologist Stanislav
Andreski wrote acerbically that 'one of the pleasures obtainable through
recourse to confusion and absurdity is to be able to feel, and publicly
to claim, that one knows when in reality one does not' (1974, 95).
'Opacity' in imaginative literature may intrigue or entertain, but in
interpreting and explaining unfamiliar societies, cultures and education
systems, comparativists especially ought to write in clear, accessible
language. And while all social scientists can understand the lure of the
sweeping generalisation, we should generalise with extreme caution,
especially when categorising large swathes of humanity.

Borges' earliest collection of stories is entitled A Universal History
of Iniquity. This appeared in 1935, when there were already rumblings in
both East and West of the conflict that would soon engulf Eurasia.
Implied in his title was a truth painfully obvious to many
contemporaries: that iniquity is indeed universal. The conflicts of the
mid-twentieth century starkly illuminated another truth: that iniquity
in the modern world, especially (though not only) that associated with
totalitarian societies, often consists in essentialising and
de-humanising 'the other'. Hannah Arendt -- a thoroughly Eurocentric
thinker, but one who addressed, in 'totalitarianism,' a theme with
global ramifications -- wrote of how, through 'the murder of the moral
person in man,' totalitarian systems transform their citizens into
'living corpses' capable of any outrage (2017, 591). But ironically, in
the very act of attacking essentialism as applied to 'non-Western'
cultures, the CER contributors propagate an essentialised view of 'the
West' itself. Iniquity in the form of coloniality is in their account
attributed solely to Western modernity. This view is both inaccurate and
dangerous.

The irony in this approach extends to the attribution of agency. Claims
to champion the dignity of subaltern, 'non-Western' actors are in fact
undermined by assertions of their uniform victimhood. This reproduces
the very Eurocentrism that 'decolonial' scholars quite rightly seek to
challenge. In fact, privilege and victimhood have many dimensions, by no
means all traceable to the 'phenomenon of colossal vagueness' that is
colonialism (Osterhammel 2005, 4). One group or individual can plausibly
be portrayed as victim, or perpetrator, or both, depending on context
and perspective. Were post-war German civilian refugees from Eastern
Europe, or Japanese civilians fleeing Manchuria, victims or
perpetrators? Or today, is a privately-educated, English-speaking,
upper-caste South Asian scholar more accurately to be seen as privileged
or under-privileged, in terms of access to power ('epistemic' or
otherwise) within South Asia or the global academy? 'Location' or
identity are not reducible to neat labels or discrete categories. As the
Anglo-Ghanaian philosopher Kwame Anthony Appiah emphasises, according
dignity and agency involves recognising that our identities are not just
socially given, but also actively chosen. Culture is 'a process you
join, in living a life with others,' and 'the values that European
humanists like to espouse belong as much to an African or an Asian who
takes them up with enthusiasm as to a European' (2018, 211). The same
applies with respect to value systems we have reason to regard as
iniquitous, such as those associated with colonialism or neoliberalism.

What, then, are we to make of the traction that totalising
anti-Westernism appears to be gaining within the CIE field? On one
level, this may tell us more about the state of campus politics, and
politics in general, across contemporary America and the broader
'Anglosphere', than about the wider world. The worldview that the CER
contributors espouse, even as they strain at the shackles of Western
epistemology, is redolent of America's peculiarly racialised identity
politics. And notwithstanding claims to marginal positionality, the
increasingly widespread currency of such arguments in North American and
Anglophone CIE circles reflects their status as an emergent orthodoxy
that in key respects mirrors the very ethnocentrism it rejects.

Although the ideas in the CER special issue are presented as challenging
both the scholarly mainstream and a wider neoliberal or neocolonial
establishment, the seriousness of this challenge is doubtful.
[Exhortations to]{.underline} embrace 'opacity' or [to '**think
otherwise**' in the name of '**contesting coloniality**' imply no
coherent **programme**, and suggest an **overwhelmingly** negative
agenda]{.underline}. Meanwhile, far from risking ostracism, the
contributors can expect warm endorsement of their views from regulars at
the major international conferences. For many in the CIE community in
North America and beyond, [**sweeping critiques** of Western
'**hegemony**', '**coloniality**' and so forth hold a **strong
appeal**]{.underline}; it is those seeking to question the balance or
accuracy of such theorising who risk opprobrium. As Merquior wrote of
Foucault, Derrida and their postmodernist or 'deconstructivist'
followers, their 'skepsis', 'highly placed in the core institutions of
the culture it so strives to undermine,' has come to constitute an
'official marginality' (1991, 160).

The potential -- and actual -- consequences of this are troubling.
Takayama et al call for the WCCES in particular to embrace the agenda of
'contesting coloniality,' but one conclusion to be drawn from recent
events is that this is already happening, with damaging consequences for
civility within the Comparative Education field, and for the wider
credibility of its scholarly output.15 Reducing scholarship to the
projection of the scholar's own positionality can only lead to
fragmentation and irrelevance. To quote Merquior again (paraphrasing
Hilary Putnam), 'to demote rationality, in a relativist way, to a mere
concoction of a given historical culture is as reductionist as the
logical positivist's reduction of reason to scientific calculus' (160).
What he calls the 'Elixir of Pure Negation' (159) is an intoxicating
brew, [but it is **unlikely** to inspire **coherent** or constructive
**contributions** to addressing the **pressing problems** of our age:
climate change, poverty, inequality and the ethical crisis that
underpins them all.]{.underline}

Indeed, [it is **very likely** to **do the opposite**]{.underline}. The
neoliberal cadres of the OECD or World Bank, along with [nationalist
**autocrats** from **Beijing**]{.underline} to Budapest, [will be **more
than happy** for '**critical scholars**' to fulminate against a
vaguely-defined **'West'** while embracing '**epistemological
diffidence**']{.underline} (Takayama, Sriprakash, and Connell 2017,
S18). As one critic of 'postmodernism' has put it, the promotion of
'epistemological pluralism,' combined with rejection of any 'settled
external viewpoint,' means that, ['so far as real-life **ongoing
politics** is concerned,']{.underline} postmodernists, along with
de-constructivists, [decolonialists]{.underline} and their ilk, [tend to
be 'passively **conservative in effect**']{.underline} (Butler 2002,
61). If 'decoloniality' promotes a balkanisation of the Comparative
Education field into identity-based cliques that prize 'opacity,' the
risk is that in practice this will only serve to buttress the status
quo.

##  LINK

### Scenario Planning Good\-\--2AC

#### Scenarios and causal analysis using evidence can forecast safe futures. 

**Scoblic and Tetlock 20**, \*Even Horizon Strategies, Senior Fellow,
International Security Program, New America, \*\*Professor, University
of Pennsylvania (\*Peter J. Scoblic, \*\*Philip E. Tetlock, October 13,
2020, "A Better Crystal Ball: The Right Way to Think About the Future,"
Foreign Affairs,
https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/united-states/2020-10-13/better-crystal-ball)

[**Every policy** is a **prediction**. Tax cuts will **boost** the
**economy**. Sanctions will **slow Iran's** nuclear **program**. Travel
bans will **limit** the spread of **COVID-19**. These claims all posit a
**causal relationship** between means and ends]{.underline}. Regardless
of party, ideology, or motive, no policymaker wants his or her \[their\]
recommended course of action to produce unanticipated consequences. This
makes every policymaker a forecaster. [But **forecasting** is
**difficult**, particularly when it comes to **geopolitics** --- a
domain in which the rules of **the game** are **poorly
understood**]{.underline}, information is invariably incomplete, and
expertise often confers surprisingly little advantage in predicting
future events.

These challenges present practical problems for decision-makers in the
U.S. government. On the one hand, the limits of imagination create blind
spots that policymakers tend to fill in with past experience. They often
assume that tomorrow's dangers will look like yesterday's, retaining the
same mental map even as the territory around them changes dramatically.
On the other hand, if policymakers addressed all imaginable threats, the
United States would need so large and expensive a national security
establishment that the country could do little else. By many measures,
it is nearing this point already. The United States has military bases
in more than 70 countries and territories, boasts more than four million
federal employees with security clearances, and fields 1.3 million
active-duty troops, with another million in reserve. According to one
estimate, the United States spends \$1.25 trillion annually on national
security. When it comes to anticipating the future, then, the United
States is getting the worst of both worlds. It spends untold sums of
money preparing yet still finds itself the victim of surprise ---
fundamentally ill equipped for defining events, such as the emergence of
COVID-19.

There is a better way, one that would allow [the **U**nited **S**tates
to make **decisions** based **not on** simplistic extrapolations of the
past but on smart **estimates** of the future]{.underline}. It [involves
**reconciling**]{.underline} two approaches often seen to be at
philosophical loggerheads: [**scenario planning** and probabilistic
**forecasting**]{.underline}. Each approach has a fundamentally
different assumption about the future. Scenario planners maintain that
there are so many possible futures that one can imagine them only in
terms of plausibility, not probability. By contrast, forecasters believe
it is possible to calculate the odds of possible outcomes, thereby
transforming amorphous uncertainty into quantifiable risk. Because each
method has its strengths, the optimal approach is to combine them. [This
**holistic method** would provide policymakers with both a **range** of
conceivable **futures** and regular updates as to **which** one is
**likely** to emerge]{.underline}. For once, they could make shrewd bets
about tomorrow, today.

PLANNING FOR UNCERTAINTY

Although widely used in business today, the first element of this duo
--- [**scenario planning** --- grew out of post--**World War II**
national security **concerns**, specifically the]{.underline}
overwhelming uncertainty of the [**nuclear** revolution]{.underline}.
Previously, martial experience was thought to offer some guidance
through the fog of war. Nuclear weapons, however, presented a novel
problem. With the newfound ability to destroy each other as functioning
societies in a matter of minutes or hours, the United States and the
Soviet Union faced an unprecedented situation. And unprecedented
situations are, by definition, uncertain. They lack any analogy to the
past that would allow decision-makers to calculate the odds of possible
outcomes.

Still, [**early** U.S. efforts at nuclear-**war planning** sought to
turn that problem into a **calculable one**]{.underline}. During World
War II, the Allies had great success with the new field of operations
research, [the application of statistical methods]{.underline} to
improve the outcome of tactical engagements. After the war, [the
**RAND** Corporation]{.underline} --- a "think factory" that the U.S.
Air Force established as a repository for leading researchers --- [hoped
to **parlay** this success into a new, **more rational** approach to
**war**, based]{.underline} less on the intuition of generals and more
[on the **quantification** afforded by **models** and
**data**]{.underline}.

Unfortunately, methods that worked at the tactical level proved nearly
farcical at the strategic level. As the historian David Jardini has
chronicled, RAND's first attempt to model a nuclear strategy ignored so
many key variables that it nonsensically called for deploying a fleet of
aging turboprop bombers that carried no bombs because the United States
did not have enough fissile material to arm them; the goal was simply to
overwhelm Soviet air defenses, with no regard for the lives of the
pilots. In the wake of such failures, it became clear that analysts
could not entirely banish uncertainty. In 1960, even Charles Hitch, a
man predisposed to calculation by dint of being RAND's top economist and
president of what was then the Operations Research Society of America,
cautioned, "No other characteristic of decision-making is as pervasive
as uncertainty."

[That, of course, **raised** the question of **how to** formulate
sensible **strategy**]{.underline}. Unexpectedly, it was a RAND
mathematician and physicist, Herman Kahn, who offered an answer. If the
lived past could not shape strategy, [perhaps the **imagined future**
could. Frustrated with **RAND's** attempts to **scientize war**, Kahn
devoted himself to **craft**ing scenarios]{.underline} in the pursuit of
"ersatz experience" [that would **prepare** the **U**nited **S**tates
for the **future through** what were essentially **thought
experiments**. Policymakers could **use these** scenarios]{.underline}
as "artificial 'case histories' and 'historical anecdotes,'" Kahn wrote,
[thus **making up** for a lack of actual examples or meaningful
**data**]{.underline}. They would provide analogies where there were
none.

Early methods of generating scenarios were often freewheeling and
discursive. But after scenario planning migrated to the business world,
it took on more structured forms. The most recognizable is a two-by-two
matrix in which planners identify two critical uncertainties and, taking
the extreme values of each, construct four possible future worlds.
Regardless of the specific shape they take, rigorous scenario-planning
exercises all involve identifying key uncertainties and then imagining
how different combinations could yield situations that are vastly
different from what mere extrapolation of the present would suggest. By
then "backcasting" --- taking one of these imagined futures as a given
and asking what conditions produced it --- [scenario planners **derive**
both a **story** and a **system**. They come up with a **plausible
narrative** of how a **future happened** and an internal logic that
**describes** how it **operates**. Scenarios]{.underline} are not
supposed to be predictive. They [are **meant** to be **provocative**,
challenging planners' **assumptions**, shaking up their **mental
models** of how the world works, and giving them the **cognitive
flexibility** to better sense, shape, and **adapt** to the emerging
**future**.]{.underline}

#### Knowledge and understanding are possible based on empirical observations\-\--that is the best way to debate about and understand events. 

**Walt 5**, Robert and RenÃ©e Belfer Professor of International Affairs
at HKS (Stephen Walt, 2005, "The Relationship Between Theory and Policy
in International Relations," Annual Review of Political Science, Vol. 8,
pp. 23-48)

[First]{.underline} and most obviously, [a good theory should be
**logically consistent and empirically valid**]{.underline}, because a
[logical explanation that is consistent with the available evidence
is]{.underline} more [likely to provide an accurate guide to the causal
connections that shape events]{.underline}.

[Second, a good theory is **complete**; it does not leave us wondering
about the causal relationships at work]{.underline} (Van Evera 1997).
For example, a theory stating that "national leaders go to war when the
expected utility of doing so outweighs the expected utility of all
alternative choices" (Bueno de Mesquita & Lalman 1992) may be logically
impeccable, but it does not tell us when leaders will reach this
judgment. Similarly, [a theory is unsatisfying when it identifies an
important causal factor but not the factor(s) most responsible for
determining outcomes]{.underline}. [To say that "human nature causes
war," or even that "oxygen causes war," is true in the sense that war as
we know it cannot occur in the absence of these elements]{.underline}.
[But such information does not help us understand what we want to
know]{.underline}, namely, [when is war more or less
likely?]{.underline} [Completeness also implies that the theory has no
"debilitating gaps,"]{.underline} such as an omitted variable
[that]{.underline} either [makes its predictions]{.underline}
unacceptably [imprecise or leads to biased inferences]{.underline} about
other factors (Nincic & Lepgold 2000, p. 28).

A [third]{.underline} desideratum [is **explanatory
power**]{.underline}. A theory's explanatory power [is its ability to
account for phenomena that would otherwise seem mystifying]{.underline}.
[Theories are]{.underline} especially [valuable when they illuminate a
diverse array of behavior that previously seemed unrelated and
perplexing]{.underline}, and they are most useful when they make
apparently odd or surprising events seem comprehensible (Rapaport 1972).
[In physics, it seems contrary to common sense to think that light would
be bent by gravity]{.underline}. [Yet Einstein's theory of relativity
explains why this is so]{.underline}. In economics, it might seem
counterintuitive to think that nations would be richer if they abolished
barriers to trade and did not try to hoard specie (as mercantilist
doctrines prescribed). The Smith/Ricardo theory of free trade tells us
why, but it took several centuries before the argument was widely
accepted (Irwin 1996). In international politics, it seems odd to
believe that a country would be safer if it were unable to threaten its
opponent's nuclear forces, but deterrence theory explains why mutual
vulnerability may be preferable to either side having a large capacity
to threaten the other side's forces (Wohlstetter 1957, Schelling 1960,
Glaser 1990, Jervis 1990). This is what we mean by a powerful theory:
[Once we understand it, previously unconnected or baffling phenomena
make sense]{.underline}.

Fourth, at the risk of stating the obvious, we prefer theories that
explain an important phenomenon (i.e., something that is likely to
affect the fates of many people). Individual scholars may disagree about
the relative importance of different issues, but a theory that deals
with a problem of some magnitude is likely to garner greater attention
and/or respect than a theory that successfully addresses a puzzle of
little intrinsic interest. Thus, a compelling yet flawed explanation for
great power war or genocide is likely to command a larger place in the
field than an impeccable theory that explains the musical
characteristics of national anthems.

[Fifth, a theory is]{.underline} more [useful when it is
**prescriptively rich**]{.underline}, i.e., when it yields useful
recommendations (Van Evera 1997). For this reason, George advises
scholars to "include in their research designs variables over which
policymakers have some leverage" (George 2000, p. xiv; also Glaser &
Strauss 1967, Stein 2000). Yet a theory that does not include
manipulable variables may still be useful to policy makers. For example,
a theory that explained why a given policy objective was impossible
might be very useful if it convinced a policy maker not to pursue such
an elusive goal. Similarly, [a theory that accurately forecast the risk
of war might provide a useful warning to policy makers even if the
variables in the theory were not subject to manipulation]{.underline}.

[Finally, theories are more valuable when they are **stated
clearly**]{.underline}. Ceteris paribus, [a theory that is hard to
understand is less useful simply because it takes more time for
potential users to master it.]{.underline} Although academics often like
to be obscure (because [incomprehensibility can]{.underline} both [make
scholarship seem more profound and make it harder to tell when a
particular argument is wrong]{.underline}), **[opacity
impedes]{.underline}** scientific **[progress and is not a
virtue]{.underline}** in theoreticalwork. [An obscure and impenetrable
theory is also less likely to influence]{.underline} busy [policy
makers]{.underline}.

#### Empiricism is [good]{.underline} and there's [no alternative]{.underline}\-\--the only [reasonable]{.underline} way to draw conclusions is to adopt the [best available]{.underline} theoretical explanation through [empirical adequacy]{.underline}.

**Humphreys 18**, Associate Professor and Head of Department in
Department of Politics and International Relations at the University of
Reading. (Adam R. C., 2018, Realism, empiricism and causal inquiry in
International Relations: What is at stake? European Journal of
International Relations, 25(2),
https://doi.org/10.1177/1354066118759179)

He points out, however, that while all anti-realists must reject some
part of this position, they have two options: they can either reject the
idea that scientific theories should be literally construed or reject
the idea that accepting a literally construed theory requires belief
that it is true (Van Fraassen, 1980: 10). Whereas the first option is
instrumentalist, on Van Fraassen's (1980: 10--11) view 'the apparent
statements of science really are statements, capable of being true or
false' --- if a theory's statements include 'There are electrons', then
the theory implies the existence of electrons. He therefore pursues the
second option.15 He agrees with scientific realists (against
instrumentalists) that [scientific theories should be **construed
literally**, that is, as making **existential assertions** about
**unobservable entities**]{.underline}. He insists, however, [that
construing a theory]{.underline} literally:

[relates]{.underline} not at all to our epistemic attitudes towards
theories, nor to the aim we pursue in constructing theories, but only
[to the correct understanding of what a theory says]{.underline}....
After deciding that the language of science must be literally
understood, we can still say that there is no need to believe good
theories to be true, nor to believe ipso facto that the entities they
postulate are real. (Van Fraassen, 1980: 11--12)

In other words[, scientists can **accept** a **theory** and construe it
literally while, at the same time, '"**bracketing**" its **ontological
implications**']{.underline} (Van Fraassen, 1980: 81).

[On first inspection, this may **sound unpersuasive**]{.underline}. Most
scientists, we may suppose, are entity realists --- they believe (some
of) the theories they accept to be true and hence believe that (some of)
the unobservable entities to which they refer exist. Yet, Van Fraassen
would accept this! He is [not concerned with what scientists in fact
believe, but rather with whether it is **necessary**, for science, that
scientists believe the theories they accept to be true in what they say
about the **unobservable** parts of the world]{.underline}. His view is
[that the **extent** of belief **required** for theory acceptance is
**less**]{.underline} than scientific realists suggest --- scientists
can coherently resist entity realism by [remaining '**agnostic** about
the existence of the unobservable aspects of the world **described** by
science']{.underline} (Van Fraassen, 1980: 72).16

Van Fraassen therefore presents an alternative account of theory
acceptance. [**Accepting** a theory must]{.underline}, he notes,
[involve **belief** that it is '**empirically adequate**']{.underline}
--- [that it is **true** in 'what it **says** about the observable
things and events in the world]{.underline}' (Van Fraassen, 1980: 12).
No scientist can accept a theory that they do not believe at least to
this extent. [**Accepting** a theory also **involves** 'a **commitment**
to confront any **future phenomena**' with the '**conceptual
resources**' which that theory **provides**]{.underline} (Van Fraassen,
1980: 12). This commitment compels scientists to 'immerse' themselves in
the 'world-picture' that the theory constructs and also in its language,
including language that indexes unobservable entities (Van Fraassen,
1980: 81) --- scientists will therefore respond to questions about the
world in the language of and by reference to the picture given by the
theories they accept. Van Fraassen insists, however, that [scientists
can **believe** that a theory is the **best available**]{.underline}
([out of those that are empirically adequate]{.underline}), [and **hence
commit** to it and **employ** its **language** in talking about the
world, yet still **maintain** an **open mind** about **whether** it is
**true** in what it says about the **unobservable** parts of the
world]{.underline}.17 He therefore offers the following summary
statement of constructive empiricism, which is intended to bring out the
contrast with SR:

Science aims to give us theories which are empirically adequate; and
acceptance of a theory involves as belief only that it is empirically
adequate (Van Fraassen, 1980: 12).

The difference between SR and constructive empiricism is therefore
subtle --- it concerns the extent of belief required for theory
acceptance in science. Van Fraassen argues that [**acceptance** requires
belief **only** that a theory is **empirically adequate**]{.underline}
and not, as scientific realists argue, belief that the theory is true
simpliciter. [In other words, acceptance of a theory that makes **truth
claims** about unobservable entities does not require a **commitment**
to]{.underline} entity realism --- [**agnosticism** about the
**reality** of the unobservable entities to which many scientific
theories refer is permitted]{.underline}. He also notes one important
reason for thinking that agnosticism is sufficient for science, namely,
if a theory is true in what it says about unobservable entities this
makes no difference to its empirical content, as compared to it being
[**empirically adequate** --- 'we can have **evidence** for the
**truth** of a theory only via **evidential support** for its
**empirical adequacy**']{.underline} (Van Fraassen, 1985: 255).
Consequently, he argues, belief that a theory is not only empirically
adequate, but also true in what it says about unobservables, is
'supererogatory' --- scientists may believe what theories say about
unobservables but it makes no difference for, and hence is not necessary
for, science.18 Significantly, it follows from this that there is no
theory that a realist-inclined scientist can accept that an agnostic
cannot accept. This is because a theory that is (believed to be) true
and is hence acceptable to the former is, therefore, also (believed to
be) empirically adequate and is hence equally acceptable to the latter.

[This view does **not** have the **negative methodological
consequences**]{.underline} that scientific realists attribute to
instrumentalism. Recall that both Bhaskar and Wight regard empiricism as
conservative. As Wight puts it: the 'realism principle ("is it really
the case?") provides the motor which keeps science running' --- science
progresses, in part, by developing new theories and exposing the
entities they postulate to critical scrutiny (Wight, 2007: 383; see also
Bhaskar, 2008 \[1975\]: 15--16; PatomÃ¤ki and Wight, 2000: 218). Van
Fraassen notes, however, that 'there have always been reasons to doubt
the empirical adequacy of extant theories' (Van Fraassen, 1980: 93; see
also Chernoff, 2007: 402). One reason for this is that a theory that is
claimed to be empirically adequate is claimed to be true of all the
observable parts of the world, including parts not yet observed (Van
Fraassen, 1980: 72).19 A science that aims to develop theories that are
empirically adequate will therefore not, as Wight (2007: 383) suggests,
prematurely 'come to an end', but will rather be perpetually committed
to devising new and ever-more sophisticated tests of the empirical
adequacy of its theories.

These tests may, moreover, include tests of assumptions about
unobservable entities. Making the case that causal inquiry in IR should
focus on causal mechanisms, and hence should embrace SR, Bennett (2013:
466) notes that theories involving unobservable entities:

generate implications on what should be true in the observable world if
the posited mechanisms and our instruments of observation operate in the
manner that we theorize. We can test these observable implications ...
even though we cannot directly observe mechanisms.

This could have been written by a constructive empiricist, rather than a
proponent of SR, for to test the observable implications of a theory
that postulates an unobservable entity is precisely to test its
empirical adequacy. For reasons I examine later, Van Fraassen is
reluctant to embrace the term 'causal mechanism' to characterize such
entities. Constructive [**empiricism** nonetheless **endorses**
scientists making unobservable entities objects of **inquiry** in the
**only way** that is **possible**]{.underline} --- by [**constructing**,
and **testing** the **empirical adequacy** of, theories that **invoke**
them]{.underline}.

But is covert intervention a good idea? Some analystsÂ argueÂ that it

During the 1970s, when the Third World was convulsed by ideological

the Reagan administration used a wide-ranging covert offensive to
putÂ intenseÂ pressure on Soviet clients in Afghanistan, Nicaragua and
Angola, and to drive up the costs of Moscow's global presence. Without
covert action, America might not have won the Cold War.

Unfortunately, some of [these **examples** also tell a **darker
story**]{.underline}. By aiding the Indonesian military in 1965, [the
U.S. **implicated** itself in **horrific violence**]{.underline} that
killed half a million people. The price the U.S. ultimately paid for
conspiring against Mossadegh and backing the shah of Iran was measured
in the enmity of the anti-American regime that took power in 1979. [The
U.S. supported some **decent people** fighting against Communist regimes
in the **1980s**, and some **trulyÂ awful**Â ones as well]{.underline},
including some who would go on to play an important role in a blossoming

decades.

The nature of covert intervention is that it is difficult to be choosy
in one's partners or their methods, which can create a moral mess for a
democratic superpower. A sense that almost anything would be better than
ascendant communism led the U.S. to embrace expedients --- authoritarian
regimes, efforts to kill foreign leaders such as Patrice Lumumba in the
Congo or Fidel Castro in Cuba --- that wereÂ ugly. When that happened,
covert action could become aÂ causeÂ of anti-Americanism in the Third
World rather than a solution for it.

Not least, because covert operations are high-risk endeavors, they can

Indonesia in the 1950s byÂ trying, and failing, to spur a separatist
movement against Sukarno's government. The Bay of Pigs fiasco in

overthrow U.S. allies in Latin America. It also caused Nikita Khrushchev
to deploy nuclear missiles to Cuba, leading to the most dangerous crisis
of the Cold War.

[One **tragedy** of geopolitical **rivalry** is that it often presents

#### AND the US is more likely to be decolonial and anti-imperial in its global presence.

**Deudney and Ikenberry 15**, \*John Hopkins University, \*\*Princeton
University (\*Daniel Deudney, \*\*John Ikenberry, August 2015,
"America's Impact: The End of Empire and the Globalization of the
Westphalian System,"
https://gji3.scholar.princeton.edu/sites/g/files/toruqf2666/files/gji3/files/am-impact-dd-gji-final-1-august-2015.pdf)

[In contemporary debates, this **argument** undercuts]{.underline},
modifies, and qualifies [**characterizations** held by so many of the
**U**nited **S**tates as essentially **imperial**, and the **American
order** as an **empire**]{.underline}. In our rendering, [the **U**nited
**S**tate is **not** the last Western **empire**, but the first
**anti-imperial** and post-imperial **great power** in the global
system]{.underline}. Our argument is thus focused on the consequences of
American foreign policy for the evolution of the international system,
and we do not in this confined treatment offer an explanation for the
origins of U.S. foreign policy. In short, we offer an argument about
impacts rather than the sources of America's anti-imperial and
pro-Westphalian role.

Empires and State Systems: Historical Patterns

Empire has been the historically predominant form of order in world
politics. Looking at a time frame of several millennia, there was no
global anarchic system until the European explorations and subsequent
imperial and colonial ventures connected desperate regional systems,
doing so approximately five hundred years ago.7 Prior to this emergence
of a global scope system, the pattern of world politics was
characterized by regional systems. These regional systems were initially
very anarchic, and marked by high levels of military competition. But
almost universally, they tended to consolidate into regional empires
which had fairly limited interactions with polities outside their
regions.8 Thus, it was empires -- not anarchic state systems -- that
typically dominated the regional systems in all parts of the world.

Within this global pattern of regional empires, European political order
was distinctly anomalous because it persisted so long as an anarchy.
Despite repeated efforts to consolidate Europe into one empire -- or
what the Europeans referred to as "universal monarchy" -- this region
remained a plural, multi-state political order. After the Peace of
Westphalia ending the Thirties Year War, this plural anarchic system,
the Westphalian system, and was sustained by a rough balance of power
among its autonomous states and the weakness of the claimants of
European empire. This Westphalian system was based on a roughly equal
distribution of power among its major units, sustained by various
balancing practices that thwarted a succession of regional European
empire-builders, and had an elaborate system of public international law
and ideological justification.9 While this system rested on a balance of
power, it was juridically crystallized into a system of mutually
recognized sovereigns.

Outside of Europe, however, the European states, including those that
were most active in preventing empire within Europe, were
extraordinarily successful in conquering and colonizing vast areas
across oceanic distances.10 The Europeans did not invent empire, but
they were spectacularly successful at empire building on a global scope,
largely because of the imbalance of power that stemmed from European
innovations in technology and organization.11 The Europeans conquered
and dominated empires, states, and peoples in every previously loosely
coupled or isolated regional system across the world. The Europeans also
successfully planted numerous colonies of settlers, mainly in the
temperate zones in North and South America, Oceania, and the southern
tip of Africa.12 States from the Western European core of the
Westphalian system thus brought into existence a global-scale political
system made up of vast multi-continental empires of conquered peoples
and a scattering of colonial "new Europes."13

This pattern of European empire building was different from its
predecessors, not just in its global scope, but also because the
European states were continuously warring against one another for
dominance within Europe. These struggles between states within Europe
against empire in Europe were fought on a global scale. Thus the first
"world war," defined as a war fought across multiple continents,
occurred in the later 18th century. In this struggle Britain sought to
thwart French attempts to dominate Europe and the battle lines were in
Europe, North America, South Asia, and across the global oceans. This
pattern of the globalization of intra-European warfare continued in the
20th century with the wars triggered by German efforts to dominate
Europe. The growing imbalance of power between the Europeans and the
rest of the world during the 18th and 19th century enabled the Europeans
to easily expand their empires at the expense of non-Europeans. But
during the same periods, the Europeans found it very difficult to
conquer each other within Europe. Thus vast armies wrought great
destruction fighting over tiny parcels of land in Europe, while
comparatively small European imperial expeditionary forces readily
mastered non-European armies in the Americas, Africa, and Asia. Thus a
balance of power underpinned the Westphalian system in Europe, while an
imbalance of power between Europe and the world underpinned imperial
expansion.

Anti-imperial and anti-colonial rebellions and resistance are as old as
empires, but successful rebellion against European imperial rule outside
Europe began in the 18th century with the revolt of the colonial settler
colonies in the Americas -- first in North America and then in South
America. This first wave of settler-colony rebellion marked the end of
what historians refer to as the "first British empire," as well as the
first great European empire in the Americas, that of Spain. The success
of this first wave of anti-imperial rebellion in Spanish America was
crucially facilitated by the weakening of Spain during the Napoleonic
wars for domination within Europe.

In the later-19th century, European empire building outside of Europe
entered a second wave, enabled by the new industrial technologies that
further amplified the imbalance of power between Europeans and
non-Europeans, which in turn allowed the Europeans to extend their
imperial domination into the large interior spaces of the continents,
particularly in Africa and Asia.14 In the 20th century, further wars
among the core European states weakened Britain, France, and Holland,
the leading European colonial powers, thus creating opportunities for
anti-imperial independence movements in Asia and Africa. Paradoxically,
the fact that the Europeans were continuously fighting one another
fueled their imperial ambitions and successes, while at the same time,
such wars weakened them and helped enable the success of rebellions
against their empires.15 Thus as the British empire was reaching its
territorial zenith in the early years of the 20th century, Britain was
critically weakened by the world wars in Europe and Asia against the
aspiring German and Japanese empire builders.

The territorial aggression of the Axis Powers constitute a third wave of
empire building which was short lived and thwarted by the successful
mobilization of the "United Nations," a coalition led by the United
States, Great Britain, the Soviet Union, and China. A fourth wave of
empire building, by the Soviet Union and the international communist
movement in the second half of the 20th century, was thwarted and
dismantled by the United States and its allies.

The Pattern of American Anti-Imperial, Anti-Colonial, and
Pro-Westphalian Impacts

[Against the backdrop of this evolution]{.underline} of the
international system [and the **four waves** of empire **building** and
**dismantlement**, it becomes possible to see more **clearly** the many
ways in which the **U**nited **S**tates played important
**anti-imperial**, anti-**colonial**]{.underline}, and pro-Westphalian
[roles]{.underline}. 16

[In **each** of the four **waves** of empire building and dismantlement,
the **U**nited **S**tates had an **impact**]{.underline}. The United
States was the first "new nation" to emerge from a rebellion against
European imperial rule during the first wave of modern empire. [The
**U**nited **S**tates also supported the **independence** of other
European **settler colonies**]{.underline} throughout the Americas and,
[with the **Monroe Doctrine**, helped **sustain** their **independence**
against **European** efforts to **recolonize** parts of the
**Americas**]{.underline}. In the second wave of late 19th century
empire-building, [the **U**nited **S**tates]{.underline}, despite its

**S**tates played an important **role** in thwarting a third wave of
**imperial projects** of **Germany**, Japan, and **Italy**]{.underline}.
In the second half of the 20th century, the United States played
decisive roles, both ideological and military, in thwarting the fourth
wave of empire building, the expansion of the communist great power, the
Soviet Union, as well as communist coups and revolutions in many weak
and small independent states.

The United States also played a variety of important roles in building

Taken together, [these]{.underline} varied American [activities in the
