#### 2. If it [does result]{.underline} in global presence, that engagement is [voluntary]{.underline} and [liberally beneficial]{.underline} for partner countries.

**Kane 19**, JP Conte Fellow, Immigration Studies, Hoover Institution at
Stanford University, Ph.D., Economics, University of California, San
Diego (Timothy Kane, June 17, 2019, "The United States as a Promethean
Power," Hoover Institution,
https://www.hoover.org/research/united-states-promethean-power)
\[\[Figure omitted\]\]

The global scope of American military power has been described in many
ways: [**hegemony**, primacy, and unipolarity]{.underline}. Brooks,
Ikenberry, and Wohlforth (2012)1Â cover the nuances of the terminology
well, and I agree with their preference for the term "Deep Engagement"
that Joseph Nye coined in a 1995 article.2Â The complex patterns and
nuances of engagement [remain **poorly understood**, with a focus on
**conflict** that ignores the **preponderance** of cases, where U.S.
forces have been **peacefully based** for decades]{.underline}. In
short, [foreign policy has focused on the **heat** instead of the
**light** -- countless **studies**, essays, and books on Vietnam,
Afghanistan, and **Iraq** stand in **contrast** to the negligible
**attention** given to countries where the U.S. maintained
**large-scale** and long-term **troop basing** such as Belgium,
**Korea**, Turkey, and **Kuwait**.]{.underline}

[Is the **U**nited **S**tates an **empire?** We **may** just **as well**
debate **whether** an **elephant** most closely **resembles** a
**T**yrannosaurus **Rex**]{.underline} or Triceratops. Some will point
to the imperial characteristics that are reflected in the United States
force posture.Â  But [characteristics of]{.underline} dinosaurs or
**[empires]{.underline}** (size, martial strength, breadth) really [have
**no meaning** in a world where that entire **order** is
**extinct**]{.underline}.Â  The 21stÂ century is filled with a new order
of nation-states, markedly different from eras prior to 1945.Â 

American power is best understood not by its type: hard, soft, or smart,
but its motivation. This is not to say the "imperial" motivation has
disappeared from human affairs; self-preservation and domination are
instinctive human qualities.Â  But there is a new aspect to international
relations that has been in place for more than a century, a form of
altruism, illustrated by the widespread support that exists in the U.S.
and other countries forÂ universalÂ human rights. For many decades now,
nations have routinely sought to advance something beyond their narrow
national interests. I call this a Promethean motivation, and America a
Promethean power.

The phrase is rooted in the myth of Prometheus, the rebellious god of
ancient Greek mythology who stole fire from Zeus and gave it to the
human race, sparking the beginning of technology, growth, civilization,
and prosperity. Zeus was the ruler of the Gods of Olympus, archetype of
the emperor/king. The Gods were rulers, and humans were mere subjects.
Prometheus represents rebellion, the trickster God, undercutting the
authority of the imperial order. Legend has it that Prometheus tricked
Zeus -- repeatedly -- in order to help uplift the human race.

[Critics of American **intervention** reflexively use the term
"**imperial**" when discussing **foreign affairs**]{.underline}. By far
the dominant theme of imperialism in American foreign policy has been
voiced by Leftist thinkers such as Howard Zinn, Gabriel and Joyce Kolko,
and Gore Vidal. But since 1990, the end of the Cold War,
[neo-**isolationists** on the **right** have **adopted** the term as
well]{.underline}, notably Pat Buchanan and Congressman Ron Paul. Thus
the tendency is for thinkers on the political extremes -- globalists on
the left, nationalists on the right -- to find common cause, whereas
centrists tend to view American power more favorably, as do many foreign
scholars.

Following the 9/11 attacks and concerns about state failure abroad,
proponents of American intervention began suggesting American empire in
explicit and favorable ways. Such voices included Richard Haas,
Sebastian Mallaby, and Max Boot. The fullest expression of this new
theme is found inÂ Colossus, a 2004 book by Niall Ferguson3Â (now a
colleague at the Hoover Institution), who argued "not merely that the
United States is an empire, but that it always has been an empire" and
that the ultimate threat to the nation is its own "absence of a will to
power."Â  The book is an unappreciated gem, but one wonders if Ferguson
and his intellectual opponents share the same framework, which refuses
to draw a line between imperial states of the 15th-19thÂ centuries and
the modern states of the 21st.

Ferguson wonderfully skewers the slipperiness of the termÂ hegemon, which
remains "the most popular term among writers on international
relations." Hegemony refers to a coercive state, like an empire, but one
that aims to create mutually beneficial relationships. Trying to define
the nature of state power by the distribution of benefits (exploitative
or shared) misses the point. The point is: what are first principles
that motivate foreign action?

America on the world stage should be understood in the context of its
revolutionary founding. The republic's anti-imperial birth and its sense
of manifest destiny have colored foreign affairs from early on.Â 
Consider again Thomas Jefferson's "Empire of Liberty," which was more
than a poetic phrase. In 1809 Jefferson wrote to his successor James
Madison, "I am persuaded no constitution was ever before so well
calculated as ours for extensive empire & self-government." In one of
his final letters, written in 1824, Jefferson wrote, "where this
progress will stop no-one can say. Barbarism has, in the meantime, been
receding before the steady step of amelioration; and will in time, I
trust, disappear from the earth."

To simplify matters, let's accept the framework of definitions on the
Left (and also popularly understood by Americans throughout history).
Define an empire as a nation that exploits foreign peoples, aiming to
colonize them and/or extract their resources for the advantage of the
empire and at the cost of the foreigners. And for the sake of clarity,
recognize a bright line that distinguishes imperial relationships as
those where subjugated peoples do not want but are forced to abide by
foreign intervention.Â  To be specific, [the U.S. **role** in South
**Korea** fails the **imperial test**, as does the post-1955 role of the
U.S. in **Germany** and **Japan**. Those three countries **accounted**
for nearly **three-quarters** of U.S. troop **deployments** since 1950.
Not to mention **Spain**, Turkey, **Taiwan**, and **Kuwait**. All of
these are **voluntary alliances**]{.underline}, and qualitatively
distinct from forces based in Iraq.

[For the Leftist **criticism** of American **empire** to hold, evidence
of **domination** should be **easy** to see]{.underline}. One would
expect to see the countries of the world that are occupied by American
troops suffering economic decline as a result, or at a minimum
aÂ relativeÂ decline.Â  [Here **the critique falls** apart. The countries
of the world that have **hosted** the greatest number of American
**troops** since 1950 have **grown** the **fastest** economically.
Furthermore, **host countries** have experienced faster **declines** in
child **mortality**, faster **increase** in overall **longevity**,
faster growth in **infrastructure**, and even greater **improvements**
in the broadly measured **human development** index]{.underline} (HDI).
Consider as evidence the average economic growth rates presented in
table 2, reproduced from Jones and Kane (2012).4Table 2. Annual growth
rate effect of U.S. Troops, by host country GDP per capita level in 1960
and size of troop deployment during 1950-2000.

\[\[Figure omitted\]\]

The record shows that [America's **engagement** with allied nations is
**unequivocally beneficial** for those countries]{.underline}. But the
context is what matters for future policy choices. [Public
**perceptions** of world affairs are dominated by **negatives**:
bombings in Iraqi **cities**, AIDS rampant in Africa,
**genocides**]{.underline}, earthquakes. Foreign policy experts focus on
negatives of a subtler nature: the dilemma over Iran's pursuit of
nuclear weapons, the poppy trade and corruption in Afghanistan, the
strategic mystery that is China. [Yet **despite** real and looming
**crises**, the underlying **theme** of the American century is a
**patient** march of human **prosperity**, deepening and broadening as
economic **growth unfolds** in the free world.]{.underline}

The American strategy of patient, forward deployment of U.S. troops,
even and especially when it is not self-interested, has benefitted our
allies and the world. America's engagement in Asia and Europe since 1945
created a security umbrella fostering peace and unprecedented
prosperity. If this model were applied to the Middle East -- supporting
allies rather than hunting monsters -- it would reshape the region's
future for the better. On this point, Fallows might agree.

[The **question**]{.underline} that haunts contemporary foreign policy

[Nor can US]{.underline} involvement in [foreign **wars** and

Limits to Ambition: By the Numbers

Broadly speaking, then, [the **default setting** of US foreign policy is
**hardly** one of fervent interventionism]{.underline}. In terms of
actual military posture and spending, if the United States had truly
embraced hegemonic policies, there would be a trajectory of continually
rising commitments, military spending, and interventions since 1945. Yet
the actual record is starkly different. Table 1 tells an interesting
story about one key focus of the restraint proponents---global military
presence.

[Between the late 19**80s** and roughly **2018**, US troop levels
**declined**]{.underline} slightly in Japan, more than 40 percent in
Korea, and 80 percent in Europe. [The result was that]{.underline}, as
the Pew Research Center put it, by 2016 [the "U.S. military **overseas**

after having reached a peak of **1.2 million**]{.underline} in the late
1960s and remaining at over 600,000 as recently as 1990. In 2016, only
15 percent of active-duty US military troops were deployed
overseas---the lowest proportion since 1957.21

[One partial **exception** to this trend, of course, is the **Middle
East**]{.underline}, where after a history of "extremely light force
presence" 22 before 1990, US regional deployments expanded across the
region in the wake of the Gulf War and ramped up dramatically during the
Iraq War. Various factors---including the flow of units into and out of
the region, the use of private contractors to fulfill some functions,
and limits on public information---make it impossible to put a precise
figure on US deployments; the Congressional Research Service has
estimated that as of 2019, there were 60,000 to 80,000 US troops in the
Central Command (CENTCOM) area of responsibility.23 [Yet **even here**,
these numbers are **well down** from the recent peak: the **Obama**
administration's **withdrawal** of most US combat **forces** from Iraq
meant that **numbers** there **plummeted**.]{.underline}

from over 160,000 in 2006-- 07 to residual levels by 2012.24

#### 2. Threat con is wrong:

#### A. Foreign policy is [reflexive]{.underline}, and failures spur [positive changes]{.underline} to avoid mistakes.

**Inboden et al. 20**, \*William Powers Executive Director of the

The only problem with this argument is that every component of it is
wrong. [The **foreign policy** establishment is **not a closed cabal**,
American **statecraft** has **not** been a giant
**failure**]{.underline}, and scrapping professionalism for amateurism
would be a disaster. In truth, the [foreign policy]{.underline}
establishment [is an American **strength** rather than weakness. It is
more **open-minded** and **accountable** than its critics
allow]{.underline}. It acts as a storehouse of accumulated professional
wisdom, providing intellectual ballast to the ship of state. On balance,
[the establishment's practical **track record** has been **impressive**,
with some well-known **fiascos outweighed** by many quiet **successes**.
And the current administration's foreign policy **blunders**---including
in its response to the current **pandemic**---demonstrate what happens
when the establishment's **experience** and expertise are
**rejected**]{.underline}. In short, the Blob is not the problem. It is

A CONTENTIOUS CLUB

Blob theorists view the establishment as a club of like-minded elite
insiders who control everything, take care of one another, and brush off
challenges to conventional wisdom. In reality, [the **U**nited
**S**tates actually has a **healthy market**place of foreign policy
ideas. Discussion over American foreign **policy** is loud, contentious,
**diverse**, and generally **pragmatic**---and as a result, the nation
gets the opportunity to **learn** from its **mistakes**]{.underline},
build on its successes, and improve its performance over time.

In both absolute and relative terms, [the expert community]{.underline}
dealing with foreign policy and national security [in the **U**nited
**S**tates is remarkably large and **heterogeneous**]{.underline}.
Inside government, cadres of professionals make vast amounts of
technocratic knowledge and institutional memory available to
policymakers. Every department and agency with an international role has
distinctive regional or functional expertise it can bring to bear. [This
inhouse **knowledge** is complemented by an even **larger** and more
diverse **network** of experts]{.underline} in the many hundreds of
think tanks and contract research institutions [that **surround** the
government and offer views ranging from **right** to **left**, hawk to
dove]{.underline}, free trader to protectionist, [technocratic to
ideological]{.underline}. Pick any policy issue and you can put together
a lively debate with ease. Should the United States engage with China or
contain it? Negotiate with Iran or squeeze it? Withdraw from the Middle
East or redouble its efforts? Reasoned arguments on all sides are widely
available, in any form you want---all supplied from within the
supposedly monolithic establishment.

Moreover, unlike such communities in other leading powers, the American
foreign policy establishment is connected to society rather than cut off
from it, because the top several layers of U.S. national security
bureaucracies are staffed by political appointees rather than civil
servants. The Blob comprises government officials, outside experts, and
many people who go back and forth between the two. Insiders know how
government works and what is practical. Outsiders think independently.
And in-and-outers bridge the gaps. Other countries simply do not have
comparably large, diverse, permeable, expert communities that encourage
[vigorous **debate** over national **policy**---which is why, say, the
caliber of U.S. debate about nuclear policy is **more nuanced** and
better **informed** than in other nuclear **powers**]{.underline}, and
which is why other countries would love to have such a Blob of their
own.

[The American **foreign policy** establishment]{.underline}, finally,
[is generally **more pragmatic** than ideological]{.underline}. It
values prudence and security over novelty and creativity. It knows that
thinking outside the box may be useful in testing policy assumptions,
but the box is usually there for a reason, and so reflexively embracing
the far-out option is dangerous. Its members have made many mistakes,
individually and collectively, but several features of the system
enforce accountability over time. [Foreign **policy
failures**]{.underline}, for example, [are politically **toxic** and
often **spur positive change**]{.underline}. The [monumental
intelligence **failures** that allowed the **September 11**
attacks]{.underline} to happen [were followed by policy and
**institutional reforms**]{.underline} that have helped prevent other
mass-casualty terrorist attacks on U.S. targets for almost two decades.
Early [misjudgments in the **Iraq war** led to the adoption of a **new**
counterinsurgency **strategy** that restored stability]{.underline}, at
least for a while. The international economic imbalances and financial
procedures that led to the 2008 global financial crisis were addressed
by policies that contributed to a decade-long recovery.

Taken together, these virtues reinforce one another and help the United
States tackle the countless national and global challenges that confront
a superpower. Blob critics claim there are no meaningful arguments over
U.S. foreign policy. But this is just not true. [Intense **disputes**
over the **Korea**n War, the **Vietnam** War, dÃ©tente and **arms
control**, the opening to **China**, and policies in **Central America**
and the **Middle East** were followed by **battles** over **the Gulf**
War, **NATO** expansion, military **interventions** in Haiti,
**Somalia**, and the **Balkans**, and the wars in Afghanistan and
**Iraq**---not to mention heated **arguments** over positions toward
**China**, Iran, North Korea, **Russia**]{.underline}, and other issues
today. It is true that beneath all this controversy lies a relatively
stable consensus on the value of power, alliances, and constructive
global engagement. Most members of the establishment believe that global
problems usually improve when the United States engages responsibly and
worsen when the United States retreats. Yet that reflects not some
nefarious groupthink but the wisdom of professional crowds, arrived at
through painful trial and error over more than a century.

#### B. No insular [rent-seeking]{.underline}\-\--people [are fired]{.underline} for bad predictions. 

**Ravenal 9**, Professor Emeritus, Georgetown University School of
Foreign Service (Earl C. Ravenal, 2009, "WHAT'S EMPIRE GOT TO DO WITH
IT? THE DERIVATION OF AMERICA'S FOREIGN POLICY," Critical Review, Vol.
21, No. 1, pp. 21-75, University of Kansas Libraries, Taylor and
Francis)

[The underlying notion of "the **security bureaucracies** . . . looking
for new **enemies**" is a **threadbare** concept]{.underline} that has
somehow taken hold across the political spectrum, from the radical left
(viz. Michael Klare \[1981\], who refers to a "threat bank"), to the
liberal center (viz. Robert H. Johnson \[1997\], who dismisses most
alleged "threats" as "improbable dangers"), to libertarians (viz. Ted
Galen Carpenter \[1992\], Vice President for Foreign and Defense Policy
of the Cato Institute, who wrote a book entitled A Search for Enemies).
[What is **missing** from most analysts' claims of "**threat
inflation**," however, is a **convincing theory** of why, say, the
American government significantly]{.underline} (not merely in excusable
rhetoric) [might **magnify** and even **invent threats**]{.underline}
(and, more seriously, act on such inflated threat estimates).

In a few places, Eland (2004, 185) suggests that such behavior might
stem from military or national security bureaucrats' attempts to enhance
their personal status and organizational budgets, or even from the
influence and dominance of "the military-industrial complex"; viz.:
"Maintaining the empire and retaliating for the blowback from that
empire keeps what President Eisenhower called the military-industrial
complex fat and happy." Or, in the same section:

In the nation's capital, vested interests, such as the law enforcement
bureaucracies . . . routinely take advantage of "crises" to satisfy
parochial desires. Similarly, many corporations use crises to get pet
projects--- a.k.a. pork---funded by the government. And national
security crises, because of people's fears, are especially ripe
opportunities to grab largesse. (Ibid., 182)

Thus, "bureaucratic-politics" theory, which once made several
reputations (such as those of Richard Neustadt, Morton Halperin, and
Graham Allison) in defense-intellectual circles, and spawned an entire
sub-industry within the field of international relations,5 is put into
the service of dismissing putative security threats as imaginary.

So, too, can a surprisingly cognate theory, "public choice,"6 which can
be considered the right-wing analog of the "bureaucratic-politics"
model, and is a preferred interpretation of governmental decision-making
among libertarian observers. As Eland (2004, 203) summarizes:

Public-choice theory argues \[that\] the government itself can develop
separate interests from its citizens. The government reflects the
interests of powerful pressure groups and the interests of the
bureaucracies and the bureaucrats in them. Although this problem occurs
in both foreign and domestic policy, it may be more severe in foreign
policy because citizens pay less attention to policies that affect them
less directly.

There is, in this statement of public-choice theory, a certain
ambiguity, and a certain degree of contradiction: Bureaucrats are
supposedly, at the same time, subservient to societal interest groups
and autonomous from society in general.

This journal has pioneered the argument that state autonomy is a likely
consequence of the public's ignorance of most areas of state activity
(e.g., Somin 1998; DeCanio 2000a, 2000b, 2006, 2007; Ravenal 2000a). But
state autonomy does not necessarily mean that bureaucrats substitute
their own interests for those of what could be called the "national
society" that they ostensibly serve. I have argued (Ravenal 2000a) that,
precisely because of the public-ignorance and elite-expertise factors,
and especially because the opportunities---at least for bureaucrats (a
few notable post-government lobbyist cases nonwithstanding)---for
lucrative self-dealing are stringently fewer in the defense and
diplomatic areas of government than they are in some of the
contract-dispensing and more under-the-radar-screen agencies of
government, the "public-choice" imputation of self-dealing, rather than
working toward the national interest (which, however may not be
synonymous with the interests, perceived or expressed, of citizens!) is
less likely to hold. In short, state autonomy is likely to mean, in the
derivation of foreign policy, that "state elites" are using rational
judgment, in insulation from self-promoting interest groups---about what
strategies, forces, and weapons are required for national defense.

Ironically, "public choice"---not even a species of economics, but
rather a kind of political interpretation---is not even about "public"
choice, since, like the bureaucratic-politics model, it repudiates the
very notion that bureaucrats make truly "public" choices; rather, they
are held, axiomatically, to exhibit "rent-seeking" behavior, wherein
they abuse their public positions in order to amass private gains, or at
least to build personal empires within their ostensibly official niches.
Such sub-rational models actually explain very little of what they
purport to observe. Of course, there is some truth in them, regarding
the "behavior" of some people, at some times, in some circumstances,
under some conditions of incentive and motivation. But the factors that
they posit operate mostly as constraints on the otherwise rational
optimization of objectives that, if for no other reason than the playing
out of official roles, transcends merely personal or parochial
imperatives.

My treatment of "role" differs from that of the bureaucratic-politics
theorists, whose model of the derivation of foreign policy depends
heavily, and acknowledgedly, on a narrow and specific identification of
the roleplaying of organizationally situated individuals in a partly
conflictual "pulling and hauling" process that "results in" some policy
outcome. Even here, bureaucratic-politics theorists Graham Allison and
Philip Zelikow (1999, 311) allow that "some players are not able to
articulate \[sic\] the governmental politics game because their
conception of their job does not legitimate such activity." This is a
crucial admission, and one that points--- empirically---to the need for
a broader and generic treatment of role.

Roles (all theorists state) give rise to "expectations" of performance.
[My point is that **virtually every** governmental **role**, and
especially **national-security** roles]{.underline}, and particularly
the roles of the uniformed military, [**embody** expectations
of]{.underline} devotion to the "national interest"; [**rationality** in
the derivation of policy at every functional level; and **objectivity**
in the treatment of parameters, **especially** external parameters such
as "**threats**"]{.underline} and the power and capabilities of other
nations.

Sub-rational models (such as "public choice") fail to take into account
even a partial dedication to the "national" interest (or even the
possibility that the national interest may be honestly misconceived in
more parochial terms). In contrast, an official's role connects the
individual to the (state-level) process, and moderates the (perhaps
otherwise) self-seeking impulses of the individual. [Role-derived
**behavior** tends to be **formalized** and **codified**; relatively
**transparent** and at least **peer-reviewed**, so as to be consistent
with expectations]{.underline}; surviving the particular individual and
transmitted to successors and ancillaries; [measured **against** a
standard and thus **corrigible**]{.underline}; defined in terms of the
performed function and therefore derived from the state function; and
uncorrrupt, because personal cheating and even egregious aggrandizement
are conspicuously discouraged.

My own direct observation suggests that [defense **decision-makers**
attempt to "**frame**" the structure of the **problems** that they try
to solve on the **basis** of the most **accurate
intelligence**]{.underline}. They make it their business to know where
the threats come from. [Thus, threats are not "socially
**constructed**"]{.underline} (even though, of course, some values are).

[A major reason for the **rationality**, and the **objectivity**, of the
process is that **much** security **planning** is done]{.underline}, not
in vaguely undefined circumstances that offer scope for idiosyncratic,
subjective behavior, but rather [in **structured** and **reviewed**
organizational frameworks. Non-rationalities]{.underline} (which are bad
for understanding and prediction) [tend to **get filtered out**. People
are **fired** for presenting **skewed analysis** and for making bad
predictions. This is because **something important** is riding on the
causal **analysis**]{.underline} and the contingent prediction.

For these reasons, "public choice" does not have the "feel" of reality
to many critics who have participated in the structure of defense
decision-making. In that structure, obvious, and even not-so-obvious,
["**rent-seeking**" would]{.underline} not only be shameful; it would
[present a **severe risk** of career **termination**]{.underline}. And,
as mentioned, the defense bureaucracy is hardly a productive place for
truly talented rent-seekers to operate, compared to opportunities for
personal profit in the commercial world. A bureaucrat's very
self-placement in these reaches of government testifies either to a
sincere commitment to the national interest or to a lack of sufficient
imagination to exploit opportunities for personal profit.

### AT: Link\-\--China Threat\-\--2AC

#### China is a realist, aggressive power that does not act in response to US action but in pursuit of their own international interest.

**Vaughan 21**, M.A., Defense and Strategic Studies, Missouri State
University (Alexander Vaughan, 2021, "Chinese Realism: Why Were the
Liberal Internationalists Wrong on China?" National Institute for Public
Policy, Information Series, No. 500,
https://nipp.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/IS-500.pdf) \[Acronyms\]

The Shift to Great Power Competition

[The cracks in **liberal** internationalist **hopes** widened
during]{.underline} President Barack [Obama's terms]{.underline} in
office [as the **reality** of China's unchanging **nature set
in**]{.underline}. President [Obama took office believing]{.underline}
the [**U**nited **S**tates could **cooperate** with China
on]{.underline} issues in which the President believed both nations had
a shared global responsibility, such as the 2008-09 [**econ**omic
crisis, global **warming**, and nuclear **proliferation**. However,
China posed **immediate challenges**, including increased **aggression**
in the **S**outh **C**hina **S**ea and opposition to U.S.-**So**uth
**Ko**rean military **exercises**]{.underline}.41 Exasperated at the
lack of progress with North Korean denuclearization, President Obama
warned Beijing that he "would have to take steps to shield the United
States from the threat of a nuclear missile attack from the North."42 In
2011, the Obama administration proposed its "pivot" to the Asia-Pacific
away from the Middle East.43 Nonetheless, U.S.-China engagement was
preserved along more pragmatic lines by avoiding linking issues
together, dealing with points of contention behind the scenes while
publicly trumpeting successes, such as cooperation on climate change.44

The Trump administration declared China to be a strategic competitor in
the 2017 National Security Strategy, stating, "after being dismissed as
a phenomenon of an earlier century, great power competition returned.
China and Russia began to reassert their influence regionally and
globally" and that "China seeks to displace the United States in the
Indo-Pacific region, expand the reaches of its state-driven economic
model, and reorder the region in its favor."45 The Quadrilateral
Security Dialogue ("the Quad") among the United States, Australia,
Japan, and India was revived and joint military training exercises among
Quad members are increasingly part of its agenda. Predictably, Beijing's
Cold War-era fear of American "encirclement" resurfaced.46 President
Donald Trump also bolstered U.S. support for Taiwan, including
increasing defensive arms sales and lifting restrictions on U.S.
diplomats meeting Taiwanese government officials.47 The Biden
administration is expected to continue the trend towards competition
with China. The Interim National Security Strategic Guidance states, "We
must also contend with the reality that the distribution of power across
the world is changing, creating new threats. China, in particular, has
rapidly become more assertive. It is the only competitor potentially
capable of combining its economic, diplomatic, military, and
technological power to mount a sustained challenge to a stable and open
international system" and stresses the U.S. commitment to the Quad
alliance.48 Recently, the Biden administration approved its first arms
sales to Taiwan.49

Implications for U.S. Security Policy

[Understanding China's **realist worldview** is key to crafting a
**strategy** that is consistent with the **U.S. interest** in sustaining
a **stable** western **Pacific**]{.underline}. Engagement will prove
difficult in areas where U.S. and Chinese strategic interests clash.
Taiwan is the "lens" through which Beijing views other security issues,
such as North Korean denuclearization. Indeed, according to academic
Andrew Scobell, it was the Taiwan Strait crises of the 1950s that
influenced the PRC's decision to develop nuclear weapons, despite having
been previously dismissive of them.50 Because of its geostrategic
importance, a hostile, independent Taiwan is perceived as a dagger aimed
at the PRC's heart, and Beijing has never ruled out using force to bring
Taiwan under its power. 51 A stable western Pacific depends on a stable
Taiwan Strait. Three broad policy approaches, each with their own
dangers, are:

â€¢ [**Accommodating** Beijing's national **security** aims]{.underline}
by abandoning Taiwan, which [would **allow** the PRC to **annex
Taiwan**]{.underline}, likely through coercive means. After securing
Taiwan, [an **emboldened** China will be better positioned to
**aggressively** assert illegal **territorial claims** in the **S**outh
and **E**ast **C**hina **S**eas, leading to greater **instability** in
the **Pacific**]{.underline}.52 Furthermore, [appeasing Beijing risks
undermining the **credibility** of U.S. defense commitments in the face
of **Chinese aggression**.]{.underline}

â€¢ Backing Taiwan in a bid for de jure independence, which risks war with
a China that feels it has no other recourse on the Taiwan issue.

â€¢ Sustaining the status quo whereby Taiwan is independent in all but
name, preventing Taiwan from falling under PRC control by deterring
aggression while avoiding provoking Beijing.

The risks inherent in the first two approaches make them unpalatable.
The third approach is the most viable and has been sustainable since the
passage of the Taiwan Relations Act (TRA) in 1979, requiring the United
States to supply defensive articles to Taiwan necessary for self-defense
and, although the TRA is not a formal defense commitment, requiring the
United States to sustain its own capabilities for defending Taiwan from
aggressive coercion. 53 However, [since the Third **Taiwan** Strait
**Crisis**, Beijing has undertaken to develop \[**A2AD**\]
anti-access/area denial capabilities that make **U.S. intervention** far
**riskier** than it was 25 years ago. Such capabilities include
**a**nti-**s**hip **b**allistic **m**issile**s**]{.underline} (AShBM)
such as the DF-21D and DF-26, [as well as new]{.underline} capabilities
such as the DF-ZF [**hypersonic** glide vehicle]{.underline} (HSGV). 54
[The **greater** the **risk** to U.S. forces, the less **credible** U.S.
intentions for intervening]{.underline} on Taiwan's behalf [will
**appear** to Beijing]{.underline}.

[Therefore, to **sustain** the **status quo**, the **U**nited **S**tates
will **have to** make China's use of force **costlier**]{.underline}
than tolerating a de facto-independent Taiwan. It should be clear to
Beijing that its only hope for Taiwan reunification is through peaceful,
noncoercive means whereby Taiwan itself chooses to unify with the
mainland. A formal commitment to Taiwan's security that leaves no doubt
in the minds of China's leaders will be necessary. [Steps to
**credibly** deter Chinese **aggression**]{.underline} in the Taiwan
Strait [include:]{.underline}

â€¢ [Reducing the **vulnerability** of U.S. **forces** in the Pacific,
particularly U.S. warships, to **missile** attack]{.underline} via
bolstering theatre missile defenses.

â€¢ [**Raising** the **cost** of aggression against Taiwan intolerably
high]{.underline} by extending the U.S. nuclear umbrella to Taiwan.

â€¢ [Reinforcing the **credibility** of U.S. defense
**commitments**]{.underline} to Taiwan by making the National Missile
Defense system capable of defending the U.S. homeland from nearpeer
missile strikes.

â€¢ Investing in the development of [new **missile**]{.underline} defense
**[technologies]{.underline}**, such as directed energy weapons and
space-based interceptors, [to counter **new missile**
threats]{.underline} (e.g., the DF-ZF HSGV).

The final two steps will require law and policymakers to have the
political will for protecting U.S. interests in the Taiwan Strait.
[Critics may **object** that these two **steps** risk starting a
so-called "**action-reaction** cycle" but]{.underline}, [**even with**
the currently limited U.S.]{.underline} missile defense [capabilities,
the **Chinese** have developed **operational** advanced **missile
systems**]{.underline}, much as the Soviet ballistic missile stockpile
continued to grow despite inhibitions regarding U.S. missile defense
development during the Cold War.55 [Artificially preserving the
**vulnerability** of the **U**nited **S**tates and its **forces** to
missile attacks will **not convince** Beijing to **forgo** valuable
"assassin's maces" such as **a**nti-**s**hip **b**allistic
**m**issile**s**]{.underline} (AShBMs) [and **HSGVs**. The PRC is
**determined** that the Fourth **Taiwan** Strait Crisis will **not be a
repeat** of the Third.]{.underline} Additionally, [defending the
**homeland** from near-peer **missile threats** has become all the
**more relevant** with the]{.underline} recent public [revelations
regarding the **construction** of multiple new Chinese missile **silo
sites**]{.underline}, reportedly intended [to house]{.underline} DF-41
intercontinental ballistic missiles (**[ICBMs]{.underline}**) equipped
with multiple, independently targetable reentry vehicles (MIRVs),
[which]{.underline} United States Strategic Command (USSTRATCOM) head
Admiral Charles Richard [has called a Chinese "**strategic
breakout**."]{.underline}56

Conclusion

[U.S. cooperative **engagement** with the **PRC** has **not** made the
**interests** of the **U**nited **S**tates more **secure** -- indeed,
the American "**holiday from realism**"]{.underline} (as Mearsheimer
puts it) [has made the **Pacific** more **dangerous**. Rather than
**vainly hoping** China will politically **change** in ways that
**suit** the **U**nited **S**tates, Washington should **assert** its

defense]{.underline} commitment to Taiwan\'s security [will **promote**

**boost** the confidence of **U.S. allies**]{.underline}, such as Japan
-- who, recognizing Taiwan's pivotal role in regional stability, has
recently become more outspoken in defense of Taiwan's security.57
Finally, a strong defense commitment to Taiwan, a peaceful and
prosperous democracy, is also an area where realist self-interest
happily coincides with American values.

## IMPACT

### Utilitarianism Good\-\--2AC

#### Consequentialism first.

**Greene 7**, Professor, Harvard University. (Joshua D., 2007, "The
Secret Joke of Kant's Soul," in *Moral Psychology Volume 3*)

What turn-of-the-millennium science is telling us is that human moral
judgment is not a pristine rational enterprise---that our [**moral
judgments** are driven by a **hodgepodge** of **emotional
dispositions**, which]{.underline} themselves [were shaped by a
**hodgepodge** of **evolutionary forces**]{.underline}, both biological
and cultural. Because of this, [it is **exceedingly unlikely** that
there is any **rationally coherent normative** moral theory that can
**accommodate**]{.underline} our **[moral intuitions]{.underline}**.
Moreover, anyone who claims to have such a theory, or even part of one,
almost certainly does not. Instead, what that person probably has is a
moral rationalization.

It seems then that we have somehow crossed the infamous "is" "ought"
divide.14 How did this happen? Didn't Hume (1978) and Moore (1903) warn
us against trying to derive an "ought" from an "is?" How did we go from
descriptive scientific theories concerning moral psychology to
skepticism about a whole class of normative moral theories? The answer
is that we did not, as Hume and Moore anticipated, attempt to derive an
"ought" from an "is." That is, our method has been inductive rather than
deductive. We have inferred on the basis of the available evidence that
the phenomenon of rationalist deontological philosophy is best explained
as a rationalization of evolved emotional intuition (Harman, 1977).

Missing the Deontological Point

I suspect that rationalist [deontologists will remain
**unmoved**]{.underline} by the arguments presented here. Instead, I
suspect, [they will insist that]{.underline} I [have simply
**misunderstood** what **Kant** and like-minded deontologists are all
about. Deontology]{.underline}, they [will say]{.underline}, isn't about
this intuition or that intuition. It's not defined by its normative
differences with consequentialism. Rather, deontology is about taking
humanity seriously. Above all else, [it's about **respect** for
**persons**]{.underline}. It's about treating others as fellow rational
creatures rather than as mere objects, about acting for reasons that
rational beings can share; and so on (Korsgaard, 1996a, 1996b).

This is, no doubt, how many deontologists see deontology. However, this
insider's view, as I have suggested, may be misleading. [The
problem]{.underline}, more specifically, [is that it **defines**
deontology in terms of **values** that are not **distinctively
deontological**]{.underline}, though they may appear to be from the
inside. Consider the following analogy with religion. When one asks a
religious person to explain the essence of his religion, one often gets
an answer like this: "It's about love, really. It's about looking out
for other people, looking beyond oneself. It's about community, being
part of something larger than oneself." This sort of answer accurately
captures the phenomenology of many people's religion, but it is
nevertheless inadequate for distinguishing religion from other things.
This is because many, if not most, nonreligious people aspire to love
deeply, look out for other people, avoid self-absorption, have a sense
of a community, and be connected to things larger than themselves. In
other words, secular humanists and atheists can assent to most of what
many religious people think religion is all about. From a secular
humanist's point of view, in contrast, what is distinctive about
religion is its commitment to the existence of supernatural entities as
well as formal religious institutions and doctrines. And they are right.
These things really do distinguish religious from nonreligious
practices, although they may appear to be secondary to many people
operating from within a religious point of view.

In the same way, I believe that most of the standard
[deontological/**Kantian** self-characterizations **fail** to
**distinguish** deontology from **other approaches** to
ethics]{.underline}. (See also Kagan, 1997, pp. 70--78, on the
difficulty of defining deontology.) It seems to me that
[consequentialists, **as much** as **anyone else**, have **respect** for
persons, are against **treating** people as mere **objects**, wish to
act for reasons that **rational creatures** can **share**, etc. A
consequentialist respects other persons and **refrains** from treating
them as **mere objects** by **counting every** person's
**well-being**]{.underline} in the decision-making process. Likewise, [a
consequentialist attempts to act according to reasons that **rational**
creatures can **share** by acting according to **principles** that give
**equal weight** to **everyone's** interests]{.underline}, i.e., [that
are **impartial**]{.underline}. This is not to say that
consequentialists and deontologists do not differ. They do. It's just
that the real differences may not be what deontologists often take them
to be.

What, then, distinguishes deontology from other kinds of moral thought?
A good strategy for answering this question is to start with concrete
disagreements between deontologists and others (such as
consequentialists) and then work backward in search of deeper
principles. This is what I have attempted to do with the trolley and
footbridge cases and other instances in which deontologists and
consequentialists disagree. [If you ask a **deontologically minded**
person why it is **wrong** to **push someone** in front of **speeding
trolley** in order to save **five others**, you will get
**characteristically deontological** answers. Some will be
**tautological**: "Because it's **murder**!"]{.underline} Others will be
more sophisticated: ["The **ends don't justify** the **means**." "You
have to **respect people's rights**."]{.underline} As we know, [these
answers **don't** really **explain anything**, because if you give the
**same people**]{.underline} (on different occasions) [the **trolley
case**]{.underline} or the loop case (see earlier discussion), [they
will make the **opposite judgment**, even though their **initial
explanation**]{.underline} concerning the footbridge case [**applies
equally** well to]{.underline} one or [**both** of these
cases]{.underline}. Talk about rights, respect for persons, and reasons
we can share are natural attempts to explain, in "cognitive" terms, what
we feel when we find ourselves having emotionally driven intuitions that
are at odds with the cold calculus of consequentialism. Although these
explanations are inevitably incomplete, there seems to be "something
deeply right" about them because they give voice to powerful moral
emotions. However, as with many religious people's accounts of what is
essential to religion, they don't really explain what is distinctive
about the philosophy in question.

#### Policies must consider [consequences]{.underline}\-\--intent and moral imperatives are [incoherent]{.underline} AND risk [further violence]{.underline}.

**Hirschel-Burns 16**, Ph.D. in comparative politics and international
relations from Yale University, and current Postdoctoral Research
Associate in Princeton's Program in Latin American Studies. (Danny,
4-19-2016, "In Defense of Consequentialism: A Response to Shadi Hamil,"
The Widening Lens,
https://thewideninglens.wordpress.com/2016/04/19/in-defense-of-consequentialism-a-response-to-shadi-hamid/)

My difference of opinion is fundamental: I believe most US foreign
policy to be short-sighted, and consequentialism, or the weighing of
long-term ramifications against the initial intended effect of a
particularly intervention to represent the ideal method of policymaking.
[Policies cannot **solely** be **judged** on **intention**, due to the
**frequency** with which good intentions **produce negative outcomes**,
nor can they be judged **solely** on **initial effects** due to the
**long-running causal chains** produced by **order-altering**
things]{.underline} like military interventions. However, Hamid is right
that it is impossible to foresee some ramifications (even if we can see
general correlations) of foreign policy, but he doesn't apply that
standard of doubt consistently across his analysis.

Early in the essay, Hamid makes the point that to evaluate the Libyan
intervention, it is necessary to compare the current situation with the
counterfactual: what would Libya look like if the US hadn't intervened.
In general, the assertion is correct, but the practice of
counterfactuals is tricky. Hamid's analysis of where the Libyan conflict
was at when the US intervened is enlightening, but his conclusion that
Libya would likely look like Syria today had the US not intervened is
highly questionable. Political prediction, especially on rare events
like mass atrocities or civil wars, is really, really hard. And when you
consider all the differences between Libya and Syria (total population,
population density, salience of sectarian divides, regime configuration,
military capability of opposition, etc.) along with all contingencies
that could have occurred in the past four years, it is impossible to say
with any certainty that Libya would bear a resemblance to Syria. Syria
is merely a convenient standard of comparison because it's an ongoing
civil war in the Middle East, but saying Libya would be Syria doesn't
actually tell us that much about Libya or the effects of intervention.
It's not that the intervention can't be justified with counterfactuals,
but they need to be more carefully constructed.

The central thrust of Hamid's essay is to deride what he calls
consequentialism, or evaluating the efficacy of foreign policy based on
events years after the initial intervention in the target location. For
Hamid, such an approach is particularly problematic because it a policy
cannot be retroactively deemed a mistake if the limited goal of the
intervention is achieved initially. Therefore consequentialism creates
an impossibly high bar for foreign policy decisions: unless a foreign
policy results in a peaceful, liberal democracy, than it's a failure.
This is, however, a major straw man. Certainly there are some critics
that would deem the Libyan intervention a failure based on this
standard, but Hamid lumps in those with reasonable concerns that a civil
war (likely to continue for many years based on what we know about civil
wars and foreign intervention) at least partially produced by the NATO
intervention will have more negative long-term effects on Libyans than
Gaddafi's intended repression. [**Worrying** about **consequences** does
not **preclude** making **fo**reign **po**licy decisions.
**Recognizing** that every decision has potential **positive** and
**negative effects** is **no more** than an **accurate framework** for
**analyzing** policy]{.underline}.

There are an additional two problems with Hamid's argument here. First,
[the **dismissal** of **consequentialism** is one of the **central
dynamics** that **leads** Western policymakers to **struggle** with
**conflict prevention**. Short-term thinking produces short-term
solutions. Policymakers become **trapped** in a **vicious circle** of
**continual crises** that **overwhelm** them and prevent longer-term
thinking that could go a **long way** in **preventing
violence**]{.underline}.Â Second, Hamid's insistence that the initial
moral righteousness of an intervention negates any negative effects, is
deeply problematic. As many before me have argued, [focusing only on
**moral imperatives** disincentives **careful planning** and allows
policymakers to **wash** their **hands** of **responsibility** if the
situation starts to go **south**]{.underline}. Evaluating military
interventions isn't personal morality, because very rarely can doing the
right thing in your personal life lead to deaths of thousands of people.
Afghanistan is a valid example. The United States was going after the
Taliban in response to 9/11 initially, but the war has had disastrous
long-term effects for the country. It would take quite a bit of chutzpah
to declare it a success.

[Moral arguments **without** strategic and humanitarian]{.underline}
(writ large) [considerations are]{.underline} also [**prone** to abuse,
because **liberal** interventionists and **neoconservatives** aren't
actually that **far apart**]{.underline}: both believe in the wisdom of
Western democracies to improve the world through military force. Without
more consequentialist standards, there's not a clear line the prevents
Iraq-like decisions. SoÂ Hamid's own argumentÂ that Obama being right
about Iraq decreases his likelihood he'll be right about other
situations is undermined by a lack of a standard that allows leaders to
tell the difference between the two.

#### Their geographies studies alt fails:

#### 1. Research doesn't influence reality. 

**De Leeuw and Hunt 18**, professor @ University of Northern British

"unsettling decolonizing geographies," Geography Compass,
https://doi.org/10.1111/gec3.12376,)

Importantly, [geographers are]{.underline} also
**[decolonizing]{.underline}** the [discipline through]{.underline} the
creation of [collaborative writing and organizing practices in which
Indigenous and settler scholars work side by side in disrupting
normative power dynamics in the very spaces in which geography
reproduces itself]{.underline}. This work has included developing
decolonizing frameworks in organizing conferences which were previously
asserting criticality (centering feminist, queer, anti-capitalist,
critical race approaches) absent a significant consideration of settler
colonialism ("Decolonizing Cascadia? Rethinking Critical Geographies"
conference organizing committee, 2014). [Further, collaborative dialogue
and writing among Indigenous and non-Indigenous scholars is a growing
practice in which dynamics of colonialism, decolonization, and
Indigenous resurgence can be thought through in order to make visible
some of the tensions in decolonizing work on the ground and in everyday
life]{.underline} (Holmes, Hunt, & Piedalue, 2015; Hunt & Holmes, 2015;
de Leeuw, Cameron, & Greenwood, 2012.) While some of this work is
difficult to find in standard search engines due to its circulation
among more localized networks---a purposeful effort to produce research
outcomes for the communities they are about---other publications have
been written for scholarly audiences. As discussed by Coombes, Johnson,
and Howitt (2014), unsettling community-based research and publication
with Indigenous communities requires "a new relational ethics which
unsettles any remaining binaries that survived the qualitative
revolution in human geography: Ethics becomes method; data becomes life;
landscape becomes author\' participants become family" (p. 850).
Geographers have reflected on their own relational processes for truly
shifting power of authorial voice within collaborative research (McLean,
Howitt, Colyer, Raven, & Woodward, 2016; Woodward & Marrfurra McTaggart,
2016); for example, working in the settler colonial context of
Australia, geographers Sarah Wright, Kate Lloyd, and Sandie
Suchet-Pearson have worked to center the agency of Indigenous peoples
and places by making the place itself, Bawaka Country, the lead author
in several research publications (see Bawaka Country et al., 2013, 2014,
2016). While this work is by no means complete, nor without potential
for scrutiny in the future, it does signal concrete practices meant to
disrupt normative colonial ways of thinking and being.

[Geographers **theorizing**]{.underline} and enacting what they name as
**[decolonization]{.underline}**---[especially non-Indigenous settler

**[series of ideals]{.underline}** [that they gesture
toward]{.underline}. [Such gestures]{.underline}, however,
**[are]{.underline}** often **[still decoupled from]{.underline}**
actual **[Indigenous peoples]{.underline}**, voices, [and]{.underline}
**[places]{.underline}**: [While there may be a **stated
allegiance**]{.underline} with Indigenous communities and scholars
[sometimes things as simple as locating oneself in an active colonial
context, citing a significant number of Indigenous scholars, or
including Indigenous voices, is lacking]{.underline}. **[Non-Indigenous
geographers]{.underline}** working on decolonizing geographies
**[idealize]{.underline}** a kind of **[hybrid
scholar-activism]{.underline}** that privileges on-the-ground
anticolonial work focused on unsettling the supremacy of settler states:
Here, geographers might be participating in rallies or anticolonial

support of land defense (McCreary & Milligan, 2014; Pasternak, 2014),
working with Indigenous youth organizations or even working with other
settlers toward "culturally safe" interactions (Matthews, 1998). Still,
[it is **important** to consider the **limits** of these

**[Decolonization]{.underline}** [as it is increasingly being

#### 2. Critical geographies are institutionally ineffective.

**Kinkaid 19**, post-doctoral scholar in human geographies @ university
of Delaware. (Eden, 2019, "At the limits of critical geography: Creative
interventions into the exclusionary spaces of U.S. geography," Gender,
Place & Culture: A Journal of Feminist Geography, Routledge, Taylor &
Francis Group)

Having introduced select pieces from the CFVA, I will now discuss some
of the institutional difficulties encountered in advocating for their
inclusion. I will focus on dialogues surrounding two works in particular
that arose during the planning of the exhibit. Upon critical analysis,
it seems that these works were seen as potentially controversial by
virtue of the fact that they worked to disrupt institutional space,
identities, and norms, a concern that was rather ironic given the nature
of the conference. [While]{.underline} intellectual
'**[disruption]{.underline}**,' especially 'spatial disruption,' is an
effect we might expect [of]{.underline} **[critical-creative geographic
interventions]{.underline}** -- [if not the entire purpose of engaging
these approaches]{.underline} -- [it became clear that **some forms of
disruption were** seen as **acceptable** while **others** were less
'**legitimate**.']{.underline}

This potentially disruptive character of some works produced tensions in
vetting and planning the exhibit. At the time of planning, there were no
agreed upon criteria for judging artworks; it was mostly left to the
curator (the author) to organize the exhibit. As the specter of
controversial content arose, conversations occurred between the curator
and a key organizer, as well as with a departmental administrator. The
larger committee (named 'the critical geography collective') composed of
faculty and graduate students was only brought in on these conversations
toward the end of the process, specifically to make sure everyone was
'on board' for a drag performance and to approve a list and descriptions
of the works. [Thus, many of these **issues** and **decisions** were
**engaged** **outside** of the '**collective**,' **closing down**
opportunities for more sustained and **democratic** forms of **debate**
and decision-making.]{.underline}

Envisioning 'critical geographies'

At the outset of this discussion, it is worth considering how **[the
self-perception of critical geographers as 'critical']{.underline}**
[can ironically operate as an obstacle to identifying the inflection of
racism]{.underline}, sexism, homophobia, [and]{.underline} **[other
forms of oppression and exclusion]{.underline}** in this institutional
space. Ahmed aptly remarks:

> [I suspect that criticality -- the self-perception that in being
> critical we do not have a problem or **that in being critical we are
> over it** -- is often used and performed in these academic spaces. I
> have called critical racism and critical sexism this: the racism and
> sexism reproduced by those who think of themselves as **too critical
> to reproduce racism and sexism**]{.underline}. (2017, 155)

These contradictions do not go unnoticed, at least by those of us who
experience the personal and professional impacts of such lapses in
criticality. Indeed, '\[t\]his 'not translation' is something we
experience: it is a gap between a symbolic commitment and a lived
reality' (2017, 90). It is in these moments of 'not translation' that
the lines delineating what we do and do not do as critical geographers,
who we are and are not, what we challenge and what we accept, are drawn,
articulated, and reinforced. [At the most benign, I argue, when we fail
to confront these moments critically, we fail to provide models to
others of scholarship that challenges and addresses exclusionary norms,
and we fail to educate each other]{.underline}. At worst, when we do not
intervene, [we send messages to students and colleagues about what kinds
of **knowledge**]{.underline}, **[meanings]{.underline}**,
[and]{.underline} **[experience]{.underline}** [will be recognized in
the discipline, and consequently, what **kinds of bodies** and
**identities** will be recognized.]{.underline}

This is to say that seemingly minor decisions, made in the heat of the
moment or without much thought, reinforce norms that constitute the
institutional and intellectual space of the discipline. For some, these
moments are experienced as anything but minor: the confusion and
internal conflict they spawn produces a sense of bodily and intellectual
alienation that becomes a constitutive, almost ontological condition of
one's experience of institutional space as a minority subject (Puwar
2004). Yet these difficult moments might also serve as a critical
intellectual resource: '\[w\]e might work over, mull over, these
experiences; we might keep coming back to them because they do not make
sense' (Ahmed 2017, 21). [While these ideas may seem abstract or removed
from everyday institutional life, in the following analysis, I aim to
demonstrate how they are actually very concrete and how 'minor'
incidents accumulate to reproduce structures and spaces of
inequality]{.underline}.

A critical reading of the production of the exhibit illuminates how
**[particular understandings]{.underline}** [of 'critical geography'
shaped the institutional management of the exhibit, while **reinforcing
normative subject positions and marginalizing others**]{.underline}.
Here, the erratic and at times [contradictory discursive mobilizations
of the meanings of 'critical geographies', intellectual 'legitimacy',
'institutional optics', 'political climate', nonconsensual or forceful
intellectual confrontation, 'blowback', and 'family friendliness' in
relation to apparently worrisome interventions into institutional space
and disciplinary identity prove instructive for evaluating the
potentials and limitations of critical-creative geographic
scholarship.]{.underline}

Political climate' and the threat of 'blowback'

The various **[departmental reactions]{.underline}** to the prospect of
displaying artistic works in the hallways [speak to the recognized
ability of artworks to disrupt and challenge all kinds of
norms]{.underline}. In fact, [much of the discussion surrounding the
exhibit focused on]{.underline} **[managing controversies]{.underline}**
that it might produce. [One of the first questions I was asked by an
administrator (who was not otherwise involved in organizing the
conference) was whether or not the artwork would be suitable for public
consumption.]{.underline} When I asked for clarification, the question
was rephrased as '**[is it going to be family friendly?']{.underline}**
As it happened, one of the 'potentially controversial' pieces to be
discussed was a drag performance. As a queer-identified person
inhabiting a heteronormative institution, I was incapable of answering
this question: were drag queens family friendly? Are non-normatively
gendered people family friendly?

[This vague signaling of controversy accomplished through the phrase
'family friendly' can be critically read as an attempt to draw on
commonsense norms and expectations about what (and who) does and does
not belong in this institutional space]{.underline}. Even before
specific works were discussed, certain terms -- 'family friendly,'
'political climate,' and 'blowback' -- were commonsensically deployed
and set the tone of the larger conversation. For example, as a preface
to a discussion of the exhibit, the administrator argued that 'in this
political climate' we had to be careful about displaying artwork in the
hallways. [These terms were never defined or described, they merely
functioned to heighten the sense of **risk and potential controversy
that attended the prospect of engaging artwork**]{.underline}.

While most of the works in the exhibit were anything but controversial,
there were a few submissions that I felt the need to discuss in advance,
given the general sense of concern voiced by my seniors. The first such
contribution was the drag performance, which I will detail at length in
a moment. The second work was a sculpture entitled 'Redbone,' submitted
by Ciara Newton, the artist who produced Slave Money. The sculpture was
not graphic or offensive in any way: it was a large, red sculpture of a
bone. However, given the title, which could be read as 'racially
charged' (though likely would be entirely overlooked by white people), I
felt the need to contextualize the work. The sculpture was a commentary
on the artist's racial identity as a person of white, black, and
indigenous heritage. In explaining the work to me, she described an
experience as a young girl walking in a parking lot in her Midwestern
hometown and having 'redbone' shouted at her. The work, then, was a
critical commentary and redeployment. I thus explained to the
administrator that 'redbone' could function as a slur if shouted at a
person in a parking lot, but that here it was not functioning as such. I
provided the analogy of queer: if someone shouts 'queer' at a person in
a parking lot, it functions as an epithet, if a person self-describes as
queer, its meaning is different. As such, it is nonsensical and
problematic (as a group of white people) to police the use of the term
in this case. [This discussion was met with discomfort, and it was
ultimately suggested that this piece be displayed apart from the other
works in a graduate lounge, with the implicit assumption that this would
prevent people from seeing it and being offended by it.]{.underline}

Here, we can see how certain **[institutional code words function as
performatives]{.underline}**: [they serve to produce (and police) the
reality they claim to merely name]{.underline} (Butler 1990). Seemingly
innocent questions about 'family friendliness' and 'political climate'
are in fact loaded with meanings and implications; as Ahmed remarks,
'\[t\]hese questions only appear to be questions; they often work as
assertions' (2017, 117). When an unspecified 'political climate' was
commonsensically deployed to interrogate and police potentially
controversial content, 'political climate,' along with the inevitability
of 'blowback,' became a performative code word that naturalized our
individual and collective decisions about particular works. [These terms
function effectively as political tools because they are sufficiently
vague that they cannot be fully interrogated -- they assume a
common-sense meaning -- yet at the same time, they function in very
specific ways to produce consensus about institutional boundaries and
possibilities.]{.underline}

Legitimate geographies and illegitimate geographers

These concerns about 'blowback' 'political climate' and 'family
friendliness' surrounded the very idea of the exhibit, before its
specificities were even considered. However, these more diffuse concerns
about the disruptive potential of artworks particularly condensed around
one work: a proposed drag performance, which, after various discussions
and negotiations, ultimately took the form of The Future Looks Queer.
This contribution, partly by virtue of its genre and largely by virtue
of its content (or imagined content), raised particular concerns, ones
which, from the perspective of a queer person, allegorize the
heteronormative tendencies of the department's institutional and social
culture. [Accordingly, it is worth attending at length to the particular
discourses mobilized in the 'approval' of this piece.]{.underline}

[The first concern to emerge was that a drag performance could
'**delegitimize critical geography**.]{.underline}' If a drag
performance was part of the conference, [people might not take critical
geography seriously]{.underline}. In particular, [if members of the
department, who do not know much about critical geography or already
hold it in disrepute, were to encounter a drag performance, this could
further delegitimize the subfield in their eyes and make it difficult to
**advocate** for **institutional support** at the **departmental
level**]{.underline}. [It was argued that these '**institutional
optics'** did not bode well for the future of critical **geographies in
the department**.]{.underline}

The concern that queer submissions would delegitimize the discipline
were difficult to stomach, or even understand, as a queer person
studying queer geographies and cultural forms. Drag has long been a site
of analysis for scholarly work and, with the institutionalization of
geographies of sexuality and queer theory, has long ceased to be a
controversial site for academic interest. This is not to argue that such
a performance would be without potential 'blowback' or discomfort, but
that, as critical geographers, we ought to be willing to take such risks
and defend such interventions on the grounds that we are committed to
working against these exclusionary norms of institutional space.
[Regardless, to be implicitly asked to reconsider the necessity of
including a queer performance to protect the future prospects of the
subfield -- a subfield in which, by logical extension, my identity and
work are marginalized -- put me, as the curator, in an uncomfortable
position.]{.underline}

Another concern raised during discussions on the 'drag queen issue,'
revolved around the content of the performance. Indeed, concerns about
'blowback' dominated the conversation before the details of the
performance were even established or discussed. The prospect of having a
drag performance in the conference space was likened to unspecified
incidents (i.e., sex scandals?) at other universities that resulted in
the resignation of a provost and several faculty members. This reaction
was presumably in response to an expectation that the performance would
involve sexual content, though this was never directly stated. At this
point, the exact content of the performance had not been determined, as
the artist had not come to the building to explore potential performance
spaces, and any such considerations were suspended until they could be
assured that the performance would be permitted and that the exhibit
would be a safe space. Accordingly, I could not specify exactly what the
performance would entail. This lack of information did not help in
discussions about potentially controversial content, yet, even with
assurances that there would be absolutely no sexual content, discomfort
and resistance remained.

As a queer person, I can only imagine that my assurances that there
would be no inappropriate (i.e. sexual) content were ineffective because
it seemed that, for the person with this concern (a heterosexual male),
queer men in drag seemed to already necessarily express a sexual
content. Indeed, homophobia and transphobia are often sustained 'by
reading the expression of gender identity as itself a sexual act'
(Salamon 2018, 5). Here, following Ahmed, we can understand gender 'in
concrete forms of accommodation' (Ahmed 2017, 122). If we understand
that space is produced as heterosexual through particular practices
(Bell et al. 1994; Bell and Valentine 2003), the failure to accommodate
persons of non-normative genders and sexualities necessarily upholds and
reaffirms the heteronormativity of that space. [Thus, in order to
protect and accommodate problematic aspects of heterosexual, white male
gender identity, we must un-accommodate the marginalized identities that
might cause them minor discomfort or self-reflection]{.underline}. Too
often, **[the inclusion of 'diversity' in institutional spaces is meted
in this way]{.underline}**: [it is tolerated to the extent that it does
not call into question dominant identities, resulting in the
marginalization and 'affective inequality' (Rodo-De-Z arate 2017) of
bodies that disrupt (hetero)normative space.]{.underline}

Intellectual 'confrontation' and externalization

[What is most interesting (and deeply ironic) about the management of
the 'potentially controversial' art is the manner in which **space**,
**boundaries**, and even **maps**, were the **technologies** used to
**police** these works at a **critical geography
conference**]{.underline}. Space became a central issue in managing the
threat of the drag performance. The original proposal for the submission
involved several performers stationed in the elevators, who would engage
visitors, put on makeup, and improvise as visitors travelled from the
first to third floor for refreshments and to visit the exhibit. This
plan was eventually rejected because it was a violation of safety codes.
However, before that judgment was reached, various other concerns
emerged that speak directly to the management of institutional space and
intellectual boundaries.

[A major issue identified in the elevator performance scenario was that
the elevators did not belong to the department; they were used by people
on other floors of the building and we could not disrupt their
functioning]{.underline}. While this is surely reasonable, this concern
about physical safety and functionality blended easily with reoccurring
concerns about the 'content' of the performance and what would happen if
an 'inappropriately gendered' person were detected in the building. For
example, [it was argued that a student might come to the building
Saturday morning and use the elevator to get to the fifth floor to work
on their math homework. It was deemed that this hypothetical student did
not 'sign up' to encounter a drag queen, and while this moment of
unexpected encounter might be the very point of the performance, it was
seen as crossing a boundary]{.underline}. In the same discussion, one
organizer added that he had some information that there was a
'conservative' meteorologist on another floor, and if he encountered the
performance, there would certainly be 'blowback.' Here, a vague
reference to an unspecified person's conservatism (by which we mean
homophobia) was sufficient to cause us to second-guess what was
appropriate at the conference. Here, it is clear how '\[i\]nnuendo,
gossip, \[and\] hearsay... become indispensable forms of knowledge' in
the act of censorship (Tyburczy 2015, 59).

Ultimately, [the potential 'space invasion' posed by both the drag
performance and 'Redbone' were **managed with spatial
tactics**]{.underline}. For safety as well as political reasons, the
drag performers could not use the elevators. [It was resolved that they
could make use of various spaces on the third floor (one of the floors
belonging to the Dept. of Geography), including meeting rooms and other
miscellaneous spaces. It was also suggested that the time and location
of this performance would be clearly marked on a map of the conference
space, presumably so that one could make an informed choice about
attending it or not]{.underline}. This way, one would not unwillingly
encounter a nonnormatively gendered performance artist when they had not
explicitly 'signed up' to do so by attending a critical geography
conference. In the same discussion, it was suggested that the other
controversial piece, 'Redbone,' be sequestered in the graduate student
lounge, while the rest of the works were displayed prominently in the
hallway and that it also be placed on a map, so that people interested
in viewing it could find it (it was later excluded due to code issues).
Here, the map emerged as a key technology in including some works
differently. [Given that the rest of the artworks were all contained in
one stretch of hallway]{.underline} (making a map unnecessary), [we can
wonder if the map was meant to orient visitors to the works and to
render them more visible, or if it was a strategy proposed to hide works
that made political statements, particularly in light of the fact that
the only two works subjected to this treatment were ones concerned
explicitly with gender, sexuality, and race]{.underline}.

These spatial tactics were deemed necessary to avoid offending people,
be they participants in the conference or people who somehow happened to
be in the conference space. At various points, these concerns were
embodied and conjured up in the figure of 'old-school' department
members, conference-going children, conservative meteorologists, and
members of an unspecified, yet seemingly present reactionary polity. The
audience at issue never included us. In all cases, external threats
loomed over the process of including certain works. In retrospect, these
various substitutions projected the discomfort and concern of those
involved onto vaguely defined others, others we could not control or
communicate with rationally, others that would respond with 'blowback.'
It was these others and their conservative political viewpoints that
drew the boundaries of what we could and could not include. [This
tendency to externalize our own fears, racial privilege, and homophobia
onto imaginary others undercut the potential for collective reflection
on the boundaries of critical geographies. If we are concerned about the
future of critical geographies, it is worth reflecting on how this
silence was guaranteed and indeed, rendered apolitical, by our
self-assured status as 'critical geographers.']{.underline}

**[New methods (of vetting), old forms of exclusion]{.underline}**

What these negotiations demonstrate most forcefully is how social
positionalities, **[relations of authority]{.underline}**,
[and]{.underline} [**imaginaries of the institution** and its values
**constrain** potential **interventions into disciplinary

'**[belonged']{.underline}** [in]{.underline} the department's
**[intellectual]{.underline}** and physical **[space]{.underline}**
[were closely sutured to the identities of those with the power to make

constitute, and are, in turn, constituted by social structures' (Beisel
1993) including social identities, creating real obstacles for
marginalized subjects to intervene in the dominant -- and
institutionalized -- ways of imagining and practicing space and
knowledge production. [Here, the label 'controversial' functions as 'a
broad category that covers any representation or visitor experience that
upsets the naturalized order of normalcy,]{.underline}' (Tyburczy 2016,
xvi) [serving to police and reproduce what and who counts as legitimate
within the halls of the department]{.underline}. Given these dynamics,
we should not be surprised that, as scholars in creative geographies

'appropriate']{.underline} (de Leeuw and Hawkins 2017).

As this article has argued, a close attention to the dynamics of staging
